The Syllogism
intermediate
5 min
The Scope of the Four Figures: Categorical and Conditional
Resolves an apparent contradiction about the figures' scope.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to resolve the apparent contradiction between the matn's claim and the later logicians' consensus regarding the scope of the four figures.

Core Idea
The four figures apply to both categorical and conditional conjunctive syllogisms; the matn's phrasing is a linguistic nuance.

In this passage, the matn states: 'And these forms are specific to categorical, not to conditional.' This seems to limit the figures to categorical propositions. However, later logicians agree that the figures also apply to conditional syllogisms. The commentator offers a lexical resolution: the phrase 'bi-l-hamli' means that categorical propositions are restricted to these forms, not that the forms are restricted to categoricals. This lesson will help you understand the correct interpretation and why conditional syllogisms can indeed be placed in the four figures.

Guided Path
1
Identify the apparent claim in the matn
Read the matn statement: 'وهذه الأشكال بالحملي مختصة وليس بالشرطي'. What does this seem to say about the four figures?
The apparent meaning is that the four figures are composed only from categorical propositions.
2
Recognize the disagreement with later logicians
The commentator points out that this contradicts what later scholars ('المتأخرون') have agreed upon. They hold that the figures are composed from both categoricals and conditionals.
This indicates a need for interpretation.
3
Analyze the commentator's linguistic resolution
The commentator suggests that the preposition 'ba' in 'bi-l-hamli' is used for restriction (اختصاص), meaning categoricals are restricted to these forms, not the other way around. This allows conditionals to also form these figures in conjunctive syllogisms.
He supports this usage with examples from Arabic grammar.
4
Confirm the correct understanding
We now see that the four figures are a general logical structure applicable to both categorical and conditional premises. The matn's intent was to say that categoricals can only be used in these forms, but conditionals can be used in both conjunctive and exceptive syllogisms.
This aligns with the consensus.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل [في الأشكال] قَوْلُهُ : (۹۷) وَهَذِهِ الأَشْكَالُ بِالحَمْلِي مُخْتَصَّةٌ وَلَيْسَ بِالشَّرْطِي ظَاهِرُهُ : أَنَّ هَذِهِ الأَشْكَالَ الأَرْبَعَةَ لَا تَتَرَكَّبُ إِلَّا مِنَ القَضَايَا الحَمْلِيَّةِ دُونَ الشَّرْطِيَّةِ، وَهُوَ خِلافُ مَا أَطْبَقَ عَلَيْهِ المُتَأَخُرُونَ مِنْ أَنَّهَا تَتَرَكَّبُ مِنَ الحَمْلِيَّاتِ وَمِنَ الشَّرْطِيَّاتِ . وَقَدْ يُجَابُ : بِأَنَّ البَاءَ فِي قَوْلِهِ : بِالحَمْلِي دَاخِلَةٌ عَلَى المَقْصُورِ، وَأَنَّ مُرَادَهُ : أَنَّ القَضَايَا الحَمْلِيَّةَ مَقْصُورَةٌ عَلَى هَذِهِ الأَشْكَالِ، لَا يَتَرَكَّبُ مِنْهَا غَيْرُهَا، وَهُوَ القِيَاسُ الاسْتِثْنَائِيُّ ، بِخِلَافِ القَضَايَا الشَّرْطِيَّةِ، فَإِنَّهَا يُرَكَّبُ مِنْهَا كُلٌّ مِنَ الافْتِرَانِي وَالِاسْتِثْنَائِي، وَهَذَا صَحِيحٌ، وَدُخُولُ البَاءِ عَلَى المَقْصُورِ كَثِيرٌ ، كَمَا قِيلَ: وَالبَاءُ بَعْدَ الاخْتِصَاصِ يَكْثُرُ دُخُولُهَا عَلَى الَّذِي قَدْ قَصَرُوا وَعَكْسُهُ مُسْتَعْمَلٌ وَجَيِّدُ ذَكَرَهُ الحِبْرُ الهُمَامُ السَّيِّدُ انتهى [ج/ ٣٨]. ثُمَّ الاِقْتِرَانِيُّ المُرَكَّبُ مِنَ الشَّرْطِيَّاتِ تَارَةً يُرَكَّبُ مِنْ مُتَّصِلَتَيْنِ، وَهَذَا حُكْمُهُ حُكْمُ الحَمْلِي فِي جَمِيعِ مَا تَقَدَّمَ : وَمِثَالُهُ فِي الشَّكْلِ الأَوَّلِ : كُلَّمَا كَانَ المَوْجُودُ مُمْكِناً كَانَ حَادِثاً، وَكُلَّمَا كَانَ حَادِثاً كَانَ مُفْتَقِراً إِلَى الفَاعِلِ يُنْتِجُ : كُلَّمَا كَانَ المَوْجُودُ مُمْكِناً كَانَ مُفْتَقِراً إِلَى الفَاعِلِ، وَوَجْهُ إِنْتَاجِهِ : أَنَّ لَازِمَ اللَّازِمِ لِشَيْءٍ لَازِمٌ لِذَلِكَ الشَّيْءِ، وَهُوَ وَاضِحٌ. وَمِثَالُهُ مِنَ الشَّكْلِ الثَّانِي : كُلَّمَا كَانَ المَوْجُودُ مُمْكِناً كَانَ حَادِثاً، وَلَيْسَ البَتَّةَ إِذَا كَانَ قَدِيماً كَانَ حَادِثاً يُنْتِجُ : «لَيْسَ البَتَّةَ إِذَا كَانَ المَوْجُودُ مُمْكِناً كَانَ قَدِيماً، وَيَرْجِعُ إِلَى الْأَوَّلِ بِعَكْسِ الكُبْرَى. وَمِثَالُ الثَّالِثِ : كُلَّمَا كَانَ المَوْجُودُ مُمْكِناً كَانَ حَادِثاً، وَكُلَّمَا كَانَ مُمْكِناً كَانَ مُفْتَقِراً
Chapter on the figures. His statement: (97) 'And these forms are specific to categorical, not to conditional.' Its apparent meaning is that these four figures are composed only from categorical propositions, not from conditional ones. This contradicts what the later scholars have agreed upon, that they are composed from categoricals and from conditionals. And it may be answered: that the 'ba' in his statement 'bi-l-hamli' enters upon the restricted, and that his meaning is that categorical propositions are restricted to these forms, nothing other than them is composed from them, i.e., the exceptive syllogism. Unlike conditional propositions, for from them both conjunctive and exceptive are composed. And this is correct. And the entry of 'ba' upon the restricted is frequent, as it is said: 'And the ba after restriction frequently enters upon that which they restricted, and its opposite is used and good,' mentioned by the erudite master al-Sayyid. End [Vol. 38]. Then the conjunctive syllogism composed from conditionals is sometimes composed from two conditionals, and its ruling is the ruling of categoricals in all that preceded. Its example in the first figure: 'Whenever the existent is possible it is originated, and whenever it is originated it depends on the agent' yields: 'Whenever the existent is possible it depends on the agent.' And the reason for its productivity: that the necessary of the necessary of a thing is necessary for that thing, and it is obvious. And its example from the second figure: 'Whenever the existent is possible it is originated, and it is not at all that if it is eternal it is originated' yields: 'It is not at all that whenever the existent is possible it is eternal,' and it returns to the first by conversion of the major. And the example of the third: 'Whenever the existent is possible it is originated, and whenever it is possible it depends on the agent'...
Source pages p. 695

Commentary
This passage addresses a subtle point about the scope of the four figures. The matn seems to claim that the forms (first, second, third, fourth) are specific to categorical syllogisms. The commentator notes that this apparent meaning contradicts the consensus of later logicians, who hold that these figures apply to conditional syllogisms as well. A resolution is offered: understanding the Arabic phrase 'بالحملي مختصة' as meaning 'categoricals are restricted to these forms,' i.e., categoricals can only be used in these forms, but conditionals can be used in both conjunctive (iqtirani) and exceptive (istithna'i) forms. The commentator then proceeds to show that conjunctive conditional syllogisms (composed of conditionals) follow the same rules as categoricals. He provides an example in the first figure: a conditional syllogism using 'whenever...then' premises, the conclusion is derived by the transitivity of the conditional. The second figure example uses a negative conditional major premise, and the conclusion is obtained by conversion of the major. The third figure example is started but cut off. The key point is that the logical structures are parallel, and the same figures apply.

Key Terms
المتصل
الاقتراني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the commentator's resolution to the apparent contradiction about the figures?
A) The matn was mistaken and later logicians corrected it.
B) The phrase 'bi-l-hamli' means categoricals are restricted to the four figures, not that the figures are restricted to categoricals.
C) The figures apply only to conditional syllogisms when the premises are universal.
D) The matn intended the first figure only.

Key Takeaway
The four figures are a universal logical structure for conjunctive syllogisms, applicable to both categorical and conditional premises; the matn's wording is a linguistic restriction on categoricals, not on the figures themselves.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given the matn's statement 'وهذه الأشكال بالحملي مختصة', explain in your own words how the commentator resolves the apparent conflict with the later logicians' view. Then, using a fiqh example, construct a conditional syllogism in the first figure and label its components (premises, conclusion, middle term).

The Syllogism
beginner
5 min
Conditional Syllogisms in the First Figure
Provides an example of a conditional syllogism in the first figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a valid first-figure conditional syllogism and justify its validity using the principle of transitivity.

Core Idea
A conditional conjunctive syllogism in the first figure is valid by the transitivity of implication.

Having clarified that conditionals can form figures, the commentator provides a concrete example in the first figure: 'Whenever the existent is possible it is originated, and whenever it is originated it depends on the agent, therefore whenever the existent is possible it depends on the agent.' The validity is justified by the principle 'the necessary of the necessary of a thing is necessary for that thing' – a form of transitivity. This lesson will walk you through the structure and the underlying logical principle.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises and conclusion
Write out the two premises and the conclusion in the example. The first premise: 'Whenever the existent is possible, it is originated.' The second: 'Whenever it is originated, it depends on the agent.' The conclusion: 'Whenever the existent is possible, it depends on the agent.'
All propositions are conditionals with 'whenever...then' (كلما...كان).
2
Recognize the middle term
Notice that the consequent of the first premise ('it is originated') is the antecedent of the second premise. This common term 'originated' is the middle term connecting the two premises.
In the first figure, the middle term is the subject of the major premise and predicate of the minor premise. Here it is the consequent of the minor and antecedent of the major.
3
Apply the principle of transitivity
The commentator states that the validity is due to: 'the necessary of the necessary of a thing is necessary for that thing.' In other words, if A implies B and B implies C, then A implies C. This is the rule of transitivity for conditionals.
This principle justifies the conclusion directly.
4
Check the figure conditions
In the first figure, the middle term must be the subject in the major and predicate in the minor. For conditionals, the analogous structure is that the consequent of the first (which is like predicate) is the antecedent of the second (like subject). So it matches the first figure pattern.
The analogy holds.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل [في الأشكال] قَوْلُهُ : (۹۷) وَهَذِهِ الأَشْكَالُ بِالحَمْلِي مُخْتَصَّةٌ وَلَيْسَ بِالشَّرْطِي ظَاهِرُهُ : أَنَّ هَذِهِ الأَشْكَالَ الأَرْبَعَةَ لَا تَتَرَكَّبُ إِلَّا مِنَ القَضَايَا الحَمْلِيَّةِ دُونَ الشَّرْطِيَّةِ، وَهُوَ خِلافُ مَا أَطْبَقَ عَلَيْهِ المُتَأَخُرُونَ مِنْ أَنَّهَا تَتَرَكَّبُ مِنَ الحَمْلِيَّاتِ وَمِنَ الشَّرْطِيَّاتِ . وَقَدْ يُجَابُ : بِأَنَّ البَاءَ فِي قَوْلِهِ : بِالحَمْلِي دَاخِلَةٌ عَلَى المَقْصُورِ، وَأَنَّ مُرَادَهُ : أَنَّ القَضَايَا الحَمْلِيَّةَ مَقْصُورَةٌ عَلَى هَذِهِ الأَشْكَالِ، لَا يَتَرَكَّبُ مِنْهَا غَيْرُهَا، وَهُوَ القِيَاسُ الاسْتِثْنَائِيُّ ، بِخِلَافِ القَضَايَا الشَّرْطِيَّةِ، فَإِنَّهَا يُرَكَّبُ مِنْهَا كُلٌّ مِنَ الافْتِرَانِي وَالِاسْتِثْنَائِي، وَهَذَا صَحِيحٌ، وَدُخُولُ البَاءِ عَلَى المَقْصُورِ كَثِيرٌ ، كَمَا قِيلَ: وَالبَاءُ بَعْدَ الاخْتِصَاصِ يَكْثُرُ دُخُولُهَا عَلَى الَّذِي قَدْ قَصَرُوا وَعَكْسُهُ مُسْتَعْمَلٌ وَجَيِّدُ ذَكَرَهُ الحِبْرُ الهُمَامُ السَّيِّدُ انتهى [ج/ ٣٨]. ثُمَّ الاِقْتِرَانِيُّ المُرَكَّبُ مِنَ الشَّرْطِيَّاتِ تَارَةً يُرَكَّبُ مِنْ مُتَّصِلَتَيْنِ، وَهَذَا حُكْمُهُ حُكْمُ الحَمْلِي فِي جَمِيعِ مَا تَقَدَّمَ : وَمِثَالُهُ فِي الشَّكْلِ الأَوَّلِ : كُلَّمَا كَانَ المَوْجُودُ مُمْكِناً كَانَ حَادِثاً، وَكُلَّمَا كَانَ حَادِثاً كَانَ مُفْتَقِراً إِلَى الفَاعِلِ يُنْتِجُ : كُلَّمَا كَانَ المَوْجُودُ مُمْكِناً كَانَ مُفْتَقِراً إِلَى الفَاعِلِ، وَوَجْهُ إِنْتَاجِهِ : أَنَّ لَازِمَ اللَّازِمِ لِشَيْءٍ لَازِمٌ لِذَلِكَ الشَّيْءِ، وَهُوَ وَاضِحٌ. وَمِثَالُهُ مِنَ الشَّكْلِ الثَّانِي : كُلَّمَا كَانَ المَوْجُودُ مُمْكِناً كَانَ حَادِثاً، وَلَيْسَ البَتَّةَ إِذَا كَانَ قَدِيماً كَانَ حَادِثاً يُنْتِجُ : «لَيْسَ البَتَّةَ إِذَا كَانَ المَوْجُودُ مُمْكِناً كَانَ قَدِيماً، وَيَرْجِعُ إِلَى الْأَوَّلِ بِعَكْسِ الكُبْرَى. وَمِثَالُ الثَّالِثِ : كُلَّمَا كَانَ المَوْجُودُ مُمْكِناً كَانَ حَادِثاً، وَكُلَّمَا كَانَ مُمْكِناً كَانَ مُفْتَقِراً

The commentator notes that this apparent meaning contradicts the consensus of later logicians, who hold that these figures apply to conditional syllogisms as well. ?

The Syllogism
intermediate

Resolving Apparent Contradictions in Syllogistic Figures
Expansion probe on the scope of the four figures

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and resolve apparent contradictions between a matn statement and later scholarly consensus using linguistic interpretation.

Core Idea
A single preposition can dissolve a disagreement between the text and later logicians.

Sometimes a matn statement appears to contradict established scholarly consensus. In this lesson, we examine how the commentator resolved such an apparent contradiction by looking closely at the Arabic preposition 'bi' ('with') in the matn. Understanding this linguistic maneuver will sharpen your ability to harmonize texts and recognize subtle distinctions in logical propositions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the apparent contradiction
Read the matn's claim that the four figures are specific to categorical propositions ('bi al-hamli'). Compare it with the later scholars' agreement that conditional propositions also form these figures. Notice the conflict: if the four figures are only for categoricals, how can conditionals also form them?
2
Analyze the commentator's strategy
The commentator introduces a linguistic resolution: the preposition 'bi' (in 'bi al-hamli') is understood as indicating restriction (al-maqsūr). That is, 'categoricals are restricted to these forms' instead of 'these forms are only for categoricals'.
This shifts the meaning from exclusivity of the figures to confinement of categoricals.
3
Apply the linguistic rule
Recognize that in classical Arabic, the preposition 'ba' often enters upon the thing that is restricted, not the thing that is specific. The commentator cites a grammatical principle: 'The ba after restriction frequently enters upon what they have restricted.' So the matn means categoricals are restricted to these figures, not that these figures exclude conditionals.
This is a well-known pattern: 'bi' can be used to indicate the restricted entity, as in 'ختصصت المرأة بالصبر' (the woman is distinguished by patience).
4
Verify the resolution
Check the conclusion: Now there is no contradiction. Categorical propositions indeed only form conjunctive syllogisms (the four figures). Conditional propositions, however, can form both conjunctive (the four figures) and exceptive syllogisms. Thus, the later scholars' view is preserved.
The commentator concludes this interpretation is 'sahih' (correct) and cites the Sayyid for support.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وَهَذِهِ الأَشْكَالُ بِالحَمْلِي مُخْتَصَّةٌ وَلَيْسَ بِالشَّرْطِي ظَاهِرُهُ : أَنَّ هَذِهِ الأَشْكَالَ الأَرْبَعَةَ لَا تَتَرَكَّبُ إِلَّا مِنَ القَضَايَا الحَمْلِيَّةِ دُونَ الشَّرْطِيَّةِ، وَهُوَ خِلافُ مَا أَطْبَقَ عَلَيْهِ المُتَأخُرُونَ مِنْ أَنَّهَا تَتَرَكَّبُ مِنَ الحَمْلِيَّاتِ وَمِنَ الشَّرْطِيَّاتِ . وَقَدْ يُجَابُ : بِأَنَّ البَاءَ فِي قَوْلِهِ : بِالحَمْلِي دَاخِلَةٌ عَلَى المَقْصُورِ، وَأَنَّ مُرَادَهُ : أَنَّ القَضَايَا الحَمْلِيَّةَ مَقْصُورَةٌ عَلَى هَذِهِ الأَشْكَالِ، لَا يَتَرَكَّبُ مِنْهَا غَيْرُهَا، وَهُوَ القِيَاسُ الاسْتِثْنَائِيُّ ، بِخِلَافِ القَضَايَا الشَّرْطِيَّةِ، فَإِنَّهَا يُرَكَّبُ مِنْهَا كُلٌ مِنَ الِاقْتِرَانِي وَالِاسْتِثْنَائِي، وَهَذَا صَحِيحٌ، وَدُخُولُ البَاءِ عَلَى المَقْصُورِ كَثِيرٌ ، كَمَا قِيلَ: وَالبَاءُ بَعْدَ الاخْتِصَاصِ يَكْثُرُ دُخُولُهَا عَلَى الَّذِي قَدْ قَصَرُوا وَعَكْسُهُ مُسْتَعْمَلٌ وَجَيِّدُ ذَكَرَهُ الحِبْرُ الهُمَامُ السَّيِّدُ انتهى.
His saying: (And these forms are specific to the categorical and not to the conditional) Its apparent meaning is that these four forms are composed only from categorical propositions, not conditional ones. But this contradicts what later scholars have agreed upon, that they are composed from categoricals and from conditionals. An answer is given: that the preposition 'ba' in his statement 'with the categorical' is entering upon the restricted, and his intent is that categorical propositions are restricted to these forms; nothing else is composed from them, namely the exceptive syllogism, unlike conditional propositions, for from them both the conjunctive and the exceptive are composed. This is correct. The entry of 'ba' upon the restricted is frequent, as it is said: 'The ba' after restriction often enters upon what they have restricted, and its opposite is used and is good.' This was mentioned by the noble scholar the Sayyid. End.
Source pages p. 695

Commentary
This passage explains a statement from the matn that the four figures are specific to categorical propositions. The author first gives the apparent meaning, then notes the disagreement with later logicians who allow conditionals. The resolution is a linguistic interpretation: the preposition 'ba' indicates restriction, meaning that categoricals are confined to these forms (i.e., the conjunctive syllogism), while conditionals can form both conjunctive and exceptive syllogisms. This preserves agreement. The author supports the interpretation by citing a grammatical principle about 'ba' after restriction. The passage is an expansion probe typical of a sharh, clarifying an ambiguous matn statement.

Key Terms
الشكل
الحمل
الشرط
المقصور

C) He claims the matn refers only to the first figure.

Key Takeaway
Apparent contradictions in logical texts often yield to careful grammatical analysis of prepositions and restrictions.

The Syllogism
intermediate
12 min
Conditional Syllogisms: The Four Figures and Their Reduction
Author gives examples of the four figures using two connected conditionals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify each of the four figures of a conjunctive syllogism composed of two connected conditionals and describe how to reduce each to the first figure using conversion or substitution.

Core Idea
Conditional syllogisms mirror categoricals and always reduce to the first figure.

We have seen how categorical syllogisms follow strict figures. Now the author shows that the same logic applies when the premises are connected conditionals. By examining examples from each figure, you will learn to recognize and reduce conditional syllogisms, reinforcing the universal form of the conjunctive syllogism.

Guided Path
1
The First Figure: Transitivity of Implication
The first figure example shows that if A implies B and B implies C, then A implies C. This is the principle that 'the necessary of the necessary is necessary'.
2
The Second Figure: Using a Negative Major
The second figure has a negative major premise (e.g., 'it is absolutely not the case that if it is eternal, it is originated'). The reduction is done by converting the major premise to get a first figure.
3
The Third Figure: Converting the Minor
The third figure example has the middle term as subject in both premises (e.g., 'whenever possible' in both). Reduction: convert the minor premise to make it the subject.
4
The Fourth Figure: Substitution and Conversion
The fourth figure requires both substitution (making the minor the major) and conversion of the conclusion to revert to the first figure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثُمَّ الِاقْتِرَانِيُّ المُرَكَّبُ مِنَ الشَّرْطِيَّاتِ تَارَةً يُرَكَّبُ مِنْ مُتَّصِلَتَيْنِ، وَهَذَا حُكْمُهُ حُكْمُ الحَمْلِي فِي جَمِيعِ مَا تَقَدَّمَ : وَمِثَالُهُ فِي الشَّكْلِ الأَوَّلِ : «كُلَّمَا كَانَ المَوْجُودُ مُمْكِناً كَانَ حَادِثاً، وَكُلَّمَا كَانَ حَادِثاً كَانَ مُفْتَقِراً إِلَى الفَاعِلِ» يُنْتِجُ : «كُلَّمَا كَانَ المَوْجُودُ مُمْكِناً كَانَ مُفْتَقِراً إِلَى الفَاعِلِ» ، وَوَجْهُ إِنْتَاجِهِ : أَنَّ لَازِمَ اللَّازِمِ لِشَيْءٍ لَازِمٌ لِذَلِكَ الشَّيْءِ، وَهُوَ وَاضِحٌ. وَمِثَالُهُ مِنَ الشَّكْلِ الثَّانِي : «كُلَّمَا كَانَ المَوْجُودُ مُمْكِناً كَانَ حَادِثاً، وَلَيْسَ البَتَّةَ إِذَا كَانَ قَدِيماً كَانَ حَادِثاً» يُنْتِجُ : «لَيْسَ البَتَّةَ إِذَا كَانَ المَوْجُودُ مُمْكِناً كَانَ قَدِيماً، وَيَرْجِعُ إِلَى الْأَوَّلِ بِعَكْسِ الكُبْرَى. وَمِثَالُ الثَّالِثِ : «كُلَّمَا كَانَ المَوْجُودُ مُمْكِناً كَانَ حَادِثاً، وَكُلَّمَا كَانَ مُمْكِناً كَانَ مُفْتَقِراً إِلَى الفَاعِلِ» يُنْتِجُ : «قَدْ يَكُونُ إِذَا كَانَ المَوْجُودُ حَادِثاً كَانَ مُفْتَقِراً» ، وَيَرْجِعُ لِلأَوَّلِ بِعَكْسِ الصُّغْرَى . وَمِثَالُ الرَّابعِ : «كُلَّمَا كَانَ المَوْجُودُ مُمْكِناً كَانَ حَادِثاً، وَكُلَّمَا كَانَ مُحْتَاجاً كَانَ مُمْكِناً» يُنْتِجُ : «قَدْ يَكُونُ إِذَا كَانَ المَوْجُودُ حَادِثاً كَانَ مُحْتَاجاً» ، وَيَرْجِعُ إِلَى الْأَوَّلِ بِالتَّبْدِيلِ وَعَكْسِ النَّتِيجَةِ . قوله : (وَيَرْجِعُ إِلَى الأَوَّلِ بِالتَّبْدِيلِ) أي : جعل الصُّغرى كبرى.
Then the conjunctive composed of conditionals: sometimes it is composed of two connected conditionals, and its ruling is the same as the categorical in all that preceded. Its example in the first figure: 'Whenever the existent is possible, it is originated; and whenever it is originated, it is dependent on the agent' yields: 'Whenever the existent is possible, it is dependent on the agent.' The reason for its productivity is that the necessary of the necessary of a thing is necessary for that thing, which is clear. Its example from the second figure: 'Whenever the existent is possible, it is originated; and it is absolutely not the case that if it is eternal, it is originated' yields: 'It is absolutely not the case that whenever the existent is possible, it is eternal.' It reverts to the first by conversion of the major premise. Example of the third: 'Whenever the existent is possible, it is originated; and whenever it is possible, it is dependent on the agent' yields: 'It may be that if the existent is originated, it is dependent.' It reverts to the first by conversion of the minor premise. Example of the fourth: 'Whenever the existent is possible, it is originated; and whenever it is needy, it is possible' yields: 'It may be that if the existent is originated, it is needy.' It reverts to the first by substitution and conversion of the conclusion. His saying: 'and it reverts to the first by substitution' means making the minor premise the major.
Source pages p. 695

Commentary
This passage explains how the conjunctive syllogism (qiyas iqtirani) can be formed from two connected conditional (mutasila) premises. It first states that the rules are the same as for categoricals. Then it provides examples from all four figures, each illustrating the reduction to the first figure. The first figure example uses the transitivity of implication (luzum). The second figure example uses a negative major premise and reverts by converting the major. The third figure reverts by converting the minor. The fourth figure reverts by substitution (making the minor the major) and conversion of the conclusion. The commentary explains the term 'tabdeel' as making the minor premise the major. These examples show that conditional syllogisms follow the same patterns as categorical ones, reinforcing the harmony between the two types of propositions.

Key Terms
متصلة (muttaṣila)
لازم (lāzim)
عكس ('aks)
تبديل (tabdīl)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the second figure example with conditionals, how is the syllogism reduced to the first figure?
A) By converting the minor premise.
B) By converting the major premise.
C) By substitution.
D) By conversion of the conclusion.

Key Takeaway
Conditional syllogisms follow the same logical patterns as categorical syllogisms and can be reduced to the first figure for verification.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
1. Take the following second-figure conditional syllogism: 'Whenever the act is obligatory (wajib), it is commanded; but it is absolutely not the case that if it is forbidden (haram), it is commanded.' Determine the conclusion and reduce it to the first figure by converting the major premise.
?

The Syllogism
intermediate

The Complete Part Condition for Disjunctive Syllogisms
Explanation of the complete part condition for disjunctive syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a syllogism composed of two disjunctive premises has a valid middle term (complete part) or is sterile.

Core Idea
A disjunctive syllogism is valid only if the shared term is a whole antecedent or whole consequent.

In this passage, the author explains that composing a syllogism from two disjunctives is generally sterile unless the sharing is in a complete part. He defines what a complete part means: it must be the entire antecedent or the entire consequent of the conditional. Incomplete parts are too complex and were avoided by earlier scholars. We will dissect this condition and learn to evaluate such syllogisms.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two disjunctive premises
Locate the two disjunctive conditional propositions that form the syllogism. Each is typically formulated as 'Either ... or ...'.
2
Find the shared term (middle term)
Look for a term or proposition that appears in both disjunctives. This is the middle term that connects the two premises.
The shared term must be identical in both.
3
Check if the shared term is a complete part
Determine whether the shared term is the entire antecedent of one premise and the entire consequent of the other (or vice versa). If it is only a part of a side, it is incomplete.
A complete part means the whole side, e.g., 'C is D' in 'Either A is B or C is D'.
4
Assess validity
If the shared term is a complete part, the syllogism is valid (provided the disjunctives are of the correct types). If it is incomplete, the syllogism is sterile.
The author states that incomplete parts are of little benefit.
5
Verify the type of disjunctives
Ensure the two disjunctives are one of the six valid combinations (taught in the next lesson). This is necessary for the syllogism to be productive.
This step uses knowledge from the subsequent lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَتَارَةٌ يُرَكَّبُ مِنْ مُنْفَصِلَتَيْنِ وَالشِّرْكَةِ بَيْنَهُمَا فِي جُزْءٍ تَامٌ، وَهَذَا مِنْ حَيْثُ تَرْكِيبُهُ عَقِيمٌ. قوله : (فِي جُزءٍ تَامٌ) المرادُ بـ «الجزء التام»: أن يكون المشترك وهو الوسط أحد طرفي الشَّرطيَّة بكماله ؛ إمَّا المقدَّم بكماله ، وإمَّا التّالي بكماله، وأمَّا الجزء الغير التّامّ وهو ما يكون جزءاً مِنَ المقدَّم أوِ التَّالي لا جميع المقدَّم أوِ التَّالي، فقليلُ الجدوى، ولذا لم يتعرّض له السنوسي والقادري ؛ لكثرة تشعبه، وشدَّة تصعبه، حتّى قال فيه بعض الشُّعراء على سبيل التمليح : «رَامُوا كَمَالَ مَنْطِقٍ كَيْ يُتْقِنُوا عِلْمَ الكَلَامِ أَنَّى لَهُمْ كَمَالَه». ثمَّ إنَّ المنفصلين اللتين يتر كَّب منهما القياس ستَّة أقسام ؛ لأنَّهما : إمَّا حقيقيَّتان، وإما مانعتا جمع، وإما مانعتا خلو، وإما حقيقيَّة ومانعة جمع، أو خلو، أو مانعة جمع وخلو. مثالُ الحقيقيَّتين: «دائماً إما أن يكون العدد زوجاً، وإما أن يكون فرداً، ودائماً إما أن يكون العدد فرداً وإما أن ينقسم بمتساويين». ومثالُ مانعتي الجمع : «إما أن يكون الموجود جرماً، وإما أن يكون عرضاً، ودائماً إما أن يكون عرضاً وإما أن يكون قائماً بنفسه». ومثال مانعتي الخلو: «دائماً إما أن يكون الجرم غير أبيض، وإما أن يكون غير أسود، ودائماً إما أن يكون غير أسود، وإما أن يكون غير أحمر». ومثالُ المركَّب من حقيقيَّة ومانعة جمع : «دائماً إما أن يكون الموجود حادثاً، وإما أن يكون قديماً، ودائماً إما أن يكون قديماً، وإما أن يكون عرضاً». ومثالُ المركَّب مِن حقيقيَّة ومانعة خلو : «دائماً إما أن يكون الإنسان في البر، وإما أن يكون في البحر، ودائماً إما أن يكون في البحر، وإما أن لا يغرق».
And sometimes it is composed of two disjunctives with sharing between them in a complete part, and this in terms of its composition is sterile. His saying: 'in a complete part' – what is meant by 'the complete part' is that the shared term (the middle) is one of the two sides of the conditional in its entirety; either the antecedent in its entirety, or the consequent in its entirety. As for the incomplete part, which is a part of the antecedent or consequent, not the whole antecedent or consequent, it is of little benefit, and therefore al-Sanusi and al-Qadiri did not address it, due to its numerous branches and extreme difficulty. So much so that one poet said in jest: 'They sought the perfection of logic to master the science of theology; how could they attain its perfection?' Then the two disjunctives from which the syllogism is composed are six kinds: because they are either both real (haqiqiyyatayn), both exclusive of conjunction (mani'atay jam'), both exclusive of disjunction (mani'atay khuluw), or one real and one exclusive of conjunction, or one real and one exclusive of disjunction, or one exclusive of conjunction and one exclusive of disjunction. Examples: Real both: 'Always, either the number is even or it is odd; and always, either the number is odd or it is divisible by two equals.' Both exclusive of conjunction: 'Either the existent is a body or it is an accident; and always, either it is an accident or it is self-subsistent.' Both exclusive of disjunction: 'Always, either the body is not white or it is not black; and always, either it is not black or it is not red.' Real and exclusive of conjunction: 'Always, either the existent is originated or it is eternal; and always, either it is eternal or it is an accident.' Real and exclusive of disjunction: 'Always, either man is on land or he is at sea; and always, either he is at sea or he does not drown.'
Source pages p. 696

Commentary
This passage addresses the conjunctive syllogism (qiyas iqtirani) composed of two disjunctive (munfasila) premises. It first states that such a syllogism is sterile unless there is sharing in a complete part. The 'complete part' means the middle term must be either the entire antecedent or the entire consequent of both disjunctives. Incomplete parts are too complex and thus skipped by earlier scholars. Then it enumerates six possible combinations of the two disjunctives based on their types: real (true disjunction), exclusive of conjunction (incompatible), exclusive of disjunction (exhaustive), and their mixtures. Examples are given for each: mathematical (even/odd) for real, metaphysical (body/accident) for man'at jam', color predicates for man'at khuluw, originated/eternal for mixed, and land/sea for real with man'at khuluw. These examples illustrate how the middle term (the shared part) appears in various types of disjunctives.

Key Terms
منفصلة
جزء تام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the condition for a syllogism composed of two disjunctives to be productive?
A) The two disjunctives must be of the same type.
B) The middle term must be a complete part (entire antecedent or entire consequent).
C) The premises must be real disjunctives.
D) The middle term must be incomplete.

Key Takeaway
A syllogism from two disjunctive premises is valid only if the shared middle term is an entire antecedent or entire consequent of both.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the following pair of disjunctives: 'Either knowledge is acquired or it is innate; either it is innate or it is conferred by Allah.' Identify the shared term and check if it is a complete part. If not, explain why the syllogism would be sterile.

The Six Kinds of Disjunctive Premise Pairs
Enumeration of six kinds of disjunctive pairs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which of the six possible combinations of two disjunctive premises is present in a given example and verify the types.

Core Idea
Six combinations of disjunctive types yield all possible valid syllogisms from two disjunctives.

After understanding the need for a complete middle term, we now learn the six valid combinations of disjunctive premises. These are determined by the three types of disjunctives: real (haqiqiyya), exclusive of conjunction (mani'at jam'), and exclusive of disjunction (mani'at khuluw). We will go through each combination with examples from theology, mathematics, and everyday scenarios.

Guided Path
1
Recall the three types of disjunctives
Haqlqiyya: both true and both false impossible. Mani'at jam': both true impossible, both false possible. Mani'at khuluw: both false impossible, both true possible.
These are standard definitions in logic.
2
List the six combinations
The pairs are: both real, both man'at jam', both man'at khuluw, real+man'at jam', real+man'at khuluw, man'at jam'+man'at khuluw.
Order does not matter.
3
Identify the shared complete part
For each example, locate the middle term and verify it is a complete part (entire side). This is necessary for the syllogism to be valid.
The middle term must be identical.
4
Classify the example
Using the given examples, determine which combination each pair represents by analyzing the type of each premise.
Refer to the definitions of the types.

وَتَارَةٌ يُرَكَّبُ مِنْ مُنْفَصِلَتَيْنِ وَالشِّرْكَةِ بَيْنَهُمَا فِي جُزْءٍ تَامٌ، وَهَذَا مِنْ حَيْثُ تَرْكِيبُهُ عَقِيمٌ. قوله : (فِي جُزءٍ تَامٌ) المرادُ بـ «الجزء التام»: أن يكون المشترك وهو الوسط أحد طرفي الشَّرطيَّة بكماله ؛ إمَّا المقدَّم بكماله ، وإمَّا التّالي بكماله، وأمَّا الجزء الغير التّامّ وهو ما يكون جزءاً مِنَ المقدَّم أوِ التَّالي لا جميع المقدَّم أوِ التَّالي، فقليلُ الجدوى، ولذا لم يتعرّض له السنوسي والقادري ؛ لكثرة تشعبه، وشدَّة تصعبه، حتّى قال فيه بعض الشُّعراء على سبيل التمليح : «رَامُوا كَمَالَ مَنْطِقٍ كَيْ يُتْقِنُوا عِلْمَ الكَلَامِ أَنَّى لَهُمْ كَمَالَه». ثمَّ إنَّ المنفصلين اللتين يتر كَّب منهما القياس ستَّة أقسام ؛ لأنَّهما : إمَّا حقيقيَّتان، وإما مانعتا جمع، وإما مانعتا خلو، وإما حقيقيَّة ومانعة جمع، أو خلو، أو مانعة جمع وخلو. مثالُ الحقيقيَّتين: «دائماً إما أن يكون العدد زوجاً، وإما أن يكون فرداً، ودائماً إما أن يكون العدد فرداً وإما أن ينقسم بمتساويين». ومثالُ مانعتي الجمع : «إما أن يكون الموجود جرماً، وإما أن يكون عرضاً، ودائماً إما أن يكون عرضاً وإما أن يكون قائماً بنفسه». ومثال مانعتي الخلو: «دائماً إما أن يكون الجرم غير أبيض، وإما أن يكون غير أسود، ودائماً إما أن يكون غير أسود، وإما أن يكون غير أحمر». ومثالُ المركَّب من حقيقيَّة ومانعة جمع : «دائماً إما أن يكون الموجود حادثاً، وإما أن يكون قديماً، ودائماً إما أن يكون قديماً، وإما أن يكون عرضاً». ومثالُ المركَّب مِن حقيقيَّة ومانعة خلو : «دائماً إما أن يكون الإنسان في البر، وإما أن يكون في البحر، ودائماً إما أن يكون في البحر، وإما أن لا يغرق».
And sometimes it is composed of two disjunctives with sharing between them in a complete part, and this in terms of its composition is sterile. His saying: 'in a complete part' – what is meant by 'the complete part' is that the shared term (the middle) is one of the two sides of the conditional in its entirety; either the antecedent in its entirety, or the consequent in its entirety. As for the incomplete part, which is a part of the antecedent or consequent, not the whole antecedent or consequent, it is of little benefit, and therefore al-Sanusi and al-Qadiri did not address it, due to its numerous branches and extreme difficulty. So much so that one poet said in jest: 'They sought the perfection of logic to master the science of theology; how could they attain its perfection?' Then the two disjunctives from which the syllogism is composed are six kinds: because they are either both real (haqiqiyyatayn), both exclusive of conjunction (mani'atay jam'), both exclusive of disjunction (mani'atay khuluw), or one real and one exclusive of conjunction, or one real and one exclusive of disjunction, or one exclusive of conjunction and one exclusive of disjunction. Examples: Real both: 'Always, either the number is even or it is odd; and always, either the number is odd or it is divisible by two equals.' Both exclusive of conjunction: 'Either the existent is a body or it is an accident; and always, either it is an accident or it is self-subsistent.' Both exclusive of disjunction: 'Always, either the body is not white or it is not black; and always, either it is not black or it is not red.' Real and exclusive of conjunction: 'Always, either the existent is originated or it is eternal; and always, either it is eternal or it is an accident.' Real and exclusive of disjunction: 'Always, either man is on land or he is at sea; and always, either he is at sea or he does not drown.'
Source pages p. 696

Commentary
This passage addresses the conjunctive syllogism (qiyas iqtirani) composed of two disjunctive (munfasila) premises. It first states that such a syllogism is sterile unless there is sharing in a complete part. The 'complete part' means the middle term must be either the entire antecedent or the entire consequent of both disjunctives. Incomplete parts are too complex and thus skipped by earlier scholars. Then it enumerates six possible combinations of the two disjunctives based on their types: real (true disjunction), exclusive of conjunction (incompatible), exclusive of disjunction (exhaustive), and their mixtures. Examples are given for each: mathematical (even/odd) for real, metaphysical (body/accident) for man'at jam', color predicates for man'at khuluw, originated/eternal for mixed, and land/sea for real with man'at khuluw. These examples illustrate how the middle term (the shared part) appears in various types of disjunctives.

حقيقية
مانعة جمع
مانعة خلو

Reducing the Fourth Figure Conditional Syllogism to the First
Author gives an example of the fourth figure of conditional syllogism and explains reduction to the first via substitution and conversion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reduce a fourth-figure conditional syllogism to the first figure using substitution and conversion.

Core Idea
The fourth figure reduces to the first by swapping premises and converting the conclusion.

This passage demonstrates how the fourth figure of a conditional syllogism, though seemingly indirect, can be transformed into the first (productive) figure. The author provides a concrete example from kalam on contingency. Understanding this reduction ensures you can validate any fourth-figure argument.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conditional premises
In the example, the premises are: 'Whenever the existent is possible, it is originated' and 'Whenever it is needy, it is possible'. The middle term 'possible' appears in both.
2
Determine the figure
Here the middle term is predicate in the first premise and subject in the second, making it the fourth figure.
3
Apply substitution (tabdil)
Swap the premises so the original minor becomes major. This yields a first-figure arrangement of the premises.
The commentator specifies: tabdil means making the minor premise the major.
4
Convert the conclusion
The original conclusion 'It may be that when the existent is originated, it is needy' is reversed (converted) to match the first figure's direct order. After conversion, the conclusion becomes 'Whenever the existent is needy, it is originated'.
This conversion ('aks al-natija) is an immediate inference that preserves truth.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمِثَالُ الرَّابع : كُلَّمَا كَانَ المَوْجُودُ مُمْكِناً كَانَ حَادِثاً ، وَكُلَّمَا كَانَ مُحْتَاجاً كَانَ مُمْكِناً بنتِجُ : «قَدْ يَكُونُ إِذَا كَانَ المَوْجُودُ حَادِثاً كَانَ مُحْتَاجاً»، وَيَرْجِعُ إِلَى الْأَوَّلِ بِالتَّبْدِيلِ وَعَكْس النَّتِيجَةِ . قوله : (وَيَرْجِعُ إِلَى الأَوَّلِ بِالتَّبْدِيلِ) أي : جعل الصُّغرى كبرى .
And the example of the fourth [figure]: 'Whenever the existent is possible, it is originated, and whenever it is needy, it is possible' produces: 'It may be that when the existent is originated, it is needy.' And it returns to the first by substitution and conversion of the conclusion. His statement: 'And it returns to the first by substitution' means: making the minor premise the major.
Source pages p. 696

Commentary
This passage provides an example of the fourth figure of a conditional syllogism and explains how to reduce it to the first figure, which is the only productive figure. The fourth figure here is a compound conditional syllogism composed of two conditional premises. The example: 'Whenever the existent (al-mawjud) is possible (mumkin), it is originated (hadith); and whenever it is needy (muhtaj), it is possible.' The conclusion drawn is: 'It may be that when the existent is originated, it is needy.' The author then states that this fourth figure can be reduced to the first figure by two operations: al-tabdil (substitution), which the commentator explains as making the original minor premise the major premise, and 'aks al-natija (conversion of the conclusion). This is a standard technique in logic to transform weaker figures into the first figure to ensure validity. The example is rooted in Islamic kalam discussions about the contingency of the world: possible things are originated, and needy things are possible, so originated things are needy – an argument for a creator.

Six Types of Compound Disjunctive Syllogisms
Author enumerates six types of compound disjunctive syllogisms with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the six possible combinations of two disjunctive types (haqiqiyya, mani'at jam', mani'at khuluww) in a compound syllogism and give a correct example for each.

Core Idea
A compound disjunctive syllogism has six types based on the two disjunctives' exclusivity, separativity, or inclusivity.

This passage explains the six types of compound syllogisms formed from two disjunctives. The author clarifies that the middle term must be a complete part, not an incomplete one, which is too complex. You will learn to recognize each type and its example.

Guided Path
1
Define 'complete part' and 'incomplete part'
The author says the shared term (middle) must be an entire antecedent or consequent, not a part. This is the 'complete part' (juz' tam). The incomplete part is of little use.
Earlier scholars like al-Sanusi and al-Qadiri avoided the incomplete part due to difficulty.
2
Identify the three disjunctive types
Recall the three types: haqiqiyya (exclusive, exactly one true), mani'at jam' (separative, not both true but may be both false), mani'at khuluww (inclusive, not both false but may be both true).
3
List the six combinations systematically
The two disjunctives can be both haqiqiyya, both mani'at jam', both mani'at khuluww, or mixed: haqiqiyya+mani'at jam', haqiqiyya+mani'at khuluww, mani'at jam'+mani'at khuluww.
These are the six types the author enumerates.
4
Match each combination with the author's example
For each type, the author gives a concrete example: e.g., both haqiqiyya: numbers even/odd; both mani'at jam': body/accident; etc. Study these examples.
Pay attention to the wording: 'daiman imma an...' for each disjunctive.

وَتَارَةٌ يُرَكَّبُ مِنْ مُنْفَصِلَتَيْنِ وَالشِّرْكَةِ بَيْنَهُمَا فِي جُزْءٍ تَامٌ، وَهَذَا مِنْ حَيْثُ تَرْكِيبُهُ عَقِيمٌ. قوله : (فِي جُزءٍ نَامٌ) المرادُ بـ «الجزء السَّام»: أن يكون المشترك وهو الوسط أحد طرفي الشَّرطيَّة بكماله ؛ إمَّا المقدَّم بكماله ، وإمَّا التّالي بكماله، وأمَّا الجزء الغير التّامّ وهو ما يكون جزءاً مِنَ المقدَّم أوِ التَّالي لا جميع المقدَّم أوِ التَّالي، فقليلُ الجدوى، ولذا لم يتعرّض له السنوسي والقادري ؛ لكثرة تشعبه، وشدَّة تصعبه، حتّى قال فيه بعض الشُّعراء على سبيل التمليح : رَامُوا كَمَالَ مَنْطِقٍ كَيْ يُتْقِنُوا عِلْمَ الكَلَامِ أَنَّى لَهُمْ كَمَالَه. ثمَّ إنَّ المنفصلين اللتين يتركَّب منهما القياس ستَّة أقسام ; لأنَّهما : إمَّا حقيقيَّتان، وإما مانعتا جمع، وإما مانعتا خلو، وإما حقيقيَّة ومانعة جمع، أو خلو، أو مانعة جمع وخلو. مثالُ الحقيقيَّتين: «دائماً إما أن يكون العدد زوجاً، وإما أن يكون فرداً، ودائماً إما أن يكون العدد فرداً وإما أن ينقسم بمتساويين». ومثالُ مانعتي الجمع : «إما أن يكون الموجود جرماً، وإما أن يكون عرضاً، ودائماً إما أن يكون عرضاً وإما أن يكون قائماً بنفسه». ومثال مانعتي الخلو: دائماً إما أن يكون الجرم غير أبيض، وإما أن يكون غير أسود، ودائماً إما أن يكون غير أسود، وإما أن يكون غير أحمر». ومثالُ المركَّب من حقيقيَّة ومانعة جمع : دائماً إما أن يكون الموجود حادثاً، وإما أن يكون قديماً، ودائماً إما أن يكون قديماً، وإما أن يكون عرضاً». ومثالُ المركَّب مِن حقيقيَّة ومانعة خلو : دائماً إما أن يكون الإنسان في البر، وإما أن يكون في البحر، ودائماً إما أن يكون في البحر، وإما أن لا يغرق». ومثالُ المركب من مانعتي جمع وخلو : دائماً إما أن يكون الجرم حجراً، وإما أن يكود حيواناً ، ودائماً إما أن يكون حيواناً، وإما أن يكون غير ناطق . قوله : (لَكِنْ قَدْ يُنْتِجُ بِالنَّظَرِ إِلَى لَوَازِمِ مُقَدِّمَتَيْهِ مِنَ المُتَّصِلَاتِ) بيانه في الحقيقيَّتين: «دائماً إمَّا أن يكون العدد زوجاً، وإما أن يكون فرداً، ودائماً إما أن يكون العدد فرداً، وإما أن ينقسم بمتساويين»، فيستلزم : كلَّما كان العدد زوجاً لم يكن فرداً، وكلما لم يكن فرداً انقسم بمتساويين» ينتج : كلَّما كان العدد زوجاً انقسم بمتساويين. قوله : (وَكَذَا إِذَا رُكَّبَ مِنْ مُتَّصِلَةٍ وَمُنْفَصِلَةٍ) كقولك : كلَّما كان الشَّيء قديماً كان غنيًّا عن الفاعل، ودائماً إما أن يكون غنيا عن الفاعل وإما أن يكون حادثاً، فلا نتيجة له من حيث تركيبه، وإنما ينتج من حيث تركيب لوازمه، فتركَّب الصُّغرى مع لازم الكبرى هكذا : كلما كان الشَّيء قديماً كان غنيًّا عن الفاعل، وكلَّما كان غنيًّا عن الفاعل لم يكن حادثاً، فنجده مِن أوَّل ضروب الشكل الأوّل مستوفياً للشُّروط، فينتج : كلَّما كان الشيء قديماً لم يكن حادثاً.
And sometimes it is composed from two disjunctives with a shared complete part, and this composition is barren. His statement: 'in a complete part' means: the shared [term] which is the middle is one of the two extremes of the conditional in its entirety—either the antecedent in its entirety or the consequent in its entirety. As for the incomplete part, which is a part of the antecedent or consequent, not the whole antecedent or consequent, it is of little use, and so al-Sanusi and al-Qadiri did not address it due to its many branches and extreme difficulty, until some poet said humorously: 'They sought perfection of logic to master the science of kalam—how could they attain its perfection?' Then the two disjunctives from which the syllogism is composed are six types: because they are either both exclusive (haqiqiyyatayn), both separative (mani‘ata jam‘), both inclusive (mani‘ata khuluww), or one exclusive and one separative, or one exclusive and one inclusive, or one separative and one inclusive. Example of both exclusives: 'Always, either the number is even or odd, and always, either the number is odd or divisible into two equal parts.' Example of both separatives: 'Either the existent is a body or an accident, and always, either it is an accident or subsisting by itself.' Example of both inclusives: 'Always, either the body is not white or it is not black, and always, either it is not black or it is not red.' Example of composite from exclusive and separative: 'Always, either the existent is originated or eternal, and always, either it is eternal or an accident.' Example of composite from exclusive and inclusive: 'Always, either man is on land or at sea, and always, either he is at sea or he does not drown.' Example of composite from separative and inclusive: 'Always, either the body is a stone or an animal, and always, either it is an animal or non-rational.' His statement: 'But it may produce a conclusion by considering the necessary implications of its two premises from conditionals.' Its explanation for the two exclusives: 'Always, either the number is even or odd, and always, either the number is odd or divisible by two equal parts,' implies: 'Whenever the number is even, it is not odd; and whenever it is not odd, it is divisible by two equal parts,' producing: 'Whenever the number is even, it is divisible by two equal parts.' His statement: 'And likewise if it is composed from a conditional and a disjunctive' — as you say: 'Whenever a thing is eternal, it is independent of an agent; and always, either it is independent of an agent or originated' — there is no result from its composition, but it results from composing its implications. So the minor premise is composed with the necessary implication of the major as follows: 'Whenever a thing is eternal, it is independent of an agent; and whenever it is independent of an agent, it is not originated.' Then we find it from the first mode of the first figure fulfilling the conditions, producing: 'Whenever a thing is eternal, it is not originated.'
Source pages p. 696

Commentary
This passage is a detailed explanation of compound syllogisms formed from two disjunctive propositions. The author first defines such a composition: a syllogism where the two premises are both disjunctives (munfasilatayn) and share a 'complete part' (juz’ tam), meaning the shared middle term is one entire side (either the whole antecedent or the whole consequent) of each disjunctive. He notes that this composition is 'barren' ('aqim) — that is, it does not directly yield a conclusion. Then he explains that the 'incomplete part' (juz’ ghayr tam), where the shared term is only part of a side, is of little use and was avoided by earlier scholars like al-Sanusi and al-Qadiri due to its difficulty, and he quotes a humorous verse about its complexity. Next, he enumerates the six possible combinations of the two disjunctives based on their types: haqiqiyya (exclusive disjunction, true only when exactly one disjunct is true), mani‘at jam‘ (separative disjunction, preventing coexistence but allowing both false), and mani‘at khuluww (inclusive disjunction, preventing both false but allowing both true). For each combination, he gives an example from logic and natural philosophy: e.g., numbers, bodies, accidents, etc. After presenting the types, he addresses the apparent barrenness: although the direct composition yields no conclusion, one can still derive a result by considering the ‘necessary implications’ (luwāzim) of the premises — that is, by converting the disjunctives into conditionals that follow from them. He illustrates with the two exclusive disjunctives: from 'either even or odd' and 'either odd or divisible by two,' one can infer the conditional 'if even then not odd, and if not odd then divisible by two,' which then yields a conclusion via a hypothetical syllogism (a first figure conditional). He gives a second example of a compound of a conditional and a disjunctive, showing that by taking the necessary implication of the disjunctive as a conditional, a valid categorical syllogism emerges, reducing to the first figure. This demonstrates the advanced technique of using logical implications to salvage what appears to be a useless form.

جزء تام
حقيقية
مانعة جمع
مانعة خلو

The Syllogism
intermediate
10 min
Producing Conclusions from Barren Disjunctive Syllogisms
Author demonstrates how to derive a conclusion from a barren two-disjunctive syllogism using necessary implications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to convert a barren compound disjunctive syllogism into a valid hypothetical syllogism by extracting necessary implications (luwāzim) and using the first figure.

Core Idea
Even a barren disjunctive syllogism can yield a conclusion if you replace each disjunctive with its implied conditional.

The author previously said the compound disjunctive syllogism is barren. Now he reveals how it can still produce a result: by considering the necessary implications of the disjunctives. You will learn this powerful technique.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two disjunctives in the given example
Look at the example: 'Always, either the number is even or odd' and 'Always, either the number is odd or divisible by two.' Write them down.
These are both haqiqiyya (exclusive) disjunctives.
2
Derive the necessary conditional implications
For each disjunctive, extract a conditional that follows necessarily. From 'either even or odd' we get 'if even then not odd' (or 'if odd then not even'). From 'either odd or divisible by two' we get 'if not odd then divisible by two'.
The author calls these 'lamazim' (necessary implications).
3
Compose the conditionals into a hypothetical syllogism
Use the derived conditionals as premises: 'If even then not odd' and 'If not odd then divisible by two'. This is a conditional syllogism. Check if it fits a valid figure.
This is a first-figure hypothetical syllogism (transitivity of implication).
4
State the final conclusion
The conclusion is: 'If the number is even, then it is divisible by two.' This is derived from the two conditionals by hypothetical syllogism.
The author shows that by this method, the barren syllogism becomes productive.

وَتَارَةٌ يُرَكَّبُ مِنْ مُنْفَصِلَتَيْنِ وَالشِّرْكَةِ بَيْنَهُمَا فِي جُزْءٍ تَامٌ، وَهَذَا مِنْ حَيْثُ تَرْكِيبُهُ عَقِيمٌ. قوله : (فِي جُزءٍ نَامٌ) المرادُ بـ «الجزء السَّام»: أن يكون المشترك وهو الوسط أحد طرفي الشَّرطيَّة بكماله ؛ إمَّا المقدَّم بكماله ، وإمَّا التّالي بكماله، وأمَّا الجزء الغير التّامّ وهو ما يكون جزءاً مِنَ المقدَّم أوِ التَّالي لا جميع المقدَّم أوِ التَّالي، فقليلُ الجدوى، ولذا لم يتعرّض له السنوسي والقادري ؛ لكثرة تشعبه، وشدَّة تصعبه، حتّى قال فيه بعض الشُّعراء على سبيل التمليح : رَامُوا كَمَالَ مَنْطِقٍ كَيْ يُتْقِنُوا عِلْمَ الكَلَامِ أَنَّى لَهُمْ كَمَالَه. ثمَّ إنَّ المنفصلين اللتين يتركَّب منهما القياس ستَّة أقسام ; لأنَّهما : إمَّا حقيقيَّتان، وإما مانعتا جمع، وإما مانعتا خلو، وإما حقيقيَّة ومانعة جمع، أو خلو، أو مانعة جمع وخلو. مثالُ الحقيقيَّتين: «دائماً إما أن يكون العدد زوجاً، وإما أن يكون فرداً، ودائماً إما أن يكون العدد فرداً وإما أن ينقسم بمتساويين». ومثالُ مانعتي الجمع : «إما أن يكون الموجود جرماً، وإما أن يكون عرضاً، ودائماً إما أن يكون عرضاً وإما أن يكون قائماً بنفسه». ومثال مانعتي الخلو: دائماً إما أن يكون الجرم غير أبيض، وإما أن يكون غير أسود، ودائماً إما أن يكون غير أسود، وإما أن يكون غير أحمر». ومثالُ المركَّب من حقيقيَّة ومانعة جمع : دائماً إما أن يكون الموجود حادثاً، وإما أن يكون قديماً، ودائماً إما أن يكون قديماً، وإما أن يكون عرضاً». ومثالُ المركَّب مِن حقيقيَّة ومانعة خلو : دائماً إما أن يكون الإنسان في البر، وإما أن يكون في البحر، ودائماً إما أن يكون في البحر، وإما أن لا يغرق». ومثالُ المركب من مانعتي جمع وخلو : دائماً إما أن يكون الجرم حجراً، وإما أن يكود حيواناً ، ودائماً إما أن يكون حيواناً، وإما أن يكون غير ناطق . قوله : (لَكِنْ قَدْ يُنْتِجُ بِالنَّظَرِ إِلَى لَوَازِمِ مُقَدِّمَتَيْهِ مِنَ المُتَّصِلَاتِ) بيانه في الحقيقيَّتين: «دائماً إمَّا أن يكون العدد زوجاً، وإما أن يكون فرداً، ودائماً إما أن يكون العدد فرداً، وإما أن ينقسم بمتساويين»، فيستلزم : كلَّما كان العدد زوجاً لم يكن فرداً، وكلما لم يكن فرداً انقسم بمتساويين» ينتج : كلَّما كان العدد زوجاً انقسم بمتساويين. قوله : (وَكَذَا إِذَا رُكَّبَ مِنْ مُتَّصِلَةٍ وَمُنْفَصِلَةٍ) كقولك : كلَّما كان الشَّيء قديماً كان غنيًّا عن الفاعل، ودائماً إما أن يكون غنيا عن الفاعل وإما أن يكون حادثاً، فلا نتيجة له من حيث تركيبه، وإنما ينتج من حيث تركيب لوازمه، فتركَّب الصُّغرى مع لازم الكبرى هكذا : كلما كان الشَّيء قديماً كان غنيًّا عن الفاعل، وكلَّما كان غنيًّا عن الفاعل لم يكن حادثاً، فنجده مِن أوَّل ضروب الشكل الأوّل مستوفياً للشُّروط، فينتج : كلَّما كان الشيء قديماً لم يكن حادثاً.
And sometimes it is composed from two disjunctives with a shared complete part, and this composition is barren. His statement: 'in a complete part' means: the shared [term] which is the middle is one of the two extremes of the conditional in its entirety—either the antecedent in its entirety or the consequent in its entirety. As for the incomplete part, which is a part of the antecedent or consequent, not the whole antecedent or consequent, it is of little use, and so al-Sanusi and al-Qadiri did not address it due to its many branches and extreme difficulty, until some poet said humorously: 'They sought perfection of logic to master the science of kalam—how could they attain its perfection?' Then the two disjunctives from which the syllogism is composed are six types: because they are either both exclusive (haqiqiyyatayn), both separative (mani‘ata jam‘), both inclusive (mani‘ata khuluww), or one exclusive and one separative, or one exclusive and one inclusive, or one separative and one inclusive. Example of both exclusives: 'Always, either the number is even or odd, and always, either the number is odd or divisible into two equal parts.' Example of both separatives: 'Either the existent is a body or an accident, and always, either it is an accident or subsisting by itself.' Example of both inclusives: 'Always, either the body is not white or it is not black, and always, either it is not black or it is not red.' Example of composite from exclusive and separative: 'Always, either the existent is originated or eternal, and always, either it is eternal or an accident.' Example of composite from exclusive and inclusive: 'Always, either man is on land or at sea, and always, either he is at sea or he does not drown.' Example of composite from separative and inclusive: 'Always, either the body is a stone or an animal, and always, either it is an animal or non-rational.' His statement: 'But it may produce a conclusion by considering the necessary implications of its two premises from conditionals.' Its explanation for the two exclusives: 'Always, either the number is even or odd, and always, either the number is odd or divisible by two equal parts,' implies: 'Whenever the number is even, it is not odd; and whenever it is not odd, it is divisible by two equal parts,' producing: 'Whenever the number is even, it is divisible by two equal parts.' His statement: 'And likewise if it is composed from a conditional and a disjunctive' — as you say: 'Whenever a thing is eternal, it is independent of an agent; and always, either it is independent of an agent or originated' — there is no result from its composition, but it results from composing its implications. So the minor premise is composed with the necessary implication of the major as follows: 'Whenever a thing is eternal, it is independent of an agent; and whenever it is independent of an agent, it is not originated.' Then we find it from the first mode of the first figure fulfilling the conditions, producing: 'Whenever a thing is eternal, it is not originated.'
Source pages p. 696

Commentary
This passage is a detailed explanation of compound syllogisms formed from two disjunctive propositions. The author first defines such a composition: a syllogism where the two premises are both disjunctives (munfasilatayn) and share a 'complete part' (juz’ tam), meaning the shared middle term is one entire side (either the whole antecedent or the whole consequent) of each disjunctive. He notes that this composition is 'barren' ('aqim) — that is, it does not directly yield a conclusion. Then he explains that the 'incomplete part' (juz’ ghayr tam), where the shared term is only part of a side, is of little use and was avoided by earlier scholars like al-Sanusi and al-Qadiri due to its difficulty, and he quotes a humorous verse about its complexity. Next, he enumerates the six possible combinations of the two disjunctives based on their types: haqiqiyya (exclusive disjunction, true only when exactly one disjunct is true), mani‘at jam‘ (separative disjunction, preventing coexistence but allowing both false), and mani‘at khuluww (inclusive disjunction, preventing both false but allowing both true). For each combination, he gives an example from logic and natural philosophy: e.g., numbers, bodies, accidents, etc. After presenting the types, he addresses the apparent barrenness: although the direct composition yields no conclusion, one can still derive a result by considering the ‘necessary implications’ (luwāzim) of the premises — that is, by converting the disjunctives into conditionals that follow from them. He illustrates with the two exclusive disjunctives: from 'either even or odd' and 'either odd or divisible by two,' one can infer the conditional 'if even then not odd, and if not odd then divisible by two,' which then yields a conclusion via a hypothetical syllogism (a first figure conditional). He gives a second example of a compound of a conditional and a disjunctive, showing that by taking the necessary implication of the disjunctive as a conditional, a valid categorical syllogism emerges, reducing to the first figure. This demonstrates the advanced technique of using logical implications to salvage what appears to be a useless form.

Key Terms
لازم
عقيم
منفصلتان
متصلات

Key Takeaway
A barren compound disjunctive syllogism becomes productive when you extract the conditional implications of its premises.

The Syllogism
intermediate
7 min
Deriving Conclusions from Compound Syllogisms via Implications
Author explains that compound syllogisms of mixed premises do not directly produce a conclusion but can do so via necessary implications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to derive a valid conclusion from a compound syllogism by identifying and using the necessary implications (lawāzim) of its premises.

Core Idea
Compound syllogisms yield conclusions only through the implications of their premises, not directly.

In classical logic, a syllogism composed of two conditional premises—both conjunctive (muttasila) or one conjunctive and one disjunctive (munfasila)—does not produce a conclusion from its overt form. However, the author shows that by analyzing the necessary implications (lawāzim) of the premises, we can extract a valid syllogism. This lesson will guide you through that process using the examples from the text, particularly the theological example about eternity and dependence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Premises
Locate the two conditional premises in the compound syllogism. In the eternal example: (1) 'Whenever a thing is eternal, it is independent of an agent' (conjunctive), (2) 'Always it is either independent of an agent or temporal' (disjunctive).
The second premise is a disjunctive ('either-or'), not a conditional.
2
Classify Each Premise
Determine the type: the first is a conjunctive conditional (muttasila), the second is a disjunctive (munfasila). This classification affects what implications can be drawn.
A conjunctive states a conditional relationship; a disjunctive offers alternatives.
3
Extract the Necessary Implication (Lazim)
For the disjunctive premise, derive its implication: from 'either independent or temporal', we get 'if independent then not temporal' (i.e., 'whenever it is independent, it is not temporal'). This is a necessary implication.
The implication must be logically entailed by the original premise.
4
Recombine into a Valid Syllogism
Combine the first premise with the implication of the second: 'Whenever eternal, independent' and 'Whenever independent, not temporal'. This forms a first-figure categorical syllogism: A→B, B→C, therefore A→C.
The new syllogism is now in the first figure (Barbara) and fulfills its conditions.
5
State the Conclusion
The conclusion is the consequent of the chained conditionals: 'Whenever a thing is eternal, it is not temporal'. This follows necessarily from the implications of the original premises.
The original compound syllogism does not give this directly; only after extracting implications does it become productive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لَا إِنْتَاجَ لَهُ، لَكِنْ قَدْ يُنْتِجُ بِالنَّظَرِ إِلَى لَوَازِمِ مُقَدِّمَتَيْهِ مِنَ المُتَّصِلَاتِ، كَمَا يُعْلَمُ مِنْ شَرْح المُخْتَصَرِ، وَكَذَا إِذَا رُكَّبَ مِنْ مُتَّصِلَةٍ وَمُنْفَصِلَةٍ . قوله : (لَكِنْ قَدْ يُنْتِجُ بِالنَّظَرِ إِلَى لَوَازِمِ مُقَدِّمَتَيْهِ مِنَ المُتَّصِلَاتِ) بيانه في الحقيقيَّتين: «دائماً إمَّا أن يكون العدد زوجاً، وإما أن يكون فرداً، ودائماً إما أن يكون العدد فرداً، وإما أن ينقسم بمتساويين»، فيستلزم : كلَّما كان العدد زوجاً لم يكن فرداً، وكلما لم يكن فرداً انقسم بمتساويين» ينتج : كلَّما كان العدد زوجاً انقسم بمتساويين. قوله : (وَكَذَا إِذَا رُكَّبَ مِنْ مُتَّصِلَةٍ وَمُنْفَصِلَةٍ) كقولك : كلَّما كان الشَّيء قديماً كان غنيًّا عن الفاعل، ودائماً إما أن يكون غنيا عن الفاعل وإما أن يكون حادثاً، فلا نتيجة له من حيث تركيبه، وإنما ينتج من حيث تركيب لوازمه، فتركَّب الصُّغرى مع لازم الكبرى هكذا : كلما كان الشَّيء قديماً كان غنيًّا عن الفاعل، وكلَّما كان غنيًّا عن الفاعل لم يكن حادثاً، فنجده مِن أوَّل ضروب الشكل الأوّل مستوفياً للشُّروط، فينتج : كلَّما كان الشيء قديماً لم يكن حادثاً».
It has no production, but it may produce by considering the necessary implications of its two premises from conjunctives, as is known from Sharh al-Mukhtasar. Likewise when it is composed of a conjunctive and a disjunctive. His statement: (But it may produce by considering the necessary implications of its two premises from conjunctives) its explanation in the two real conjunctives: 'Always the number is either even or odd, and always the number is either odd or divisible into two equal parts,' entails: 'Whenever the number is even it is not odd, and whenever it is not odd it is divisible into two equal parts' produces: 'Whenever the number is even it is divisible into two equal parts.' His statement: (And likewise when composed of a conjunctive and a disjunctive) as your saying: 'Whenever a thing is eternal it is independent of an agent, and always it is either independent of an agent or temporal,' so there is no result from its composition, but it produces from the composition of its implications. So the minor premise is combined with the implication of the major thus: 'Whenever a thing is eternal it is independent of an agent, and whenever it is independent of an agent it is not temporal.' We find it from the first mode of the first figure fulfilling the conditions, so it produces: 'Whenever a thing is eternal, it is not temporal.'
Source pages p. 697

Commentary
This passage discusses compound syllogisms that do not yield a conclusion directly from their given premises. The author explains that such syllogisms can still produce a result by considering the necessary implications (لَوَازِم) of the premises. Two cases are given: first, a compound of two conjunctive conditionals (مُتَّصِلَتَان), with the example of even/odd numbers. The two conjunctives are: 'Always the number is either even or odd' and 'Always the number is either odd or divisible into two equal parts.' From these, we derive the implication that whenever the number is even, it is not odd, and whenever it is not odd, it is divisible into two equal parts. Thus, the conclusion 'Whenever the number is even, it is divisible into two equal parts' follows. Second, a compound of a conjunctive and a disjunctive premise. The example: 'Whenever a thing is eternal, it is independent of an agent' (conjunctive) and 'Always it is either independent of an agent or temporal' (disjunctive). Here, no conclusion arises from the direct combination, but when we take the implication of the major premise (that being independent of an agent implies not being temporal), we can combine it with the minor to form a valid first figure syllogism: 'Whenever a thing is eternal, it is independent of an agent; whenever it is independent of an agent, it is not temporal; therefore, whenever a thing is eternal, it is not temporal.' The author thus illustrates how compound syllogisms can be productive through indirect reasoning.

Key Terms
لَوَازِم
مُتَّصِلَة
مُنْفَصِلَة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Compound syllogisms of mixed conditional premises do not produce a conclusion directly; you must convert the premises into their necessary implications to construct a valid syllogism.

Understanding Deletion (Ḥadhf) in Syllogisms
Author explains permissibility of omitting known premises or conclusions in syllogisms, with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and reconstruct deleted premises or conclusions in categorical, compound, and exceptive syllogisms from Islamic logical texts.

Core Idea
Deletion is permissible when the omitted premise or conclusion is known contextually—a tool for concision in logical discourse.

In the Islamic logical tradition, syllogisms are often presented in abbreviated form: a premise or the conclusion may be omitted if it is obvious to the audience. This lesson examines the rules of deletion (ḥadhf) as explained in the commentary on al-Bannānī's logic text. Understanding deletion is crucial for reconstructing complete arguments from real-world juristic and theological debates. The passage provides clear examples from categorical syllogisms, compound syllogisms (where the conclusion of one becomes the premise of the next), and exceptive syllogisms, with a note correcting a potential misunderstanding.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Type of Syllogism
First, determine whether the argument is a simple categorical syllogism, a compound syllogism (qiyās murakkab), or an exceptive syllogism (qiyās istithnāʾī). Each type has its own conventions for deletion.
The passage gives examples of all three types.
2
Locate the Deleted Part
Find which element is omitted: the major premise, the minor premise, or the conclusion. In a compound syllogism, the conclusion of the first syllogism may be deleted if it becomes the premise of the second. In an exceptive syllogism, deletion is also allowed.
In the first example, 'كل إنسان حيوان فهو جسم', the major premise is deleted. In the compound example about An-Nabbas, the conclusion of the first syllogism is deleted.
3
Reconstruct the Deleted Part from Context
Using the stated premises and the conclusion, infer the missing premise or conclusion. The key is that the deleted part must be clearly known from the context or from common knowledge. For the first example, the deleted major is 'كل حيوان جسم' because the syllogism is valid only if that is supplied.
Reconstruction must make the syllogism valid and conform to the rules of categorical logic.
4
Verify the Validity of the Reconstructed Syllogism
Once you supply the missing part, check whether the syllogism is valid. For categorical syllogisms, identify the figure and mood. For compound syllogisms, ensure each step is valid. For exceptive syllogisms, confirm the modus ponens or tollens structure.
The first example is a valid first-figure (Barbara) syllogism: Every human is an animal, every animal is a body, so every human is a body.
5
Recognize the Permissibility in Exceptive Syllogisms
The commentator explicitly states that deletion is also allowed in exceptive syllogisms, correcting a potential misimpression from the versifier's ordering. This shows that the rule is general, not restricted to categorical syllogisms.
No example is given in the passage, but the principle is affirmed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَالَ النَّاظِمُ : (وَالحَذْفُ فِي بَعْضِ المُقَدِّمَاتِ أَوِ النَّتِيجَةِ لِلْعِلْمِ آتِ يَعْنِي: أَنَّهُ يَجُوزُ الحَذْفُ فِي إِحْدَى المُقَدِّمَتَيْنِ لِلعِلْمِ بِهَا، وَيَجُوزُ ذَلِكَ فِي النَّتِيجَةِ أَيْضاً ، فَنَقُولُ فِي حَذْفِ الكُبْرَى : كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ حَيَوَانٌ، فَهُوَ جِسْمٌ، وَتَقْدِيرُهَا : وَكُلُّ حَيَوَانٍ جِسْمٌ، وَأَكْثَرُ مَا تُحْذَفُ الصُّغُرَى وَالنَّتِيجَةُ لِلْعِلْم بِهِمَا فِي القِيَاسِ المُرَكَّبِ كَمَا سَيَأْتِي؛ نَحْوُ : «النَّبَّاسُ آخِذٌ لِلْمَالِ خُفْيَةً، وَكُلُّ آخِذِ لِلمَالِ خُفْيَةً سَارِقٌ، وَكُلُّ سَارِقٍ تُقْطَعُ يَدُهُ» ، فَقَدْ حُذِفَتْ فِيهِ النَّتِيجَةُ مِنَ القِيَاسِ الأَوَّلِ وَهِيَ صُغْرَى لِلثَّانِي، وَهِيَ قَوْلُنَا : النَّبَّاسُ سَارِقٌ، وَيَجُوزُ الحَذْفُ فِي القِيَاسِ الِاسْتِثْنَائِيِّ أَيْضاً، قوله: (وَيَجُوزُ الْحَذْفُ فِي الْقِيَاسِ الِاسْتِثْنَائِي ... إلخ) أشار بهذا إلى الرَّدِّ على الناظم، حيث ذكر البيت قبل القياس الاستثنائي، فإنَّه يُوهم أنَّ هذا لا يجوز في الاستثنائي، فلذلك نصَّ عليه هنا الشَّارح بقوله : ويجوز ... إلخ».
The versifier said: (And deletion in some premises or the conclusion for knowledge comes) meaning: it is permissible to delete one of the two premises due to knowledge of it, and that is also permissible in the conclusion. So we say in deletion of the major: 'Every human is an animal, therefore it is a body,' and its estimated [minor] is 'Every animal is a body.' And most often the minor and the conclusion are deleted due to knowledge of them in compound syllogism, as will come; for example: 'An-Nabbas takes property secretly, and every taker of property secretly is a thief, and every thief his hand is cut off.' So in it the conclusion from the first syllogism is deleted, which is the minor for the second, and it is our saying: 'An-Nabbas is a thief.' And deletion is also permissible in the exceptive syllogism. His statement: (And deletion is permissible in the exceptive syllogism... etc.) indicated by this a refutation of the versifier, where he mentioned the verse before the exceptive syllogism, for it implies that this is not permissible in the exceptive. Therefore the commentator explicitly stated it here by saying 'and it is permissible... etc.'
Source pages p. 697

Commentary
This passage explains the concept of deletion (حَذْف) in syllogisms. The author states that it is permissible to omit a premise or the conclusion when it is known from context. Three types are illustrated: deletion of the major premise (e.g., 'Every human is an animal, therefore it is a body' where the deleted major is 'Every animal is a body'), deletion of the minor premise, and deletion of the conclusion. The example given is a compound syllogism about An-Nabbas (a thief): the first syllogism's conclusion ('An-Nabbas is a thief') is omitted because it becomes the minor premise of the second syllogism. The author also notes that deletion is allowed in exceptive syllogisms (qiyas istithna'i), and this is a refutation of the versifier (matn author) who implied otherwise by placing a verse about deletion before discussing exceptive syllogisms. The commentator clarifies that the matn's placement might be misleading, so he explicitly affirms that deletion applies to exceptive syllogisms as well.

Key Terms
حَذْف
مُقَدِّمَة
نَتِيجَة
قِيَاس مُرَكَّب
قِيَاس اسْتِثْنَائِي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The major premise of the first syllogism
B) The minor premise of the first syllogism
C) The conclusion of the first syllogism

Key Takeaway
Deletion (ḥadhf) of premises or conclusions is permissible in both categorical and exceptive syllogisms when the omitted part is contextually known, allowing concise reasoning in Islamic scholarly discourse.
