The Syllogism
intermediate
5 min
Definition of the Exceptive Syllogism and Its Distinction from the Conjunctive
Author defines the exceptive syllogism and distinguishes it from the conjunctive syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the defining features of the exceptive syllogism and distinguish it from the conjunctive syllogism in Islamic logic texts.

Core Idea
The exceptive syllogism states its conclusion explicitly; the conjunctive hides it.

In this passage, al-Bajuri defines the exceptive syllogism (al-qiyās al-istithnā'ī) and explains how it differs from the conjunctive syllogism. The key distinction is that the exceptive syllogism contains its conclusion actually (bi'l-fi'l), while the conjunctive contains it only potentially. Understanding this difference is crucial for identifying syllogism types in Islamic logical works.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
Find the term 'al-istithnā'ī' (the exceptive) and note that it is also called 'al-sharṭī' (conditional). The major premise must be a conditional, and the minor premise must contain the exception particle 'lakin' (but).
2
Extract the genus and differentia
The genus is 'qiyās' (syllogism). The differentia is that it 'indicates the conclusion or its opposite actually, not potentially'. This means the conclusion is explicitly stated in the syllogism itself.
3
Understand 'actually' vs 'potentially'
In the conjunctive syllogism, the conclusion is not stated but implied by the premises; it is present 'potentially'. In the exceptive, the conclusion is spelled out: 'so daytime exists' is written after the exceptive premise.
4
Apply to the example
The example: 'If the sun is shining, then daytime exists; but the sun is shining; therefore daytime exists.' The conclusion 'daytime exists' is explicitly stated. This is an exceptive syllogism with modus ponens.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ فِي الاسْتِثْنَائِي. (وَمِنْهُ مَا يُدْعَى بِـ : الاسْتِثْنَائِي يُعْرَفُ بِـ : «الشَّرْطِيْ» بِلَا امْتِرَاءِ) وَهْوَ : الَّذِي دَلَّ عَلَى النَّتِيْجَةِ أَوْضِدِّهَا بِالفِعْلِ لَا بِالقُوَّةِ لَمَّا فَرَغَ مِنْ أَوَّلِ قِسْمَيِ القِيَاسِ وَهُوَ الِاقْتِرَانِيُّ، شَرَعَ فِي ثَانِيهِمَا وَهُوَ الِاسْتِثْنَائِيُّ، وَذَكَرَ أَنَّهُ يُسَمَّى: «شَرْطِيّا» أَيْضاً ، وَذَلِكَ لِأَنَّ مُقَدِّمَتَهُ الأُوْلَى لَا تَكُونُ إِلَّا شَرْطِيَّةٌ، وَمُقَدِّمَتَهُ الأُخْرَى لَا تَكُونُ إِلَّا اسْتِثْنَائِيَّةٌ ؛ أَي : مُشْتَمِلَةٌ عَلَى حَرْفِ الاِسْتِثْنَاءِ، وَهُوَ: «لَكِنْ»؛ لِأَنَّ المُسْتَدِلَّ بِهَا يَنْعَطِفُ بِهَا عَلَى مَا ذَكَرَ فِي الشَّرْطِيَّةِ ، فَيَضَعُهُ أَوْ يَرْفَعُهُ؛ قَالَهُ السَّيِّدُ. وَعَرَّفَ المُصَنِّفُ هَذَا القِسْمَ بِأَنَّهُ الَّذِي دَلَّ عَلَى النَّتِيجَةِ أَوْ ضِدّهَا بِالفِعْلِ لَا بِالقُوَّةِ، وَبِقَوْلِهِ : بِالفِعْلِ لَا بِالقُوَّةِ خَرَجَ الاِقْتِرَانِيُّ ؛ لِأَنَّهُ مُشْتَمِلٌ عَلَى نَتِيجَتِهِ بِالقُوَّةِ كَمَا تَقَدَّمَ، لَا بِالفِعْلِ. وَمَعْنَى كَوْنِ هَذَا القِسْمِ مُشْتَمِلاً عَلَيْهَا بِالفِعْلِ أَنْ تَكُونَ مَذْكُورَةٌ فِيهِ بِمَادَّتِهَا : وَصُورَتِهَا، وَكَذَا عَلَى نَقِيضِهَا وَإِنْ كَانَتْ فِي القِيَاسِ جُزْءَ قَضِيَّةٍ، لَا قَضِيَّةٍ كَامِلَةٍ، وَلَا مُحْتَمِلَة لِلصِّدْقِ وَالكَذِبِ، وَصَارَتْ فِي النَّتِيجَةِ قَضِيَّةٌ كَامِلَةٌ مُحْتَمِلَةٌ لِلصِّدْقِ وَالكَذِبِ، وَبِهَذَا الاعْتِبَارِ تَكُونُ مُغَايرَةٌ لِمُقَدِّمَتَي القِيَاسِ؛ كَمَا قَالَ في حَدٌ القِيَاسِ : «مُسْتَلْزِماً بِالذَّاتِ قَوْلاً آخَرَ ، فَالمُشْتَمِلُ عَلَيْهَا بِالفِعْلِ ؛ كَقَوْلِنَا : إِنْ كَانَتِ الشَّمْسُ طَالِعَةٌ فَالنَّهَارُ مَوْجُودٌ، لَكِنَّ الشَّمْسَ طَالِعَةٌ فَالنَّهَارُ مَوْجُودٌ» ، فَقَوْلُنَا آخِراً : «فَالنَّهَارُ مَوْجُودٌ» هُوَ النَّتِيجَةُ ، وَتَرْكِيبُهُ
Chapter on the Exceptive. (And from it is what is called the exceptive, known as the 'conditional' without doubt.) And it is that which indicates the conclusion or its opposite actually, not potentially. When he finished from the first of the two divisions of the syllogism, which is the conjunctive, he began on the second of them, which is the exceptive. And he mentioned that it is also called 'conditional', because its first premise cannot be except a conditional, and its other premise cannot be except exceptive, i.e., containing a letter of exception, which is 'but' (lakin). Because the arguer with it turns with it to what he mentioned in the conditional, affirming it or negating it. Al-Sayyid said this. And the author defined this division as that which indicates the conclusion or its opposite actually, not potentially. By his saying 'actually, not potentially', the conjunctive is excluded, because it contains its conclusion potentially as previously, not actually. And the meaning of this division containing it actually is that it is mentioned in it with its matter and form, and likewise its opposite, even if in the syllogism it is part of a proposition, not a complete proposition, and not subject to truth and falsehood, and it becomes in the conclusion a complete proposition subject to truth and falsehood. And in this consideration it is different from the two premises of the syllogism, as he said in the definition of syllogism: 'necessarily entailing another statement by itself.' So that which contains it actually is like our saying: 'If the sun is shining then daytime exists, but the sun is shining, so daytime exists.' So our saying at the end: 'so daytime exists' is the conclusion, and its composition...
Source pages p. 700

Commentary
This passage defines the exceptive syllogism (al-qiyās al-istithnā'ī) and distinguishes it from the conjunctive (al-iqtirānī). The author first names it also 'conditional' because its major premise is always a conditional (sharṭiyya) and its minor premise is exceptive (istithnā'iyya), containing the exception particle 'but' (lakin). The definition given is that it indicates the conclusion or its opposite actually (bil-fi'l), not potentially (bil-quwwa). This means the conclusion is explicitly stated in the syllogism with its matter and form, whereas in the conjunctive syllogism the conclusion is only potentially present and needs to be extracted. Even though the conclusion appears as part of a proposition within the syllogism, it becomes a complete proposition in the conclusion. The author provides an example: 'If the sun is shining, then daytime exists; but the sun is shining; therefore daytime exists.' The conclusion 'daytime exists' is actually stated. The passage also notes that the definition of syllogism as 'necessarily entailing another statement' applies to both types, but in the exceptive the entailment is actual.

Key Terms
استثنائي (istithnā'ī): exceptive syllogism
بِالفِعْلِ (bil-fi'l): actually, explicitly
لَكِنْ (lakin): but (the exception particle)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Potentially, needing extraction.
B) Actually, explicitly stated in the premise.
C) Not at all; it is inferred outside.
D) Only by analogy.

Key Takeaway
The exceptive syllogism is distinguished by containing its conclusion explicitly (bi'l-fi'l) and using a conditional major premise with an exceptive minor premise containing 'lakin'.

The Syllogism
beginner
5 min
Affirming the Antecedent (Modus Ponens) in Conditional Syllogism
States the first valid mood of conditional syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a valid modus ponens argument using an Islamic theological conditional and verify its conclusion.

Core Idea
If the antecedent is true, the consequent must be true.

In conditional syllogisms (qiyas istithna'i muttasil), the first valid mood is affirming the antecedent (al-muqaddam). Al-Bajuri and al-Bannani explain that if the exceptive premise asserts the antecedent, the conclusion follows the consequent. This is the foundation of many kalam proofs, such as the argument that the world is originated because it is a body.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional major premise
Find the 'if...then' statement. In the Arabic: 'كُلَّمَا كَانَ المَوْجُودُ جِرْماً كَانَ حَادِثاً' – 'Whenever an existing thing is a body, it is originated.'
2
Locate the exceptive premise (istithna')
The exceptive premise must affirm the antecedent. Here: 'لَكِنَّهُ جِرْمٌ' – 'but it is a body.'
The term 'waḍ' (وضع) means affirmation.
3
Derive the conclusion
Since the antecedent is affirmed, the conclusion is the consequent: 'فَهُوَ حَادِثٌ' – 'so it is originated.'
This is modus ponens.
4
Justify with the principle
The existence of the relatum (malzum) necessitates the existence of the relation (lazim). Here, being a body entails being originated.
This is stated explicitly in the commentary.
5
Apply the tool to a new example
Given 'If something is a substance (jawhar), it is originated' and 'This is a substance', conclude 'It is originated.'
Test with other kalam examples.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَإِنْ يَكُ الشَّرْطِيُّ ذَا انْصَالِ: أَنْتَجَ وَضْعُ ذَاكَ وَضْعَ النَّالِـي (103) وَرَفْعُ تَالٍ رَفْعَ أَوَّلِ، وَلَا يَلْزَمُ فِي عَكْسِهِمَا لِمَا انْجَلَى. القِيَاسُ الِاسْتِثْنَائِيُّ يَنْقَسِمُ: إِلَى اتِّصَالِيٌّ، وَهُوَ: مَا كَانَتْ شَرْطِيَّتُهُ مُتَّصِلَةٌ. وَإِلَى انْفِصَالِيٌّ، وَهُوَ: مَا كَانَتْ شَرْطِيَّتُهُ مُنْفَصِلَةٌ. وَذَكَرَ أَنَّهُ يُشْتَرَطُ فِي الاِتِّصَالِي أَنْ تَكُونَ الإِسْتِثْنَائِيَّةُ فِيهِ حَكَمَتْ بِوَضْعِ مُقَدَّم الشَّرْطِيَّةِ؛ أَيْ: إِثْبَاتِهِ، أَوْ بِرَفْعِ تَالِيهَا؛ أَيْ: نَفْيِهِ، وَيُنْتِجُ فِي الأَوَّلِ عَيْنَ التَّالِي؛ لِأَنَّ وُجُودَ المَلْزُومِ يُوجِبُ وُجُودَ اللَّازِمِ، وَفِي الثَّانِي نَقِيضَ المُقَدَّمِ؛ لِأَنَّ نَفْيَ اللَّازِمِ يُوجِبُ نَفْيَ المَلْزُومِ، وَلَا يُنْتِجُ الاِسْتِثْنَائِيُّ شَيْئاً بِرَفْعِ المُقَدَّمِ، وَلَا بِوَضْعِ التَّالِي؛ لِجَوَازِ كَوْنِ اللَّازِم أَعَمَّ مِنَ المَلْزُوم، فَيَلْزَمُ مِنْ ثُبُوتِ الأَخَصَّ ثُبُوتُ الأَعَمّ، وَمِنْ نَفْيِ الأَعَمِّ نَفْيُ الأَخَصَّ. وَلَا يَلْزَمُ مِنْ نَفْيِ الأَخَصُ نَفْيُ الأَعَمِّ وَلَا مِنْ ثُبُوتِ الأَعَمِّ ثُبُوتُ الأَخَصّ. مَثَلاً: إِذَا قُلْنَا كُلَّمَا كَانَ المَوْجُودُ جِرْماً كَانَ حَادِثاً، لَكِنَّهُ جِرْمٌ، أَنْتَجَ: فَهُوَ حَادِثٌ، أَوْ قُلْنَا: لَكِنَّهُ لَيْسَ بِحَادِثٍ أَنْتَجَ: فَهُوَ لَيْسَ بِجِرْمٍ، وَلَا إِنْتَاجَ فِي عَكْسِهِمَا لِمَا مَرَّ. قَالَ النَّاظِمُ: (فَإِنْ يَكُ الشَّرْطِيُّ) قوله: (وَلَا إِنْتَاجَ فِي عَكْسِهِمَا) بيانُهُ: أَنَّكَ إِذا رفعت الأوّل بالاستثنائيَّة؛ كأن تقول فى المثال المذكور: لكنه ليس بجرم، فلا يلزم كونه ليس حادثاً ولا: «كونه حادثاً؛ لأنَّ المقدَّم قد يكون أخصَّ مِنَ التَّالي كما في المثال؛ إذ كونه جرماً أخص من كونه حادثاً، ولا يلزم رفع الأخص رفع الأعم ولا ثبوته. وكذا وضع التّالي لا يستلزم ثبوت المقدَّم.
If the conditional has connection: affirming that (the antecedent) produces the affirmation of the consequent, and denying the consequent produces the denial of the first, and the converse is not entailed, as is clear. The conditional syllogism is divided into: the connected, which is its conditional is connected; and the disjoined, which is its conditional is disjoined. And he mentioned that in the connected it is required that the exceptive clause rules by affirming the antecedent of the conditional, i.e., establishing it, or by denying its consequent, i.e., negating it. And it produces in the first the exact consequent, because the existence of the relatum requires the existence of the relation; and in the second the opposite of the antecedent, because denying the relation requires denying the relatum. And the conditional syllogism does not produce anything by denying the antecedent nor by affirming the consequent, because it is possible that the relation is more general than the relatum. Thus from the affirmation of the more specific follows the affirmation of the more general, and from the denial of the more general follows the denial of the more specific. And it does not follow from the denial of the more specific the denial of the more general, nor from the affirmation of the more general the affirmation of the more specific. For example: if we say 'Whenever an existing thing is a body, it is originated; but it is a body', it produces: 'so it is originated'; or we say 'but it is not originated', it produces: 'so it is not a body'. And there is no production in their converse for what has passed. The author said: (If the conditional is with connection) His statement: (And no production in their converse) its explanation: that if you deny the first by the exceptive, such as you say in the mentioned example: 'But it is not a body', then it does not entail that it is not originated nor that it is originated, because the antecedent might be more specific than the consequent as in the example; since being a body is more specific than being originated. And the denial of the specific does not entail the denial of the general nor its affirmation. Likewise, affirming the consequent does not entail the affirmation of the antecedent.
Source pages p. 700

Commentary
This passage presents the core rules of the conditional syllogism (al-istithnā'ī) with its division into connected (muttasil) and disjoined (munfasil). The focus is on the connected conditional. The matn verses state the two valid moods: affirming the antecedent produces the affirmation of the consequent, and denying the consequent produces the denial of the antecedent. The reverse moods are invalid. The commentary explains the underlying reason: the relation (lāzim) may be more general than the relatum (malzūm). For example, 'being a body' is more specific than 'being originated'—everything that is a body is originated, but not everything originated is a body. Therefore, if we affirm the antecedent (being a body), we can affirm the consequent (originated). If we deny the consequent (not originated), we can deny the antecedent (not a body). However, if we deny the antecedent (not a body), we cannot conclude anything about being originated, because the thing might be originated through another cause. Similarly, affirming the consequent (originated) does not allow us to affirm the antecedent (body), because the thing could be originated without being a body (e.g., an accident). The passage thus establishes the necessary conditions for a valid conditional syllogism and warns against the common fallacy of affirming the consequent or denying the antecedent.

Key Terms
الانصَال
وَضْع
المَلْزُوم
اللَّازِم
اتصال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the conclusion when the antecedent is affirmed in a connected conditional?
A) The negation of the consequent
B) The exact consequent
C) The negation of the antecedent
D) No conclusion

Key Takeaway
Affirming the antecedent in a valid conditional syllogism yields the consequent, based on the principle that the relatum necessitates the relation.

?

The Syllogism
beginner

Denying the Consequent (Modus Tollens) in Conditional Syllogism
States the second valid mood of conditional syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply modus tollens correctly in a conditional syllogism using Islamic theological examples.

Core Idea
If the consequent is false, the antecedent must be false.

The second valid mood of conditional syllogism is denying the consequent (al-tali). Al-Bajuri illustrates: if it is not originated, then it is not a body. This mood is equally essential in kalam and fiqh reasoning, allowing us to refute antecedents by showing that their consequences are false.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional major premise
The same conditional: 'If something is a body, then it is originated.'
2
Locate the exceptive premise
Here the exceptive premise denies the consequent: 'but it is not originated' (لَكِنَّهُ لَيْسَ بِحَادِثٍ).
The term 'raf' (رفع) means denial.
3
Derive the conclusion
Denying the consequent yields the denial of the antecedent: 'so it is not a body' (فَهُوَ لَيْسَ بِجِرْمٍ).
This is modus tollens.
4
Justify with the principle
Denying the relation (lazim) necessitates denying the relatum (malzum). Since being originated is a necessary consequence of being a body, if the consequence is false, the body cannot exist.
Explicit in commentary.
5
Apply to a new example
Given 'If an act is intoxicating, it is forbidden (haram).' If it is not forbidden, can we conclude it is not intoxicating? Yes, by modus tollens.
Example from fiqh.

Source Text

فَإِنْ يَكُ الشَّرْطِيُّ ذَا انْصَالِ: أَنْتَجَ وَضْعُ ذَاكَ وَضْعَ النَّالِـي (103) وَرَفْعُ تَالٍ رَفْعَ أَوَّلِ، وَلَا يَلْزَمُ فِي عَكْسِهِمَا لِمَا انْجَلَى. القِيَاسُ الِاسْتِثْنَائِيُّ يَنْقَسِمُ: إِلَى اتِّصَالِيٌّ، وَهُوَ: مَا كَانَتْ شَرْطِيَّتُهُ مُتَّصِلَةٌ. وَإِلَى انْفِصَالِيٌّ، وَهُوَ: مَا كَانَتْ شَرْطِيَّتُهُ مُنْفَصِلَةٌ. وَذَكَرَ أَنَّهُ يُشْتَرَطُ فِي الاِتِّصَالِي أَنْ تَكُونَ الإِسْتِثْنَائِيَّةُ فِيهِ حَكَمَتْ بِوَضْعِ مُقَدَّم الشَّرْطِيَّةِ؛ أَيْ: إِثْبَاتِهِ، أَوْ بِرَفْعِ تَالِيهَا؛ أَيْ: نَفْيِهِ، وَيُنْتِجُ فِي الأَوَّلِ عَيْنَ التَّالِي؛ لِأَنَّ وُجُودَ المَلْزُومِ يُوجِبُ وُجُودَ اللَّازِمِ، وَفِي الثَّانِي نَقِيضَ المُقَدَّمِ؛ لِأَنَّ نَفْيَ اللَّازِمِ يُوجِبُ نَفْيَ المَلْزُومِ، وَلَا يُنْتِجُ الاِسْتِثْنَائِيُّ شَيْئاً بِرَفْعِ المُقَدَّمِ، وَلَا بِوَضْعِ التَّالِي؛ لِجَوَازِ كَوْنِ اللَّازِم أَعَمَّ مِنَ المَلْزُوم، فَيَلْزَمُ مِنْ ثُبُوتِ الأَخَصَّ ثُبُوتُ الأَعَمّ، وَمِنْ نَفْيِ الأَعَمِّ نَفْيُ الأَخَصَّ. وَلَا يَلْزَمُ مِنْ نَفْيِ الأَخَصُ نَفْيُ الأَعَمِّ وَلَا مِنْ ثُبُوتِ الأَعَمِّ ثُبُوتُ الأَخَصّ. مَثَلاً: إِذَا قُلْنَا كُلَّمَا كَانَ المَوْجُودُ جِرْماً كَانَ حَادِثاً، لَكِنَّهُ جِرْمٌ، أَنْتَجَ: فَهُوَ حَادِثٌ، أَوْ قُلْنَا: لَكِنَّهُ لَيْسَ بِحَادِثٍ أَنْتَجَ: فَهُوَ لَيْسَ بِجِرْمٍ، وَلَا إِنْتَاجَ فِي عَكْسِهِمَا لِمَا مَرَّ. قَالَ النَّاظِمُ: (فَإِنْ يَكُ الشَّرْطِيُّ) قوله: (وَلَا إِنْتَاجَ فِي عَكْسِهِمَا) بيانُهُ: أَنَّكَ إِذا رفعت الأوّل بالاستثنائيَّة؛ كأن تقول فى المثال المذكور: لكنه ليس بجرم، فلا يلزم كونه ليس حادثاً ولا: «كونه حادثاً؛ لأنَّ المقدَّم قد يكون أخصَّ مِنَ التَّالي كما في المثال؛ إذ كونه جرماً أخص من كونه حادثاً، ولا يلزم رفع الأخص رفع الأعم ولا ثبوته. وكذا وضع التّالي لا يستلزم ثبوت المقدَّم.
If the conditional has connection: affirming that (the antecedent) produces the affirmation of the consequent, and denying the consequent produces the denial of the first, and the converse is not entailed, as is clear. The conditional syllogism is divided into: the connected, which is its conditional is connected; and the disjoined, which is its conditional is disjoined. And he mentioned that in the connected it is required that the exceptive clause rules by affirming the antecedent of the conditional, i.e., establishing it, or by denying its consequent, i.e., negating it. And it produces in the first the exact consequent, because the existence of the relatum requires the existence of the relation; and in the second the opposite of the antecedent, because denying the relation requires denying the relatum. And the conditional syllogism does not produce anything by denying the antecedent nor by affirming the consequent, because it is possible that the relation is more general than the relatum. Thus from the affirmation of the more specific follows the affirmation of the more general, and from the denial of the more general follows the denial of the more specific. And it does not follow from the denial of the more specific the denial of the more general, nor from the affirmation of the more general the affirmation of the more specific. For example: if we say 'Whenever an existing thing is a body, it is originated; but it is a body', it produces: 'so it is originated'; or we say 'but it is not originated', it produces: 'so it is not a body'. And there is no production in their converse for what has passed. The author said: (If the conditional is with connection) His statement: (And no production in their converse) its explanation: that if you deny the first by the exceptive, such as you say in the mentioned example: 'But it is not a body', then it does not entail that it is not originated nor that it is originated, because the antecedent might be more specific than the consequent as in the example; since being a body is more specific than being originated. And the denial of the specific does not entail the denial of the general nor its affirmation. Likewise, affirming the consequent does not entail the affirmation of the antecedent.
Source pages p. 700

Commentary
This passage presents the core rules of the conditional syllogism (al-istithnā'ī) with its division into connected (muttasil) and disjoined (munfasil). The focus is on the connected conditional. The matn verses state the two valid moods: affirming the antecedent produces the affirmation of the consequent, and denying the consequent produces the denial of the antecedent. The reverse moods are invalid. The commentary explains the underlying reason: the relation (lāzim) may be more general than the relatum (malzūm). For example, 'being a body' is more specific than 'being originated'—everything that is a body is originated, but not everything originated is a body. Therefore, if we affirm the antecedent (being a body), we can affirm the consequent (originated). If we deny the consequent (not originated), we can deny the antecedent (not a body). However, if we deny the antecedent (not a body), we cannot conclude anything about being originated, because the thing might be originated through another cause. Similarly, affirming the consequent (originated) does not allow us to affirm the antecedent (body), because the thing could be originated without being a body (e.g., an accident). The passage thus establishes the necessary conditions for a valid conditional syllogism and warns against the common fallacy of affirming the consequent or denying the antecedent.

رَفْع
المَلْزُوم
اللَّازِم

C) The exact consequent
D) No conclusion

Key Takeaway
Denying the consequent (modus tollens) is a valid mood because the falsity of the relation necessitates the falsity of the relatum.

The Syllogism
intermediate
5 min
Invalid Moods: Denying Antecedent and Affirming Consequent
States the invalid moods and their reason (generality relation).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and explain why denying the antecedent or affirming the consequent in a conditional syllogism does not produce a valid conclusion.

Core Idea
Denying the antecedent or affirming the consequent never yields certainty because the consequent may be more general than the antecedent.

Al-Bajuri and al-Bannani explicitly state that the two reverse moods—denying the antecedent and affirming the consequent—do not produce any necessary conclusion. The reason is the generality relation: the consequent (lazim) may be more general than the antecedent (malzum). This lesson explores these fallacies using the same kalam example to show why they fail.

Guided Path
1
Identify the invalid exceptive premise
There are two: (1) denying the antecedent: 'but it is not a body', (2) affirming the consequent: 'but it is originated'. The passage says these do not produce anything.
2
Recall the generality principle
The relation (lazim) may be more general than the relatum (malzum). Here, 'originated' is more general than 'body'. The specific implies the general, but the general does not imply the specific.
Passage: 'لِجَوَازِ كَوْنِ اللَّازِم أَعَمَّ مِنَ المَلْزُوم'.
3
Test denying the antecedent
If we deny 'body', we cannot conclude about 'originated' because there are non-bodies that are originated (e.g., accidents). Conclusion: No necessary inference.
Passage: 'وَلَا إِنْتَاجَ فِي عَكْسِهِمَا'.
4
Test affirming the consequent
If we affirm 'originated', we cannot conclude 'body' because many originated things are not bodies. So again no necessary inference.
Commentary: 'وَكَذَا وَضْعُ التَّالِي لا يَسْتَلْزِمُ ثُبُوتَ الْمُقَدَّمِ'.
5
Recognize the logical asymmetry
The specific (body) entails the general (originated), and denying the general entails denying the specific. But the reverse is not true: denying the specific does not deny the general; affirming the general does not affirm the specific.
This is the core insight of the passage.

Conditional Syllogism: Productive Modes and Fallacies
Author defines conditional syllogism, divides into mutassil and munfasil, and states productive modes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct and validate a conjunctive conditional syllogism and identify the fallacies of denying the antecedent and affirming the consequent.

Core Idea
In a conjunctive conditional syllogism, only affirming the antecedent or denying the consequent yields a valid conclusion.

This passage introduces the conditional syllogism, explaining its two types and the two valid modes of inference. It also warns against the fallacies of denying the antecedent and affirming the consequent, using the example of 'body' and 'originated' to illustrate the logical relationship of specific and general. Understanding these rules is essential for sound reasoning in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional premise
Find the conditional proposition (shartiyya) that sets up the relationship between antecedent (muqaddam) and consequent (tali). In the example: 'Whenever a body exists, it is originated.' Here the antecedent is 'a body exists' and the consequent is 'it is originated'.
2
Determine the type of conditional
Check if the conditional is conjunctive (mutassil) or disjunctive (munfasil). The passage focuses on conjunctive, where the antecedent necessarily leads to the consequent.
Disjunctive conditionals involve exclusive alternatives and are treated separately.
3
Examine the exceptive premise
The exceptive premise (istithna'iyya) either affirms the antecedent or negates the consequent. Only these two actions produce valid conclusions. For example: 'But it is a body' affirms the antecedent; 'But it is not originated' negates the consequent.
4
Test for validity
Apply the rule: If the exceptive affirms the antecedent, the conclusion is the consequent. If it negates the consequent, the conclusion is the contradictory of the antecedent. If it negates the antecedent or affirms the consequent, no conclusion follows. The example illustrates: negating the antecedent ('not a body') or affirming the consequent ('originated') does not entail the other.
The relationship of specific (akhas) and general (a'am) explains why these are fallacies.

فصل في الاستثنائي . بِعَيْنِهِ مَذْكُورٌ فِي القِيَاسِ ؛ لِأَنَّهُ تَالِي الشَّرْطِيَّةِ ، لَكِنَّهَا حَالَ كَوْنِهَا تَالِياً جُزْءٌ قَضِيَّةٍ، وَحَالَ كَوْنِهَا نَتِيجَةٌ قَضِيَّةٌ تَامَّةٌ كَمَا مَرَّ . وَالمُشْتَمِلُ عَلَى نَقِيضِهَا بِالفِعْلِ ؛ كَمَا لَوِ اسْتَثْنَيْنَا نَقِيضَ التَّالِي فَقُلْنَا : لَكِنْ لَيْسَ النَّهَارُ بِمَوْجُودٍ ، فَإِنَّهُ يُنْتِجُ : فَلَيْسَتِ الشَّمْسُ بِطَالِعَةٍ وَهِيَ غَيْرُ مَذْكُورَةٍ فِي القِيَاسِ، لَكِنْ ذَكَرَ فِيهِ نَقِيضُهَا وَهُوَ : كَانَتِ الشَّمْسُ طَالِعَةٌ، وَالمُرَادُ أَيْضاً بِالنَّقِيضِ مَادَّتُهُ وَصُورَتُهُ فَقَط ، وَإِلَّا فَمُقَدَّمُ الشَّرْطِيَّةِ لَيْسَ بِقَضِيَّةٍ وَالنَّتيجَةُ قَضِيَّةٌ ، وَلَا تَنَاقُضَ بَيْنَ قَضِيَّةٍ وَمَا لَيْسَ بِقَضِيَّةٍ. قَوْلُهُ : (۱۰۲) فَإِنْ يَكُ الشَّرْطِيُّ ذَا انْصَالِ: أَنْتَجَ وَضْعُ ذَاكَ وَضْعَ النَّالِي (۱۰۳) وَرَفْعُ تَالٍ رَفْعَ أَوَّلِ، وَلَا يَلْزَمُ فِي عَكْسِهِمَا لِمَا انْجَلَى القِيَاسُ الِاسْتِثْنَائِيُّ يَنْقَسِمُ : (۱) - إِلَى اتِّصَالِيٌّ ، وَهُوَ : مَا كَانَتْ شَرْطِيَّتُهُ مُتَّصِلَةٌ . (٢) (۲) - وَإِلَى انْفِصَالِيٌّ ، وَهُوَ : مَا كَانَتْ شَرْطِيَّتُهُ مُنْفَصِلَةٌ . وَذَكَرَ أَنَّهُ يُشْتَرَطُ فِي الاِتِّصَالِي أَنْ تَكُونَ الإِسْتِثْنَائِيَّةُ فِيهِ حَكَمَتْ بِوَضْعِ مُقَدَّم الشَّرْطِيَّةِ ؛ أَيْ : إِثْبَاتِهِ، أَوْ بِرَفْعِ تَالِيهَا ؛ أَيْ: نَفْيِهِ، وَيُنْتِجُ فِي الأَوَّلِ عَيْنَ التَّالِي ؛ لِأَنَّ وُجُودَ المَلْزُومِ يُوجِبُ وُجُودَ اللَّازِمِ ، وَفِي الثَّانِي نَقِيضَ المُقَدَّمِ ؛ لِأَنَّ نَفْيَ اللَّازِمِ يُوجِبُ نَفْيَ المَلْزُومِ، وَلَا يُنْتِجُ الاِسْتِثْنَائِيُّ شَيْئاً بِرَفْعِ المُقَدَّمِ ، وَلَا بِوَضْعِ التَّالِي ؛ لِجَوَازِ كَوْنِ اللَّازِم أَعَمَّ مِنَ المَلْزُوم، فَيَلْزَمُ مِنْ ثُبُوتِ الأَخَصَّ ثُبُوتُ الأَعَمّ، وَمِنْ نَفْيِ الأَعَمِّ نَفْيُ الأَخَصَّ . وَلَا يَلْزَمُ مِنْ نَفْيِ الأَخَصِّ نَفْيُ الأَعَمِّ وَلَا مِنْ ثُبُوتِ الأَعَمِّ ثُبُوتُ الأَخَصّ. مَثَلاً : إِذَا قُلْنَا كُلَّمَا كَانَ المَوْجُودُ جِرْماً كَانَ حَادِثاً ، لَكِنَّهُ جِرْمٌ، أَنْتَجَ : فَهُوَ حَادِثٌ، أَوْ قُلْنَا : لَكِنَّهُ لَيْسَ بِحَادِثٍ أَنْتَجَ : فَهُوَ لَيْسَ بِجِرْمٍ، وَلَا إِنْتَاجَ فِي عَكْسِهِمَا لِمَا مَرَّ. قصارة. قال النَّاظم : (فَإِنْ يَكُ الشَّرْطِيُّ) قوله : (وَلَا إِنْتَاجَ فِي عَكْسِهِمَا) بيانُهُ: أَنَّكَ إِذا رفعت الأوّل بالاستثنائيَّة؛ كأن تقول فى المثال المذكور : الكنَّه ليس بجرم، فلا يلزم كونه ليس حادثاً ولا : كونه حادثاً؛ لأنَّ المقدَّم قد يكون أخصَّ مِنَ التَّالي كما في المثال؛ إذ كونه جرماً أخص من كونه ولا يلزم رفع الأخص رفع الأعم ولا ثبوته وكذا وضع التّالي لا يستلزم ثبوت المقدَّم حادثاً.
Chapter on the conditional syllogism. The very same [result] is mentioned in the syllogism because it is the consequent of the conditional, but while it is a consequent it is part of a proposition, and while it is a conclusion it is a complete proposition—as has passed. And that which includes its actual contradictory is like when we except the contradictory of the consequent and say: 'But the day does not exist,' for it produces: 'Then the sun is not rising,' even though [the consequent] is not mentioned in the syllogism; but its contradictory is mentioned, namely: 'The sun was rising.' And what is meant by 'contradictory' is only its matter and form. Otherwise, the antecedent of the conditional is not a proposition and the conclusion is a proposition, and there is no contradiction between a proposition and what is not a proposition. His saying: 'If the conditional is disjunctive, then positing that posits the consequent, and removing the consequent removes the antecedent, and the reverse does not follow—since it is clear.' The conditional syllogism is divided: (1) into conjunctive, whose conditional is conjunctive; (2) and into disjunctive, whose conditional is disjunctive. And it mentioned that in the conjunctive it is required that the exceptive [premise] rule by positing the antecedent; i.e., affirming it, or by removing its consequent; i.e., negating it. And it produces in the first the very consequent, because the existence of the necessitating condition necessitates the existence of the necessary consequence, and in the second the contradictory of the antecedent, because the negation of the necessary consequence necessitates the negation of the necessitating condition. The conditional [syllogism] does not produce anything by removing the antecedent nor by positing the consequent, because it is possible that the necessary consequence is more general than the necessitating condition; so from establishing the more specific the more general follows, and from denying the more general the more specific is denied, but it does not follow from denying the more specific the denial of the more general, nor from establishing the more general the establishment of the more specific. For example: if we say 'Whenever a body exists, it is originated,' and then 'but it is a body,' it produces: 'so it is originated'; or we say 'but it is not originated,' it produces: 'so it is not a body.' And there is no production in their reverses because of what has passed. Commentary: He said: 'If the conditional...' His saying: 'and no production in their reverses' its explanation: that if you remove the first by the exceptive, as when you say in the mentioned example: 'but it is not a body,' then it does not follow that it is not originated, nor that it is originated, because the antecedent may be more specific than the consequent as in the example, since being a body is more specific than being originated, and removing the more specific does not necessitate removing the more general nor its establishment; similarly, positing the consequent does not necessitate establishing the antecedent.
Source pages p. 701

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition of the conditional syllogism (qiyas istithna'i). It begins by clarifying that the result (natija) in a conditional syllogism is the very consequent of the conditional proposition when the exceptive premise affirms the antecedent, or the contradictory of the antecedent when the exceptive premise negates the consequent. The author distinguishes between two types: conjunctive (mutassil) where the conditional is a true conjunction, and disjunctive (munfasil) where it is a true disjunction. For the conjunctive type, the only productive modes are: (1) affirming the antecedent yields the consequent (because the existence of the necessitating condition (malzum) necessitates the necessary consequence (lazim)), and (2) denying the consequent yields the contradictory of the antecedent (because denial of the necessary consequence necessitates denial of the necessitating condition). The passage explicitly warns against the fallacy of affirming the consequent or denying the antecedent, using the example of 'body' and 'originated': 'body' is more specific (akhas) than 'originated' (a'am), so from 'not body' we cannot conclude anything about 'originated' (neither affirmation nor negation), and from 'originated' we cannot conclude 'body'. The commentary further illustrates this with the same example, showing that the antecedent and consequent have a relationship of akhas and a'am, which prevents valid inference in the reverse directions.

الاتصالي
الانفصالي
المقدم
التالي
الملزوم
اللازم

Checkpoint
In the example given in the passage, 'Whenever a body exists, it is originated,' which of the following exceptive premises yields a valid conclusion?
A) But it is not a body.
B) But it is originated.
C) But it is not originated.
D) But it is not a body and it is originated.

Key Takeaway
In a conjunctive conditional syllogism, the only valid modes are affirming the antecedent (to get the consequent) and denying the consequent (to get the contradictory of the antecedent).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Task 1: Consider the conditional premise from fiqh: 'If a beverage is intoxicating, it is forbidden (haram).' Construct a valid conditional syllogism by adding an exceptive premise, and check its validity. Then construct an invalid one by denying the antecedent, and explain why it fails.
?
Task 2: Identify the fallacy in the following argument: 'If a person is a believer (mu'min), they enter paradise. This person enters paradise; therefore they are a believer.' Use the concept of akhas (specific) and a'am (general) to explain why.

The Syllogism
intermediate

Three Conditions for Conditional Syllogism Production
Author states three conditions for conditional syllogism production

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and apply the three conditions for a valid conditional syllogism (affirmative, universal, necessary/incompatible).

Core Idea
A conditional syllogism requires an affirmative, universal, and necessary conditional to be productive.

This passage explains the three essential conditions that any conditional syllogism—whether conjunctive or disjunctive—must satisfy to yield a valid conclusion. Without these conditions, the inference fails, leaving the argument barren. Mastering these conditions equips you to evaluate the soundness of conditional arguments in Islamic legal and theological reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three conditions
List the three conditions given by the author: (1) the conditional must be affirmative, (2) it must be universal, (3) it must not be accidental (i.e., necessary for conjunctive, incompatible for disjunctive).
The third condition is derived from Hamdun's verse: 'absence of accident, negation, and partiality'.
2
Understand why a negative conditional is barren
Examine the example: 'Not at all: if Zayd is knowledgeable then Amr is knowledgeable.' This negative conditional yields no conclusion whether you affirm or deny any part, because the connection is denied entirely.
Similarly for disjunctive negative conditionals.
3
Analyze the universality condition
Note the refinement: a particular conditional can still produce if the exceptive premise is universal over all relevant times and conditions, as in the Friday prayer example.
The required universality is in the exceptive, not necessarily the conditional's form.
4
Distinguish accidental from necessary
Recognize that an accidental conditional (ittifaqiyya) fails because the connection is merely coincidental; only necessary (luzumiyya) for conjunctive or incompatible (inadiyya) for disjunctive conditionals are valid.
This ensures the logical implication is robust.
5
Apply the conditions to a new example
Take a conditional syllogism from usul al-fiqh: 'If the contract is valid, then it transfers ownership; this contract is valid; therefore it transfers ownership.' Check each condition: is the conditional affirmative? universal? necessary? If all hold, the syllogism is valid.
Practice with different examples to internalize the conditions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَاعْلَمْ أَنَّهُ يُشْتَرَطُ لِإِنْتاجِ الِاسْتِثْنَائِيِّ مُطْلَقاً ثَلَاثَةُ شُرُوطٍ : (١) - أَحَدُهَا : أَنْ تَكُونَ الشَّرْطِيَّةُ مُوجَبَةٌ ، فَالصَّالِبَةُ عَقِيمَةٌ ؛ لِأَنَّهُ إِذَا سُلِبَ الاتِّصَالُ وَالانْفِصَالُ، لَمْ يَلْزَمْ مِنْ وُجُودِ أَحَدِهِمَا أَوْ نَقِيضِهِ ثُبُوتُ الْآخَرِ أَوْ عَدَمُهُ . (۲) - الثَّانِي : أَنْ تَكُونَ كُلِّيَّةٌ ، وهذه العبارة قد تحتاج إلى تفصيل. قال النَّاظم: ... قوله : (وَاعْلَمْ أَنَّهُ يُشْتَرَطُ لِإِنْتاجِ الِاسْتِثْنَائِي مُطْلَقاً) أي : متصلاً أو منفصلاً، وأشار به إلى التنكيت على النّاظم، فإنَّ ظاهره الإطلاق، وليس كذلك. قوله : (ثَلَاثَةُ شُرُوط) أشار لها شيخنا سيدي حمدون في «خريدته» بقوله : وَشَرْطُهُ عَدَمُ الأَنفَاقِ وَالسَّلْبُ وَالبَعْضُ بِالاِتِّفَاقِ :أي: أن لا تكون الشَّرطيَّة المستعملة فيه اتفاقيَّة، بل لزوميَّة إن كانت متّصلة، وعناديَّة إن كانت منفصلة، وعدمُ السَّلب؛ أي: أن لا تكون الشَّرطيَّة سالبة، وعدم البعض حال كونه مع اتفاق الشَّرطيَّة فيه ؛ أي: أن لا تتفق الشَّرطيَّة، والاستثناء على البعض أي : الجزئية . قوله : (فَالصَّالِبَةُ عَقِيمَةٌ مثالها : ليس البتّة إذا كان زيد عالماً كان عمرو عالماً»، ثمَّ إذا قلنا في الاستثنائية : لكن زيد عالم ينتج : عمرو عالم ولا : ليس بعالم، وكذا إذا قلنا : «لكن عمرو ليس بعالم لم ينتج : زيد ليس بعالم ولا هو عالم». وإذا قلنا في المنفصلة : ليس البتّة إما أن يكون زيد عالماً ، وإما أن يكون عمرو فلا إنتاج في رفع أحدِ الطَّرفين ولا وضعه ؛ سواء قدرناها حقيقيةً، أو مانعة جمع، أو مانعة خلوّ . قوله : (الثَّانِي: أَنْ تَكُونَ كُلِّيَّةٌ الصَّوابُ : أنَّ الشَّرطيَّة قد تكون جزئية، وتنتج إذا كانتِ الاستثنائية كلّيّة متحققة في جميع الأوضاع التي لا تنافي وضع المقدَّم كما ذكر ذلك الشَّيخ قدورة أولاً ، خلافاً لما عند هذا الشارح. ومثال ذلك : ما إذا قلت في الجزئية: «قد يكون إذا جئتني وقت صلاة الجمعة أكرمتك، جئتني في جميع أوقات يوم الجمعة، فهذا العموم ينطبق على زمن الاتصال، فالمراد بكلية الاستثنائية عموم الأزمنة والأحوال، حتّى يشمل ذلك العموم خصوص زمن الاتصال أو حاله.
And know that for the production of the conditional syllogism absolutely, three conditions are required: (1) One of them: that the conditional be affirmative, for the negative is barren; because if the connection and disjunction are negated, it does not follow from the existence of one of them or its contradictory the establishment of the other or its non-existence. (2) The second: that it be universal. ... His saying: 'And know that it is required for the production of the conditional absolutely' means: conjunctive or disjunctive, and he pointed by that to a criticism of the versifier, for his apparent absolute statement, but it is not so. His saying: 'three conditions' – our master Sidi Hamdun indicated them in his 'Kharida' by his verse: 'Its condition is the absence of accident, negation, and partiality by agreement.' That is: that the conditional used in it must not be accidental, but necessary if it is conjunctive, and incompatible if it is disjunctive; and the absence of negation, i.e., the conditional must not be negative; and the absence of partiality when it is with agreement of the conditional, i.e., that the conditional and the exception must not be upon a part, i.e., particular. His saying: 'The negative is barren' – its example: 'Not at all: if Zayd is knowledgeable then 'Amr is knowledgeable.' Then if we say in the exceptive: 'But Zayd is knowledgeable,' it produces: ''Amr is knowledgeable'? No, it does not produce. Similarly if we say: 'But 'Amr is not knowledgeable,' it does not produce: 'Zayd is not knowledgeable' nor 'he is knowledgeable.' And if we say in the disjunctive: 'Not at all: either Zayd is knowledgeable or 'Amr is knowledgeable,' then there is no production in raising one of the two sides nor in positing it, whether we consider it as real, or exclusive of both, or exclusive of void. His saying: 'The second: that it be universal' – the correct view is that the conditional may be particular and yet produce if the exceptive is universal realized in all the conditions that do not contradict the positing of the antecedent, as Sheikh Qadoura mentioned first, contrary to what this commentator holds. An example of that: if you say in the particular: 'It may be that if you come to me at Friday prayer time, I will honor you,' and you came to me in all times of Friday, then this universality applies to the time of connection, so what is meant by universality of the exceptive is universality of times and conditions, such that that universality includes the specific time of connection or its state.
Source pages p. 702

Commentary
This passage lays out three essential conditions for any conditional syllogism (whether conjunctive or disjunctive) to produce a valid conclusion. The first condition is that the conditional proposition be affirmative (mujaba); a negative conditional (saliba) is 'barren' because if the connection or disjunction is denied, no inference can be drawn from the existence or non-existence of one part to the other. The example given: 'Not at all: if Zayd is knowledgeable then 'Amr is knowledgeable' – a negative conditional – yields no conclusion whether we affirm or deny any part. Similarly for disjunctive: 'Not at all: either Zayd is knowledgeable or 'Amr is knowledgeable' yields nothing. The second condition is that the conditional be universal (kulliyya). However, the commentator notes a refinement: a particular conditional can produce if the exceptive premise is universal in the sense of covering all times and conditions that do not contradict the antecedent's position, as exemplified by the Friday prayer invitation. The third condition (derived from Hamdun's verse) is that the conditional must be 'non-accidental' (ghayr ittifaqiyya), meaning the connection must be necessary (luzumiyya) for conjunctive or incompatible (inad) for disjunctive; it must not be negative; and it must not be partial (i.e., the conditional and exception must not be particular). The commentator also notes that the versifier's (al-Sullam) apparent absolute statement is criticized because the conditions apply generally to both types.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT a condition for a productive conditional syllogism according to this passage?
A) The conditional must be affirmative.
B) The conditional must be universal.
C) The conditional must be accidental (ittifaqiyya).
D) The conditional must be necessary (luzumiyya) or incompatible (inadiyya).

Key Takeaway
A conditional syllogism is only valid if the conditional premise is affirmative, universal, and necessary (or incompatible).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the conditional syllogism: 'If the prayer is valid, it includes sujud; this prayer includes sujud; therefore it is valid.' Check each condition: is the conditional affirmative? universal? necessary? Does it meet the conditions? If not, how would you fix it?
?
Consider a particular conditional: 'Sometimes if a person sleeps, his wudu is nullified.' Is this suitable for a conditional syllogism? Explain using the universality condition discussed in the passage.

First Condition for Conditional Syllogism: Affirmative Premise
Author states the first condition for a productive conditional syllogism: the conditional must be affirmative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a conditional syllogism has a productive affirmative premise and explain why a negative conditional is sterile.

Core Idea
A negative conditional yields no conclusion.

In this passage, the author explains the first condition for a conditional syllogism to produce a conclusion: the conditional premise must be affirmative. We will examine why negative conditionals are sterile, using examples from the text, and see how this condition applies to both connected and disjunctive conditionals.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition
Find the first condition stated: the conditional must be affirmative (موجبة). Note that the author says the negative (سالبة) is sterile (عقيمة).
2
Understand the reason
The author explains that when connection or disconnection is negated, affirming one part does not entail anything about the other. Why? Because the consequent may be more general than the antecedent.
For example, 'If it is a human, it is an animal' – affirming the consequent does not entail the antecedent.
3
Analyze the examples
The text gives an example with Zayd and Amr: 'It is absolutely not the case that if Zayd is knowledgeable then Amr is knowledgeable.' Then if we affirm Zayd is knowledgeable, we cannot conclude Amr is knowledgeable or not. Similarly for disjunctive.
4
Apply to disjunctive conditionals
The same condition holds for disjunctive conditionals: if the disjunction is negated, no conclusion follows from affirming or negating one part.
The text explicitly extends the condition to both types.

وَاعْلَمْ أَنَّهُ يُشْتَرَطُ لِإِنْتاجِ الِاسْتِثْنَائِيِّ مُطْلَقاً ثَلَاثَةٌ شُرُوطٍ : (١) - أَحَدُهَا : أَنْ تَكُونَ الشَّرْطِيَّةُ مُوجَبَةٌ ، فَالصَّالِبَةُ عَقِيمَةٌ ; لِأَنَّهُ إِذَا سُلِبَ الاتِّصَالُ وَالانْفِصَالُ، لَمْ يَلْزَمْ مِنْ وُجُودِ أَحَدِهِمَا أَوْ نَقِيضِهِ ثُبُوتُ الْآخَرِ أَوْ عَدَمُهُ . قصارة. ولا رفعه; لأنَّ التَّالي قد يكون أعمَّ مِنَ المقدَّم كما في المثال فلا يستلزم وضعه ـ أي : إثباته - ثبوت المقدَّم ولا رفعه، ونظير هذا المثال: «كلما كان الشَّيء إنساناً، كان حيواناً». قوله : (وَاعْلَمْ أَنَّهُ يُشْتَرَطُ لِإِنْتاجِ الِاسْتِثْنَائِي مُطْلَقاً) أي : متصلاً أو منفصلاً، وأشار به إلى التنكيت على النّاظم، فإنَّ ظاهره الإطلاق، وليس كذلك. قوله : (ثَلَاثَةُ شُرُوط) أشار لها شيخنا سيدي حمدون في «خريدته» بقوله : وَشَرْطُهُ عَدَمُ الأَنفَاقِ وَالسَّلْبُ وَالبَعْضُ بِالاِتِّفَاقِ :أي: أن لا تكون الشَّرطيَّة المستعملة فيه اتفاقيَّة، بل لزوميَّة إن كانت متّصلة، وعناديَّة إن كانت منفصلة، وعدمُ السَّلب؛ أي: أن لا تكون الشَّرطيَّة سالبة، وعدم البعض حال كونه مع اتفاق الشَّرطيَّة فيه ؛ أي: أن لا تتفق الشَّرطيَّة، والاستثناء على البعض أي : الجزئية . قوله : (فَالصَّالِبَةُ عَقِيمَةٌ مثالها : ليس البتّة إذا كان زيد عالماً كان عمرو عالماً»، ثمَّ إذا قلنا في الاستثنائية : لكن زيد عالم ينتج : عمرو عالم ولا : ليس بعالم، وكذا إذا قلنا : «لكن عمرو ليس بعالم لم ينتج : زيد ليس بعالم ولا هو عالم». وإذا قلنا في المنفصلة : ليس البتّة إما أن يكون زيد عالماً ، وإما أن يكون عمرو فلا إنتاج في رفع أحدِ الطَّرفين ولا وضعه ؛ سواء قدرناها حقيقيةً، أو مانعة جمع، أو مانعة خلوّ .
And know that for the productivity of the conditional syllogism absolutely [connected or disjunctive], three conditions are required: (1) The first: that the conditional be affirmative, for the negative is sterile; because when connection or disconnection is negated, it does not follow from the existence of one of them or its opposite the affirmation or negation of the other. And [the reason is that] the consequent may be more general than the antecedent as in the example, so its affirmation does not entail the affirmation of the antecedent nor its negation. An analogous example: 'Whenever something is a human, it is an animal.' His statement: 'And know that it is required for the productivity of the conditional syllogism absolutely' means connected or disjunctive, and he alluded to a subtle point against the poet, for his apparent statement is absolute but it is not so. His statement: 'Three conditions' — our teacher Sayyid Hamdun indicated them in his 'Kharida' by his saying: 'Its condition is the absence of the accidental, the negative, and the particular in conjunction with the accidental.' That is: the conditional used in it must not be accidental, but rather necessary if connected, and contrary if disjunctive; and absence of the negative: that the conditional not be negative; and absence of the particular while it is in conjunction with the accidental: that the conditional not be particular. His statement: 'So the negative is sterile; its example: 'It is absolutely not the case that if Zayd is knowledgeable then Amr is knowledgeable.' Then if we say in the conditional syllogism: 'But Zayd is knowledgeable' it yields: 'Amr is knowledgeable' and not: 'He is not knowledgeable.' Similarly if we say: 'But Amr is not knowledgeable' it does not yield: 'Zayd is not knowledgeable' nor 'He is knowledgeable.' And if we say in the disjunctive: 'It is absolutely not the case that either Zayd is knowledgeable or Amr is knowledgeable' then there is no production whether we raise one of the two sides or affirm it, whether we consider it true exclusive disjunction, or inclusive disjunction (man'at al-jam'), or exhaustive disjunction (man'at al-khuluww).
Source pages p. 702

Commentary
This passage explains the first condition for a productive conditional syllogism, whether connected or disjunctive. The condition is that the conditional premise must be affirmative (positive), not negative. The reason given is that when the connection or disconnection is negated, no conclusion can be drawn from affirming or negating one part to the other. The commentator then extends the discussion by noting that the consequent might be more general than the antecedent, as in the example 'Whenever something is a human, it is an animal' — affirming the antecedent (human) entails the consequent (animal), but affirming the consequent does not entail the antecedent, nor does negating the antecedent entail negating the consequent. The commentary cites a didactic poem (Kharida) summarizing the three conditions. Examples are given for both connected and disjunctive conditionals, illustrating that negative conditionals are sterile.

Key Terms
استثنائي
شرطية موجبة
اتفاقية

Why is a negative conditional considered 'sterile' (عقيمة) in conditional syllogism?
A) Because it contradicts the form of the syllogism
B) Because when connection or disconnection is negated, affirming or negating one part does not entail anything about the other part
C) Because the predicate must be affirmative for the syllogism to be valid
D) Because negative conditionals are always false

Key Takeaway
The first condition for a productive conditional syllogism is that the conditional premise must be affirmative; a negative conditional allows no inference.

Why the Conditional Must Be Universal
Author explains that the conditional must be universal, with exceptions for particular conditionals under certain conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a conditional proposition in a syllogism is universal or particular, and determine if the syllogism is productive.

Core Idea
A particular conditional cannot guarantee a conclusion because the antecedent may be more general than the consequent.

This lesson explores the second condition for a productive conditional syllogism: the conditional premise must be universal. We will see why a particular conditional fails and how to evaluate conditionals in Islamic logical arguments, using examples from the text.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional as universal or particular
Look at the conditional proposition. If it uses a universal quantifier like 'whenever' (كلما) or 'if' (إن) without restriction, it is universal. If it uses 'sometimes' (قد يكون) or is indefinite, it is particular.
A universal conditional applies to all instances, times, or states; a particular applies to some.
2
Check the exception (istithna') for coverage
Even if the conditional is particular, if the exception is universal and covers all times/states of the connection, the syllogism may still be productive. Otherwise, the particular conditional is sterile.
The exception must be universal in the sense of covering the same times/states as the conditional's intended scope.
3
Test with an example
Example from the text: 'It may be that if you come to me at Friday prayer time, I will honor you. You came at all times on Friday.' The exception is universal in covering the Friday time, so the syllogism is productive.
If the exception were not universal, e.g., 'You came on some Friday', the conclusion may not follow.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (الثَّانِي: أَنْ تَكُونَ كُلِّيَّةٌ الصَّوابُ : أنَّ الشَّرطيَّة قد تكون جزئية، وتنتج إذا كانتِ الاستثنائية كلّيّة متحققة في جميع الأوضاع التي لا تنافي وضع المقدَّم كما ذكر ذلك الشَّيخ قدورة أولاً ، خلافاً لما عند هذا الشارح. ومثال ذلك : ما إذا قلت في الجزئية: «قد يكون إذا جئتني وقت صلاة الجمعة أكرمتك، جئتني في جميع أوقات يوم الجمعة، فهذا العموم ينطبق على زمن الاتصال، فالمراد بكلية الاستثنائية عموم الأزمنة والأحوال، حتّى يشمل ذلك العموم خصوص زمن الاتصال أو حاله، لكنك [؟] فَالجُزْنِيَّةُ عَقِيمَةٌ; لِجَوَازِ أَنْ يَكُونَ اللُّزُومُ أَوِ العِنَادُ فِي بَعْضِ الأَحْوَالِ، وَالِاسْتِثْنَاءُ فِي وَضْعِ آخَرَ، وَمِثْلُ الكُلِّيَّةِ المَخصُوصَةِ وَإِنْ كَانَتْ غَيْرَ كُلِّيَّة. قوله : (فَالجُزْنِيَّةُ عَقِيمَةٌ) أي: لجواز كون المقدَّم في الجزئية أعم مِنَ الثَّالي; كما في مثالنا المتقدم، فلا يلزم من وضع المقدَّم الأعم وضع التّالي الأخص، ولا من رفع التّالي الأخص رفع المقدَّم الأعم، ومثل الجزئية في ذلك المهملة. قوله : (وَمِثْلُ الكُلِّيَّةِ المَخْصُوصَةِ) إطلاق في محلّ التَّقييد ; لأنَّهم اشترطوا في المخصوصة أن يكون حال اللُّزوم أوِ العِناد هو حال الاستثناء أو بعضه، ولا يكفي كون المخصوصة كليَّة اتحاد حال اللُّزوم أوِ العِناد، فلا تنتج إلا إذا كان زمن الاتصال والوضع واحداً; نحو: «كلما، أو : إن جاءني زيد يوم الجمعة أكرمته ، لكنَّه جاءني يوم الجمعة، فيلزم الإكرام، وأما إذا لم تقيد الاستثنائية بزمن المتّصلة فلا يلزم ; نحو: «كلما ، أو : إن جاءني زيد الجمعة أكرمته، لكنه جاءني فلا يقتضي الإكرام ; لاحتمال أن يكون المجيء في غيره مِنَ الأيام. ولكون الشَّرطيّة المخصوصة في حكم الكلِّيَّة، اقتصر الجلُّ على اشتراط الكلية. قوله : (وَإِنْ كَانَتْ غَيْرَ كُلِّيَّةٍ) أي: جزئيّة أو مهملة ; كقولنا : إن قدم زيد الآن، فهو مكرم، لكنَّه قدم الآن ينتج فهو مكرم.
His statement: 'The second: that it be universal.' [The correct view is that the conditional may be particular, and it produces if the exceptional [premise] is universal, realized in all situations that do not contradict the position of the antecedent, as mentioned by Sheikh Qadura first, contrary to what this commentator holds. An example of that is when you say in the particular: 'It may be that if you come to me at the time of Friday prayer, I will honor you; you came to me at all times of Friday.' So this generality applies to the time of connection, so what is meant by the universality of the exceptional premise is the generality of times and states, such that that generality includes the specific time of connection or its state, but you [?] So the particular is sterile, because it is possible that the necessary or contrary connection is in some states and the exception is in another state. And like the universal that is specified [al-kulliyya al-makhsusa] even if it is not universal. His statement: 'So the particular is sterile' means because it is possible that the antecedent in the particular is more general than the consequent, as in our previous example, so posting the more general antecedent does not entail posting the more specific consequent, nor does raising the more specific consequent entail raising the more general antecedent. And the particular is like the absolute [al-muhmala]. His statement: 'And like the universal that is specified' is an unrestricted statement in a place that requires restriction; because they stipulated in the specified [conditional] that the state of the necessary or contrary connection be the state of the exception or part of it, and it is not sufficient that the specified [conditional] be universal in the unity of the state of the necessary or contrary connection, so it does not produce unless the time of connection and the position are one; for example: 'Whenever or if Zayd comes to me on Friday, I will honor him, but he came to me on Friday; then honor is necessary.' But if the exceptional premise is not restricted to the time of the connected [conditional], then it does not follow; for example: 'Whenever or if Zayd comes to me on Friday, I will honor him, but he came to me' does not imply honor, because it is possible that the coming was on another day. And because the specified conditional is in the rule of the universal, the majority limited [the condition] to universality. His statement: 'And even if it is not universal' means particular or absolute; for example: 'If Zayd comes now, he is honored; but he came now; then it produces that he is honored.'
Source pages p. 702

Commentary
This passage addresses the second condition for the productivity of the conditional syllogism: the conditional must be universal. The commentator clarifies that a particular conditional can still be productive if the exception (istithna') is universal in a way that covers all times and states of the connection. However, the general rule is that a particular conditional is sterile because the antecedent may be more general than the consequent, so affirming the antecedent does not entail the consequent, and negating the consequent does not entail negating the antecedent. The discussion also covers the special case of the 'specified universal' (al-kulliyya al-makhsusa), which is a conditional that is universal but restricted to a specific time or condition. For such conditionals, productivity requires that the time of the connection (or state of the necessary/contrary relationship) coincides with the time of the exception. Examples are given from everyday situations, such as honoring a guest on Friday or coming at Friday prayer time. The commentator notes that the majority of scholars restrict the condition to mere universality, considering the specified universal as effectively universal.

Key Terms
كلية
جزئية
كلية مخصوصة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is a particular conditional usually sterile in conditional syllogism?
A) Because the conditional must be affirmative
B) Because the antecedent may be more general than the consequent, so affirming the antecedent does not entail the consequent
C) Because the exception must be particular as well
D) Because particular conditionals are always false

Key Takeaway
A conditional syllogism requires a universal conditional; a particular conditional is generally sterile because the antecedent may be broader than the consequent.

The Specified Universal: When a Restricted Conditional Works
Author explains that the conditional must be universal, with exceptions for particular conditionals under certain conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a specified universal conditional (kulliyya makhsusa) and test whether a conditional syllogism with such a premise is productive.

Core Idea
A specified universal conditional, though not absolutely universal, is productive if the exception falls within its restriction.

This lesson examines the specified universal (al-kulliyya al-makhsusa), a conditional that is universal but restricted to a specific time or condition. We will learn when such a conditional can produce a valid conclusion and when it cannot, using examples from the text.

Guided Path
1
Identify if the conditional is a specified universal
Look for a conditional that is universal in form but explicitly tied to a specific time or condition, e.g., 'If Zayd comes on Friday, I will honor him.' It applies universally to all cases that satisfy the condition of coming on Friday.
It is not absolutely universal because it only applies to Friday cases, not all days.
2
Check the exception's restriction
The exception (istithna') must be limited to the same time or condition as the conditional. For example, 'But Zayd came on Friday' matches the condition. If the exception is not restricted, e.g., 'But Zayd came' (without Friday), the syllogism fails.
The commentator says the state of the exception must coincide with the state of the necessary/contrary connection.
3
Test with examples from the text
Example: 'Whenever/If Zayd comes to me on Friday, I will honor him. He came on Friday. Therefore I honor him.' This is valid. Counterexample: 'Whenever Zayd comes on Friday, I will honor him. He came (without specifying Friday).' This does not imply honor because he may have come on another day.
The time of connection (Friday) must be the same as the time of the exception.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (الثَّانِي: أَنْ تَكُونَ كُلِّيَّةٌ الصَّوابُ : أنَّ الشَّرطيَّة قد تكون جزئية، وتنتج إذا كانتِ الاستثنائية كلّيّة متحققة في جميع الأوضاع التي لا تنافي وضع المقدَّم كما ذكر ذلك الشَّيخ قدورة أولاً ، خلافاً لما عند هذا الشارح. ومثال ذلك : ما إذا قلت في الجزئية: «قد يكون إذا جئتني وقت صلاة الجمعة أكرمتك، جئتني في جميع أوقات يوم الجمعة، فهذا العموم ينطبق على زمن الاتصال، فالمراد بكلية الاستثنائية عموم الأزمنة والأحوال، حتّى يشمل ذلك العموم خصوص زمن الاتصال أو حاله، لكنك [؟] فَالجُزْنِيَّةُ عَقِيمَةٌ; لِجَوَازِ أَنْ يَكُونَ اللُّزُومُ أَوِ العِنَادُ فِي بَعْضِ الأَحْوَالِ، وَالِاسْتِثْنَاءُ فِي وَضْعِ آخَرَ، وَمِثْلُ الكُلِّيَّةِ المَخصُوصَةِ وَإِنْ كَانَتْ غَيْرَ كُلِّيَّة. قوله : (فَالجُزْنِيَّةُ عَقِيمَةٌ) أي: لجواز كون المقدَّم في الجزئية أعم مِنَ الثَّالي; كما في مثالنا المتقدم، فلا يلزم من وضع المقدَّم الأعم وضع التّالي الأخص، ولا من رفع التّالي الأخص رفع المقدَّم الأعم، ومثل الجزئية في ذلك المهملة. قوله : (وَمِثْلُ الكُلِّيَّةِ المَخْصُوصَةِ) إطلاق في محلّ التَّقييد ; لأنَّهم اشترطوا في المخصوصة أن يكون حال اللُّزوم أوِ العِناد هو حال الاستثناء أو بعضه، ولا يكفي كون المخصوصة كليَّة اتحاد حال اللُّزوم أوِ العِناد، فلا تنتج إلا إذا كان زمن الاتصال والوضع واحداً; نحو: «كلما، أو : إن جاءني زيد يوم الجمعة أكرمته ، لكنَّه جاءني يوم الجمعة، فيلزم الإكرام، وأما إذا لم تقيد الاستثنائية بزمن المتّصلة فلا يلزم ; نحو: «كلما ، أو : إن جاءني زيد الجمعة أكرمته، لكنه جاءني فلا يقتضي الإكرام ; لاحتمال أن يكون المجيء في غيره مِنَ الأيام. ولكون الشَّرطيّة المخصوصة في حكم الكلِّيَّة، اقتصر الجلُّ على اشتراط الكلية. قوله : (وَإِنْ كَانَتْ غَيْرَ كُلِّيَّةٍ) أي: جزئيّة أو مهملة ; كقولنا : إن قدم زيد الآن، فهو مكرم، لكنَّه قدم الآن ينتج فهو مكرم.

Checkpoint
When is a specified universal conditional (kulliyya makhsusa) productive in a conditional syllogism?
A) When the conditional is universal in form

Key Takeaway
A specified universal conditional works like a universal only if the exception is restricted to the same time/condition as the conditional; otherwise, the syllogism fails.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Analyze this specified universal conditional: 'If a traveler intends to stay 15 days in a place, he is a resident. He intends to stay 15 days in Mecca. Is the syllogism productive? Explain why.
?
Create a valid specified universal conditional syllogism using a fiqh example, and then create an invalid one by failing to restrict the exception.

Third Condition: Conditional Must Be Necessary or Contrary
Author states the condition and explains why accidental conditionals are sterile.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a conditional is necessary (luzumiyya), contrary ('inadiyya), or accidental (ittifaqiyya), and explain why accidental conditionals are sterile in syllogisms.

Core Idea
An accidental conditional cannot produce a conclusion because its truth already depends on the truth of its parts.

In conditional syllogisms, the conditional premise must satisfy certain conditions to yield a valid conclusion. The third condition is that the conditional must be either necessary (luzumiyya) in connected conditionals or contrary ('inadiyya) in disjunctive conditionals. Accidental conditionals (ittifaqiyya), where the connection is coincidental, are sterile because they lead to circular reasoning. This lesson unpacks these distinctions and the logical flaw behind accidental conditionals.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish connected and disjunctive conditionals
Recall that conditional syllogisms use either a connected conditional (muttaṣila: 'If A then B') or a disjunctive conditional (munfaṣila: 'Either A or B'). The third condition applies differently to each.
Connected conditionals require a necessary connection (luzumiyya); disjunctive conditionals require a contrary opposition ('inadiyya).
2
Define necessary (luzumiyya) conditional
A necessary connected conditional is one where the antecedent necessarily implies the consequent, e.g., 'If something is a human, it is an animal.' The connection is essential, not coincidental.
Without this necessity, the conditional cannot guarantee the inference.
3
Define contrary ('inadiyya) disjunctive
A contrary disjunctive is one where the disjuncts are truly opposed: they cannot both be true and cannot both be false (exclusive disjunction). Example: 'Either this number is even or it is odd.'
This ensures that negating one disjunct yields the other.
4
Identify accidental (ittifaqiyya) conditionals
An accidental conditional is one where the connection is coincidental, e.g., 'Whenever the sun rises, man is a speaker.' The truth of the conditional depends on the independent truth of its parts, not on a real connection.
Accidental conditionals can be true but are not useful for inference.
5
Analyze the circularity argument
The commentator explains: To know that an accidental conditional is true, you already need to know the truth values of its parts. If you then try to use the conditional to prove one part, you assume what you want to prove, resulting in circular reasoning (dawr).
Thus, accidental conditionals are 'sterile' (ʿaqīm) – they cannot produce new knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۳) - الثَّالِثُ : أَنْ تَكُونَ لُزُومِيَّةٌ فِي المُتَّصِلَةِ، أَوْ عِنَادِيَّةٌ فِي المُنْفَصِلَةِ، فَالاتِّفَاقِيَّةُ فِيهِمَا عَقِيمَةٌ . قوله : (فَالاتِّفَاقِيَّةُ فِيهِمَا عَقِيمَةٌ) فلا إنتاج إذا قلت : كلَّما كانتِ الشَّمس طالعة، كان الإنسان ناطقاً ; لأنَّ رفع الاتفاقيَّة كذب، ووضع مقدَّمها لا فائدة له; لأنَّ نتيجته معلومة من نفس الاتفاقية; لأنَّ العِلم بصدق الاتفاقيَّة موقوفٌ على العلم بصدق جزأيها ، فلو استفدنا العلم بصدق أحد جزأيها من صدقها، لزم الدور.
(3) The third: that it be necessary in the connected [conditional], or contrary in the disjunctive; for the accidental [conditional] in both is sterile. His statement: 'So the accidental in both is sterile' — there is no production if you say: 'Whenever the sun is rising, man is speaking' because raising [the antecedent] of the accidental is false, and posting its antecedent is of no benefit because its conclusion is known from the accidental [conditional] itself; for knowledge of the truth of the accidental [conditional] depends on knowledge of the truth of its two parts, so if we were to derive knowledge of the truth of one of its parts from its truth, circularity would occur.
Source pages p. 703

Commentary
This passage explains the third condition for a productive conditional syllogism: the conditional must be necessary (luzumiyya) in the connected form (e.g., if-then with a real causal relation) and contrary ('inadiyya) in the disjunctive form (e.g., either-or with a true opposition). An accidental conditional (ittifaqiyya) is one where the connection is purely coincidental, like 'Whenever the sun rises, man is a speaker.' Such a conditional cannot produce a conclusion because the truth of the conditional itself depends on knowing the truth values of its parts independently. If we try to use the conditional to conclude one part, we would be assuming what we need to prove, leading to circular reasoning (dawr). The commentator gives the example and explains the logical flaw: the knowledge of the truth of the accidental conditional requires prior knowledge of the truth of its parts, so it cannot serve as a premise to prove either part.

Key Terms
لزومية
عنادية
اتفاقية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because it is always negative
B) Because it is particular
C) Because knowledge of its truth depends on knowledge of its parts, so using it to conclude a part would be circular
D) Because it is not a proper conditional

Key Takeaway
The third condition ensures that conditional syllogisms produce new knowledge; accidental conditionals are sterile because they involve circular reasoning.

Why Particular Conditionals Are Sterile
Author explains why particular conditionals are sterile in conditional syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a particular conditional proposition renders a conditional syllogism sterile and explain why using the example given in the text.

Core Idea
A particular conditional lacks necessity across all cases, so it cannot produce a valid conclusion.

In conditional syllogisms, not every proposition leads to a valid conclusion. The author explains that particular (juz'iyya) conditionals are sterile because the implication may hold only in some states while the exception refers to different states. This lesson examines the reasoning behind this sterility and how it applies to both conjunctive and disjunctive conditionals.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of conditional
Determine if the conditional proposition is particular (juz'iyya) or indefinite (muhmala) – look for words like 'sometimes' or lack of universal quantifier.
2
Check the relationship between antecedent and consequent
See if the antecedent is more general than the consequent, as in the example 'if a thing is an animal, it is a human'.
3
Test the syllogism
Try to derive a conclusion by positing the antecedent or denying the consequent. Does a necessary conclusion follow?
4
Observe the failure
Notice that no necessary conclusion follows because the implication is not universal. The syllogism is sterile ('aqeema).
This failure is called sterile because the conclusion does not follow necessarily.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَالجُزْنِيَّةُ عَقِيمَةٌ؛ لِجَوَازِ أَنْ يَكُونَ اللُّزُومُ أَوِ العِنَادُ فِي بَعْضِ الأَحْوَالِ، وَالِاسْتِثْنَاءُ فِي وَضْعِ آخَرَ، وَمِثْلُ الكُلِّيَّةِ المَخصُوصَةِ وَإِنْ كَانَتْ غَيْرَ كُلِّيَّة.
(۳) - الثَّالِثُ : أَنْ تَكُونَ لُزُومِيَّةٌ فِي المُتَّصِلَةِ، أَوْ عِنَادِيَّةٌ فِي المُنْفَصِلَةِ، فَالاتفاقِيَّةُ فِيهِمَا عَقِيمَةٌ .
وليس المراد بها ما فهمه العقباني كما عند قدورة؛ لأنَّه لا يكفي في تحقيق الإنتاج دوام الاستثناء؛ إذ لو قلت : «قد يكون إذا كان الشَّيء حيواناً كان إنساناً، لم ينتج وضع المقدم، ولا رفع التالي شيئاً؛ إذ لا يلزم من وضع الحيوان وضع الإنسان، ولا من رفع الإنسان رفع الحيوان، مع أنَّ الحيوانية دائمة؛ لِمَا ثبت له .
قوله : (فَالجُزْنِيَّةُ عَقِيمَةٌ) أي: لجواز كون المقدَّم في الجزئية أعم مِنَ الثَّالي؛ كما في مثالنا المتقدم، فلا يلزم من وضع المقدَّم الأعم وضع التّالي الأخص، ولا من رفع التّالي الأخص رفع المقدَّم الأعم، ومثل الجزئية في ذلك المهملة.
قوله : (وَمِثْلُ الكُلِّيَّةِ المَخْصُوصَةِ) إطلاق في محلّ التَّقييد ؛ لأنَّهم اشترطوا في المخصوصة أن يكون حال اللُّزوم أوِ العِناد هو حال الاستثناء أو بعضه، ولا يكفي كون المخصوصة كليَّة اتحاد حال اللُّزوم أوِ العِناد، فلا تنتج إلا إذا كان زمن الاتصال والوضع واحداً؛ نحو: «كلما، أو : إن جاءني زيد يوم الجمعة أكرمته ، لكنَّه جاءني يوم الجمعة، فيلزم الإكرام، وأما إذا لم تقيد الاستثنائية بزمن المتّصلة فلا يلزم ؛ نحو: «كلما ، أو : إن جاءني زيد الجمعة أكرمته، لكنه جاءني فلا يقتضي الإكرام ؛ لاحتمال أن يكون المجيء في غيره مِنَ الأيام.
ولكون الشَّرطيّة المخصوصة في حكم الكلِّيَّة، اقتصر الجلُّ على اشتراط الكلية. قوله : (وَإِنْ كَانَتْ غَيْرَ كُلِّيَّةٍ) أي: جزئيّة أو مهملة ؛ كقولنا : إن قدم زيد الآن، فهو مكرم، لكنَّه قدم الآن ينتج فهو مكرم : قوله : (فَا لا تُفَاقِيَّةُ فِيهِمَا عَقِيمَةٌ) فلا إنتاج إذا قلت : كلَّما كانتِ الشَّمس طالعة، كان الإنسان ناطقاً ؛ لأنَّ رفع الاتفاقيَّة كذب، ووضع مقدَّمها لا فائدة له؛ لأنَّ نتيجته معلومة من نفس الاتفاقية؛ لأنَّ العِلم بصدق الاتفاقيَّة موقوفٌ على العلم بصدق جزأيها ، فلو استفدنا العلم بصدق أحد جزأيها من صدقها، لزم الدور.
The particular (conditional) is sterile, because it is possible that the implication or opposition is in some states, while the exception is in another state. Likewise the restricted universal even if it is not universal. (3) The third (condition): that it be implicative in the conjunctive, or oppositional in the disjunctive; the accidental in both is sterile. And what is meant by it is not what al-'Uqbani understood, as according to Qaddura, because persistence of the exception is not sufficient for achieving production. For if you say: 'Sometimes if a thing is an animal it is a human', positing the antecedent or denying the consequent produces nothing, because from positing the animal it does not follow to posit the human, nor from denying the human to deny the animal, even though animality is constant, due to what is established for it. His saying: (the particular is sterile) means: because it is possible that the antecedent in the particular is more general than the consequent, as in our preceding example, so from positing the more general antecedent the more specific consequent does not follow, nor from denying the more specific consequent does the more general antecedent follow; and the indefinite is like the particular in that. His saying: (and like the restricted universal) is an unqualified statement in a place of restriction, because they stipulated in the restricted (universal) that the state of implication or opposition be the state of the exception or part of it, and it is not sufficient that the restricted universal be universal in the unity of the state of implication or opposition. So it does not produce unless the time of connection and the position are one; e.g., 'Whenever, or if Zaid came to me on Friday, I honor him, but he came on Friday' then honor follows. But if the exceptive is not restricted by the time of the conjunction, it does not follow; e.g., 'Whenever, or if Zaid came to me on Friday, I honor him, but he came' then honor is not necessitated, because of the possibility that the coming was on a day other than it. And because the restricted conditional is in the ruling of the universal, the majority restricted themselves to stipulating universality. His saying: (even if it is not universal) means particular or indefinite, as in our saying: 'If Zaid came now, he is honored, but he came now' produces 'he is honored'. His saying: (the accidental in both is sterile) so there is no production if you say: 'Whenever the sun is up, man is rational', because denying the accidental is false, and positing its antecedent has no benefit, because its conclusion is known from the accidental itself, because knowledge of the truth of the accidental is dependent on knowledge of the truth of its two parts, so if we were to gain knowledge of the truth of one of its parts from its truth, circularity would result.
Source pages p. 703

Commentary
This passage explains the conditions required for a conditional syllogism (qiyas istithna'i) to produce a valid conclusion. It begins by stating that a particular conditional proposition (جُزْنِيَّة) is sterile (عَقِيمَة), meaning it cannot produce a conclusion. The reason is that implication or opposition may hold only in some states, while the exception (the premise that posits or denies) may refer to a different state. For example, if we say 'Sometimes if something is an animal, it is a human' the antecedent 'animal' is more general than 'human', so positing the antecedent does not necessitate the consequent, and denying the consequent does not necessitate denying the antecedent. The same applies to indefinite propositions (مهملة). Next, the author discusses the third condition: the conditional must be implicative (لزومية) in conjunctive conditionals or oppositional (عِنَادية) in disjunctive ones. Accidental conditionals (اتفاقية) are sterile in both types. An accidental conditional is one where the connection between antecedent and consequent is merely coincidental, like 'Whenever the sun is up, man is rational'. The explanation emphasizes that it is not enough for the exception to be persistent (as al-'Uqbani thought); rather, the implication must be necessary. The text then clarifies the case of the 'restricted universal' (المخصوصة), which is a universal conditional but with a temporal or other restriction. For it to produce, the state of the implication must coincide with the state of the exception. For example, 'If Zaid comes to me on Friday, I honor him' combined with 'he came on Friday' produces 'I honor him'. But if the exception is unrestricted ('he came'), no conclusion follows. Finally, the accidental conditional is sterile because affirming the antecedent gives no new information (its truth is already known from the parts), and denying the consequent would be false. The explanation warns against circular reasoning (دور).

Key Terms
جُزْنِيَّة
عَقِيمَة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is a particular conditional (جُزْنِيَّة) sterile in a conditional syllogism?
A) Because the antecedent is always more specific than the consequent.
B) Because the implication or opposition may hold only in some states, while the exception refers to another state.
C) Because the disjunctive is always true.
D) Because the accidental is always false.

Key Takeaway
Particular conditionals are sterile because the implication is not universal, so no necessary conclusion follows.
