Why Accidental Conditionals Are Sterile
Author explains why accidental conditionals are sterile in both conjunctive and disjunctive types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify an accidental conditional (ittifaqiyya) and explain why it cannot produce a valid conclusion in a conditional syllogism.

Core Idea
An accidental conditional's truth is known from its parts, making any inference from it circular or redundant.

The third condition for production in conditional syllogisms is that the conditional must be implicative (luzumiyya) in conjunctive or oppositional (inadiyya) in disjunctive. Accidental conditionals, where the connection is coincidental, are sterile because they do not provide new information. This lesson examines the reasoning behind this and the danger of circularity.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish accidental from implicative
Check if the connection between antecedent and consequent is necessary or coincidental. If the connection is fortuitous, it is accidental.
2
Test the syllogism
Try to posit the antecedent or deny the consequent. What happens?
3
Observe the problem
Denying the consequent would be false, and positing the antecedent yields no new knowledge because the truth of the conditional already requires knowledge of the truth of both parts.
4
Identify circularity
Recognize that using the conditional to conclude one part from its truth would be circular, as the truth of the conditional depends on the truth of its parts.
This is the key logical failure.

Commentary
This passage explains the conditions required for a conditional syllogism (qiyas istithna'i) to produce a valid conclusion. It begins by stating that a particular conditional proposition (جُزْنِيَّة) is sterile (عَقِيمَة), meaning it cannot produce a conclusion. The reason is that implication or opposition may hold only in some states, while the exception (the premise that posits or denies) may refer to a different state. For example, if we say 'Sometimes if something is an animal, it is a human' the antecedent 'animal' is more general than 'human', so positing the antecedent does not necessitate the consequent, and denying the consequent does not necessitate denying the antecedent. The same applies to indefinite propositions (مهملة). Next, the author discusses the third condition: the conditional must be implicative (لزومية) in conjunctive conditionals or oppositional (عِنَادية) in disjunctive ones. Accidental conditionals (اتفاقية) are sterile in both types. An accidental conditional is one where the connection between antecedent and consequent is merely coincidental, like 'Whenever the sun is up, man is rational'. The explanation emphasizes that it is not enough for the exception to be persistent (as al-'Uqbani thought); rather, the implication must be necessary. The text then clarifies the case of the 'restricted universal' (المخصوصة), which is a universal conditional but with a temporal or other restriction. For it to produce, the state of the implication must coincide with the state of the exception. For example, 'If Zaid comes to me on Friday, I honor him' combined with 'he came on Friday' produces 'I honor him'. But if the exception is unrestricted ('he came'), no conclusion follows. Finally, the accidental conditional is sterile because affirming the antecedent gives no new information (its truth is already known from the parts), and denying the consequent would be false. The explanation warns against circular reasoning (دور).

Key Terms
اتِّفَاقِيَّة
لُزُومِيَّة
عِنَادِيَّة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is an accidental conditional (اتفاقية) sterile?
A) Because it is always false.
B) Because the connection is necessary.
C) Because its truth depends on both parts, so inferring a part from it is circular.
D) Because the antecedent is more general.

Key Takeaway
Accidental conditionals are sterile because inferring a part from the whole conditional leads to circular reasoning.

Consider the conditional 'If a person is a scholar, they are knowledgeable.' Is this accidental or implicative? Explain whether it can be used in a conditional syllogism to conclude that Ahmad, who is a scholar, is knowledgeable. What problem arises?

Restricted Universal Conditionals and Temporal Coincidence
Author explains how restricted universals can produce only when the exception matches the restriction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a restricted universal conditional and determine whether the exception matches the restriction for a valid conclusion.

Core Idea
A universal conditional with a temporal restriction produces only if the exception coincides with that restriction.

Some conditional propositions are universal but restricted to a specific time or condition, such as 'If Zaid comes on Friday, I honor him.' The author explains that for such a conditional to produce a conclusion, the exception (istithna') must be under the same restriction. If the exception is unrestricted, the conclusion does not follow. This lesson examines the condition of temporal coincidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the restriction
Find the condition in the conditional that limits its scope (e.g., time: 'on Friday').
2
Check the exception
Does the exception (the premise that posits or denies) include that same restriction?
3
Test with unrestricted exception
If the exception omitted the restriction, does the conclusion still hold? The author says it does not.
4
Observe the necessity
The conclusion only follows when the restriction matches. If it does not, the syllogism is sterile.
This is why the majority stipulate universality without restriction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَالجُزْنِيَّةُ عَقِيمَةٌ؛ لِجَوَازِ أَنْ يَكُونَ اللُّزُومُ أَوِ العِنَادُ فِي بَعْضِ الأَحْوَالِ، وَالِاسْتِثْنَاءُ فِي وَضْعِ آخَرَ، وَمِثْلُ الكُلِّيَّةِ المَخصُوصَةِ وَإِنْ كَانَتْ غَيْرَ كُلِّيَّة.
(۳) - الثَّالِثُ : أَنْ تَكُونَ لُزُومِيَّةٌ فِي المُتَّصِلَةِ، أَوْ عِنَادِيَّةٌ فِي المُنْفَصِلَةِ، فَالاتفاقِيَّةُ فِيهِمَا عَقِيمَةٌ .
وليس المراد بها ما فهمه العقباني كما عند قدورة؛ لأنَّه لا يكفي في تحقيق الإنتاج دوام الاستثناء؛ إذ لو قلت : «قد يكون إذا كان الشَّيء حيواناً كان إنساناً، لم ينتج وضع المقدم، ولا رفع التالي شيئاً؛ إذ لا يلزم من وضع الحيوان وضع الإنسان، ولا من رفع الإنسان رفع الحيوان، مع أنَّ الحيوانية دائمة؛ لِمَا ثبت له .
قوله : (فَالجُزْنِيَّةُ عَقِيمَةٌ) أي: لجواز كون المقدَّم في الجزئية أعم مِنَ الثَّالي؛ كما في مثالنا المتقدم، فلا يلزم من وضع المقدَّم الأعم وضع التّالي الأخص، ولا من رفع التّالي الأخص رفع المقدَّم الأعم، ومثل الجزئية في ذلك المهملة.
قوله : (وَمِثْلُ الكُلِّيَّةِ المَخْصُوصَةِ) إطلاق في محلّ التَّقييد ؛ لأنَّهم اشترطوا في المخصوصة أن يكون حال اللُّزوم أوِ العِناد هو حال الاستثناء أو بعضه، ولا يكفي كون المخصوصة كليَّة اتحاد حال اللُّزوم أوِ العِناد، فلا تنتج إلا إذا كان زمن الاتصال والوضع واحداً؛ نحو: «كلما، أو : إن جاءني زيد يوم الجمعة أكرمته ، لكنَّه جاءني يوم الجمعة، فيلزم الإكرام، وأما إذا لم تقيد الاستثنائية بزمن المتّصلة فلا يلزم ؛ نحو: «كلما ، أو : إن جاءني زيد الجمعة أكرمته، لكنه جاءني فلا يقتضي الإكرام ؛ لاحتمال أن يكون المجيء في غيره مِنَ الأيام.
ولكون الشَّرطيّة المخصوصة في حكم الكلِّيَّة، اقتصر الجلُّ على اشتراط الكلية. قوله : (وَإِنْ كَانَتْ غَيْرَ كُلِّيَّةٍ) أي: جزئيّة أو مهملة ؛ كقولنا : إن قدم زيد الآن، فهو مكرم، لكنَّه قدم الآن ينتج فهو مكرم : قوله : (فَا لا تُفَاقِيَّةُ فِيهِمَا عَقِيمَةٌ) فلا إنتاج إذا قلت : كلَّما كانتِ الشَّمس طالعة، كان الإنسان ناطقاً ؛ لأنَّ رفع الاتفاقيَّة كذب، ووضع مقدَّمها لا فائدة له؛ لأنَّ نتيجته معلومة من نفس الاتفاقية؛ لأنَّ العِلم بصدق الاتفاقيَّة موقوفٌ على العلم بصدق جزأيها ، فلو استفدنا العلم بصدق أحد جزأيها من صدقها، لزم الدور.
The particular (conditional) is sterile, because it is possible that the implication or opposition is in some states, while the exception is in another state. Likewise the restricted universal even if it is not universal. (3) The third (condition): that it be implicative in the conjunctive, or oppositional in the disjunctive; the accidental in both is sterile. And what is meant by it is not what al-'Uqbani understood, as according to Qaddura, because persistence of the exception is not sufficient for achieving production. For if you say: 'Sometimes if a thing is an animal it is a human', positing the antecedent or denying the consequent produces nothing, because from positing the animal it does not follow to posit the human, nor from denying the human to deny the animal, even though animality is constant, due to what is established for it. His saying: (the particular is sterile) means: because it is possible that the antecedent in the particular is more general than the consequent, as in our preceding example, so from positing the more general antecedent the more specific consequent does not follow, nor from denying the more specific consequent does the more general antecedent follow; and the indefinite is like the particular in that. His saying: (and like the restricted universal) is an unqualified statement in a place of restriction, because they stipulated in the restricted (universal) that the state of implication or opposition be the state of the exception or part of it, and it is not sufficient that the restricted universal be universal in the unity of the state of implication or opposition. So it does not produce unless the time of connection and the position are one; e.g., 'Whenever, or if Zaid came to me on Friday, I honor him, but he came on Friday' then honor follows. But if the exceptive is not restricted by the time of the conjunction, it does not follow; e.g., 'Whenever, or if Zaid came to me on Friday, I honor him, but he came' then honor is not necessitated, because of the possibility that the coming was on a day other than it. And because the restricted conditional is in the ruling of the universal, the majority restricted themselves to stipulating universality. His saying: (even if it is not universal) means particular or indefinite, as in our saying: 'If Zaid came now, he is honored, but he came now' produces 'he is honored'. His saying: (the accidental in both is sterile) so there is no production if you say: 'Whenever the sun is up, man is rational', because denying the accidental is false, and positing its antecedent has no benefit, because its conclusion is known from the accidental itself, because knowledge of the truth of the accidental is dependent on knowledge of the truth of its two parts, so if we were to gain knowledge of the truth of one of its parts from its truth, circularity would result.

The Syllogism

Disjunctive Syllogism: Types and Inference Rules
Author explains matn on disjunctive syllogism and its three types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the three types of disjunctive syllogism (haqiqi, man' al-jam', man' al-khuluw) and determine which inferences are valid for each.

Core Idea
The validity of a disjunctive syllogism depends on whether the disjunction excludes combination, emptiness, or both.

The disjunctive syllogism is a fundamental tool in Islamic logic, used extensively in kalam and fiqh debates. Its rules are more varied than the conditional syllogism because the relationship between the two disjuncts can be of three distinct types. Understanding these types is essential for constructing valid arguments and avoiding fallacies, especially when deriving rulings from authoritative texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of disjunction
Examine the conditional premise. Is it a genuine exclusive disjunction (haqiqi), a 'man' al-jam'' (exclusive but not exhaustive), or a 'man' al-khuluw' (exhaustive but not exclusive)? The passage defines these: haqiqi is composed of a thing and the equivalent of its negation, man' al-jam' prevents both being true but allows both false, and man' al-khuluw prevents both false but allows both true.
The terms are standard; the passage clarifies the haqiqi requires equivalence to the negation, not the negation itself.
2
Determine the inference rules for each type
For haqiqi: positing one disjunct yields negation of the other, and vice versa (both directions valid). For man' al-jam': only positing one yields negation of the other (one direction valid). For man' al-khuluw: only negating one yields positing the other (the opposite direction valid). The matn lines 104-106 summarize this.
Memorize the rules: haqiqi = both ways; man' al-jam' = A→¬B only; man' al-khuluw = ¬A→B only.
3
Apply the rules to a practical fiqh example
Consider the disjunction 'This act is either mubah or haram' (assuming it is exclusive but not exhaustive, so man' al-jam'). If we know it is mubah, we can validly infer it is not haram (A→¬B). However, if we know it is not mubah, we cannot infer it is haram because it could be wajib, mandub, or makruh. Thus, only one direction is valid.
This demonstrates how the type affects legal reasoning.
4
Check the special condition for genuine disjunction (haqiqi)
The passage emphasizes that a haqiqi disjunction must be composed of a thing and the equivalent of its negation, not the thing and its strict negation. For example, 'It is either even or odd' is productive because 'odd' is equivalent to the negation of 'even' in the context of integers. But 'It is either a human or not a human' is contradictory and sterile because the truth of one side automatically determines the other with no new information.
This subtlety prevents using trivial tautologies in arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَنَقَلَ ابْنُ عَرَفَةَ عَنِ الفَارَابِيِّ : أَنَّ الشَّرْطِيَّةَ فِي هَذَا القِيَاسِ مُتَّصِلَةٌ أَوْ مُنْفَصِلَةٌ الكُبْرَى، وَأَنَّ الاِسْتِثْنَائِيَّةَ هِيَ الصُّغْرَى، عَلَى عَكْسِ مَا عُهِدَ فِي الاِقْتِرَانِيِّ .
قَوْلُهُ :
(١٠٤) وَإِنْ يَكُنْ مُنْفَصِلاً: فَوَضْعُ ذَا يُنْتِجُ رَفْعَ ذَاكَ، وَالعَكْسُ كَذَا (١٠٥) وَذَاكَ فِي الأَخَ، ثُمَّ إِنْ يَكُنْ مَانِعَ جَمْعِ فَبِوَضْعِ ذَا زُكِنْ (١٠٦) رَفْعٌ لِذَاكَ دُونَ عَكْسٍ، وَإِذَا مَانِعَ رَفْعِ كَانَ فَهْوَ عَكْسُ ذَا اعْلَمْ أَنَّ الِاسْتِثْنَائِيَّ الاِنْفِصَالِيَّ إِذَا كَانَتْ شَرْطِيَّتُهُ مُنْفَصِلَةٌ حَقِيقِيَّةٌ، وَهِيَ مُرَادُهُ بـ «الأَخَص» ، اشْتُرِطَ فِيهَا مَعَ مَا مَرَّ أَنْ تَكُونَ مُرَكَّبَةٌ مِنَ الشَّيْءِ وَالمُسَاوِي لِنَقِيضِهِ، فَالمُرَكَّبَةُ مِنَ الشَّيْءِ وَنَقِيضِهِ عَقِيمَةٌ، فَإِذَا تَوَفَّرَتْ شُرُوطُهَا فَأَيُّ طَرَفٍ فِيهَا وُضِعَ
قوله : (وَنَقَلَ ابْنُ عَرَفَةَ قال شيخنا سيدي الطَّيِّب : وجهه الشَّيخ اليوسي بأنَّك لو اعتبرت الاستثنائي بالترتيب الاقتراني وجدته على هيئة الشكل الأوّل أوِ الثَّاني المركبين من متّصلة صغرى ومتصلة كبرى، ولا يختلفان إلا بالتقديم والتأخير ؛ مثلاً إذا قلنا : كلَّما كان هذا إنساناً فهو حيوان، لكنَّه إنسان وجدته عين قولك : «هذا إنسان»، «وكلَّما كان إنساناً كان حيواناً»، أو قلت : «لكنَّه ليس بحيوان وجدته عين قولك : «هذا ليس بحيوان»، وكلَّما كان إنساناً فهو حيوان»، والمراد التشبيه لا إدخال الاستثنائي في الاقتراني؛ لأنَّه قسيم له لا قسيمٌ منه . قال الناظم : (وَإِنْ يَكُنْ مُنْفَصِلاً) ارتكب النَّاظم فى هذه الأبيات غاية التّضمين، وأيضاً اعترض عليه في قوله : «فَوَضْع ذَا» بأنَّ موضوع الإشارة في اللغة تعيين المشار إليه، فهي بمنزلة وضع اليد على الرّأس، وذلك مانع من جعل مرجع الإشارة أحد الجزأين من غير تعيين، حتّى يتناول جميع الصُّور، فليتأمل.
قوله : (مَا مَرَّ) أي : مِن كونها كلَّيَّة ليست اتفاقية، ولا سلبية. قوله : (فَالمُرَكَّبَةُ مِنَ الشَّيْءِ وَنَقِيضِهِ عَقِيمَةٌ) فإن قلت : قد تقدَّم أنَّ الحقيقيَّة تتركَّب مِنَ الشَّيء ونقيضه أو المساوي لنقيضه، فكيف يذكر هنا أنَّها لا تكون إِلَّا مِنَ المساوي؟! فالجواب : أنَّه ذكر أوّلاً أنَّ الحقيقيَّة من حيث التركيب تصدق بضربين، وذكر هنا من حيث الإنتاج أنَّ أحدهما عقيم، فلا غبار عليه.
And Ibn 'Arafa narrated from al-Farabi: that the conditional in this syllogism is a conjunctive or disjunctive major premise, and that the exceptive is the minor premise, contrary to what is customary in the conjunctive (categorical) syllogism. His saying: (104) 'And if it is disjunctive, then positing this one produces the negation of that one, and the reverse likewise (105) and that is in the more specific. Then if it is man'at al-jam', then by positing this one you get (106) the negation of that one without the reverse. And when it is man'at al-khuluw, then it is the reverse of that. Know that the disjunctive exceptive syllogism, when its conditional is genuine disjunctive, and that is what he means by 'the more specific', there is stipulated in it, along with what has passed, that it be composed of the thing and the equivalent of its negation, for the one composed of the thing and its negation is sterile. So when its conditions are fulfilled, whichever disjunct is posited... His saying: (And Ibn 'Arafa narrated... Our teacher Sayyidi al-Tayyib said: al-Shaykh al-Yusi explained it that if you consider the exceptive syllogism according to the conjunctive (categorical) arrangement, you find it in the form of the first or second figure composed of a conjunctive minor and a conjunctive major, and they differ only by precedence and delay. For example, if we say: 'Whenever this is a human it is an animal, but it is a human' you find it is exactly your saying: 'This is a human' and 'Whenever it is a human it is an animal', or if you say: 'but it is not an animal' you find it is exactly your saying: 'This is not an animal' and 'Whenever it is a human it is an animal'. And the intent is analogy, not inclusion of the exceptive into the conjunctive, because it is a counterpart to it, not a part of it. The poet said: (And if it is disjunctive) the poet committed extreme conciseness in these verses. Also, objection was raised against his saying 'positing this one' because the object of reference in language is a determinate referent, like placing the hand on the head, and that prevents making the referent one of the two disjuncts without specification, so that it covers all forms. Let it be pondered. His saying: (what has passed) means: that it be universal, not accidental, and not negative. His saying: (the one composed of the thing and its negation is sterile) For if you say: It has already passed that the genuine (disjunctive) is composed of the thing and its negation or the equivalent of its negation, so how does he mention here that it is only from the equivalent? The answer: He first mentioned that the genuine, in terms of composition, applies to two types, and he mentioned here in terms of production that one of them is sterile, so there is no objection.
Source pages p. 704

Commentary
This passage discusses the disjunctive conditional syllogism (qiyas istithna'i infisali). It opens with a quotation from Ibn 'Arafa from al-Farabi: in this syllogism, the major premise is the conditional (either conjunctive or disjunctive) and the minor premise is the exceptive (which states or denies one disjunct), contrary to the categorical syllogism where the major is the universal premise. The text then presents the matn (poem) lines 104-106: In a disjunctive syllogism, if the conditional is a genuine (haqiqi) disjunction, then positing one disjunct produces the negation of the other, and vice versa (i.e., both ways are valid). This is called 'the more specific' (al-akhass) genuine disjunction. If it is man'at al-jam' (i.e., a disjunction that prevents combination but not emptiness), then positing one disjunct gives the negation of the other but not the reverse (i.e., positing A gives not-B, but from not-A nothing follows). If it is man'at al-khuluw (prevents emptiness but not combination), then the reverse holds: from not-A you get B, but from A nothing follows. The commentary explains that the genuine disjunction must be composed of a thing and the equivalent of its negation (i.e., a full exclusive disjunction), not simply a thing and its negation, because the latter (contradictories) is sterile (produces nothing new). An objection about the ambiguous pronoun in 'positing this one' is noted: the poet's use of 'this' is indeterminate. The commentator then answers a potential confusion: earlier it was said that the genuine disjunction can be composed of a thing and its negation or the equivalent; here it says only the equivalent produces. The answer is that the earlier statement was about composition (two types), while this one is about production (only one type produces).

Key Terms
مُنْفَصِل حَقِيقِي
مَانِع جَمْع
مَانِع خُلُوّ
اسْتِثْنَائِي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a genuine disjunctive syllogism (haqiqi), what is required for the syllogism to be productive?
A) The disjunction must be composed of a thing and its negation.
B) The disjunction must be composed of a thing and the equivalent of its negation.
C) The conditional must be accidental.
D) The minor premise must be the major premise.

Key Takeaway
The disjunctive syllogism has specific inference rules depending on the type of disjunction, and a genuine disjunction must be exclusive and exhaustive to be productive.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify the type of disjunction in the following statement from a fiqh debate: 'The contract is either valid or invalid.' (Assume no third option.) Determine which inferences (if any) are valid from affirming or denying one disjunct, and explain using the rules from the lesson.
?
Given the disjunction 'The act is either mubah or haram' (a man' al-jam' example from usul al-fiqh), answer: (a) If we know it is mubah, what can we conclude? (b) If we know it is not mubah, can we conclude it is haram? Why or why not? Provide the logical rule that supports your answer.

The Syllogism
intermediate
7 min
Productive vs. Barren Real Disjunctions
Conditions for a real disjunctive syllogism

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a productive real disjunction (composed of a thing and the equivalent of its negation) and a barren real disjunction (composed of a thing and its negation), and determine when a disjunctive syllogism yields a valid conclusion.

Core Idea
A real disjunction of a thing and its negation yields no new knowledge; only the equivalent of negation produces a conclusion.

In the study of disjunctive syllogisms, not all real disjunctions are productive. The classical logicians, as explained in the commentary, specify that a real disjunction must be composed of a thing and the equivalent of its negation. If it is merely a thing and its direct negation, the syllogism becomes circular and is called 'barren'. Understanding this distinction is crucial for constructing valid arguments in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of real disjunction composition
The passage states that a real disjunction can be composed of a thing and the equivalent of its negation (productive) or of a thing and its negation (barren). Note the difference: the equivalent of negation is a term that is not exactly the negation but entails it (e.g., 'not-A-but-B' instead of simply 'not-A').
2
Determine which type is productive
According to the text, only the disjunction composed of a thing and the equivalent of its negation allows for a valid conclusion. The other type, where the disjuncts are a thing and its direct negation, yields no conclusion because the conclusion would be the same as the exception, making the argument circular.
3
Test with an Islamic example
Consider: 'Either the prayer is valid or it is invalid' (thing and negation). If you affirm validity, you conclude it is not invalid, but that is trivial. Now consider: 'Either the prayer is valid or it is void due to a missing condition' (equivalent of negation). Here, affirming validity implies the negation of the voidness, which is informative.
4
Apply the condition to a syllogism
Given a disjunctive major premise, check if the disjuncts are a thing and the equivalent of its negation. If so, then positing one allows you to negate the other, and vice versa. This is the productive form. If not, the syllogism is barren.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَنَقَلَ ابْنُ عَرَفَةَ عَنِ الفَارَابِيِّ : أَنَّ الشَّرْطِيَّةَ فِي هَذَا القِيَاسِ مُتَصِلَةٌ أَوْ مُنْفَصِلَةٌ الكُبْرَى، وَأَنَّ الاِسْتِثْنَائِيَّةَ هِيَ الصُّغْرَى، عَلَى عَكْسِ مَا عُهِدَ فِي الاِقْتِرَانِيِّ . قَوْلُهُ : (١٠٤) وَإِنْ يَكُنْ مُنْفَصِلاً: فَوَضْعُ ذَا يُنْتِجُ رَفْعَ ذَاكَ، وَالعَكْسُ كَذَا (١٠٥) وَذَاكَ فِي الأَخَ، ثُمَّ إِنْ يَكُنْ مَانِعَ جَمْعِ فَبِوَضْعِ ذَا زُكِنْ (١٠٦) رَفْعٌ لِذَاكَ دُونَ عَكْسٍ، وَإِذَا مَانِعَ رَفْعِ كَانَ فَهْوَ عَكْسُ ذَا اعْلَمْ أَنَّ الِاسْتِثْنَائِيَّ الاِنْفِصَالِيَّ إِذَا كَانَتْ شَرْطِيَّتُهُ مُنْفَصِلَةٌ حَقِيقِيَّةٌ، وَهِيَ مُرَادُهُ بـ «الأَخَص» ، اشْتُرِطَ فِيهَا مَعَ مَا مَرَّ أَنْ تَكُونَ مُرَكَّبَةٌ مِنَ الشَّيْءِ وَالمُسَاوِي لِنَقِيضِهِ، فَالمُرَكَّبَةُ مِنَ الشَّيْءِ وَنَقِيضِهِ عَقِيمَةٌ، فَإِذَا تَوَفَّرَتْ شُرُوطُهَا فَأَيُّ طَرَفٍ فِيهَا وُضِعَ قوله : (وَنَقَلَ ابْنُ عَرَفَةَ قال شيخنا سيدي الطَّيِّب : وجهه الشَّيخ اليوسي بأنَّك لو اعتبرت الاستثنائي بالترتيب الاقتراني وجدته على هيئة الشكل الأوّل أوِ الثَّاني المركبين من متّصلة صغرى ومتصلة كبرى، ولا يختلفان إلا بالتقديم والتأخير؛ مثلاً إذا قلنا : كلَّما كان هذا إنساناً فهو حيوان، لكنَّه إنسان وجدته عين قولك : «هذا إنسان»، «وكلَّما كان إنساناً كان حيواناً»، أو قلت : «لكنَّه ليس بحيوان وجدته عين قولك : «هذا ليس بحيوان»، وكلَّما كان إنساناً فهو حيوان»، والمراد التشبيه لا إدخال الاستثنائي في الاقتراني؛ لأنَّه قسيم له لا قسيمٌ منه . قال الناظم : (وَإِنْ يَكُنْ مُنْفَصِلاً) ارتكب النَّاظم فى هذه الأبيات غاية التّضمين، وأيضاً اعترض عليه في قوله : «فَوَضْع ذَا» بأنَّ موضوع الإشارة في اللغة تعيين المشار إليه، فهي بمنزلة وضع اليد على الرّأس، وذلك مانع من جعل مرجع الإشارة أحد الجزأين من غير تعيين، حتّى يتناول جميع الصُّور، فليتأمل. قوله : (مَا مَرَّ) أي : مِن كونها كلَّيَّة ليست اتفاقية، ولا سلبية. قوله : (فَالمُرَكَّبَةُ مِنَ الشَّيْءِ وَنَقِيضِهِ عَقِيمَةٌ) فإن قلت : قد تقدَّم أنَّ الحقيقيَّة تتركَّب مِنَ الشَّيء ونقيضه أو المساوي لنقيضه، فكيف يذكر هنا أنَّها لا تكون إِلَّا مِنَ المساوي؟! فالجواب : أنَّه ذكر أوّلاً أنَّ الحقيقيَّة من حيث التركيب تصدق بضربين، وذكر هنا من حيث الإنتاج أنَّ أحدهما عقيم، فلا غبار عليه.
Ibn 'Arafa narrated from al-Farabi that the conditional in this syllogism, whether conjunctive or disjunctive, is the major premise, and the exceptive is the minor premise, contrary to what is customary in the categorical syllogism. His saying: (104) 'And if it is disjunctive: then positing this one produces the negation of that one, and the reverse likewise (105) and that in the other, then if it is an exclusive, then by positing this one you know that there is negation of that one without the reverse, and when it is an exhaustive then it is the reverse of this.' Know that the disjunctive exceptive syllogism, when its conditional is a real disjunction, and it is his intent by 'al-akhass', it is stipulated in it, in addition to what has passed, that it be composed of the thing and the equivalent of its negation, for composed of the thing and its negation is barren. So when its conditions are fulfilled, whichever of its two parts is posited... His saying: (and Ibn 'Arafa narrated) said our shaykh sayyidi al-Tayyib: shaykh al-Yusi explained it that if you consider the exceptive syllogism according to the categorical order, you find it in the form of the first or second figure composed of a conjunctive minor and a conjunctive major, and they only differ by precedence and delay; for example, if we say: 'Whenever this is a human it is an animal, but it is a human', you find it the same as your saying: 'This is a human' and 'Whenever it is a human it is an animal', or you say: 'But it is not an animal', you find it the same as your saying: 'This is not an animal' and 'Whenever it is a human it is an animal'. And the intent is comparison, not including the exceptive in the categorical, because it is its counter part not a part of it. The poet said: (And if it is disjunctive) the poet in these verses committed extreme conciseness. And also an objection was raised against his saying: 'So positing this one' that the subject of the demonstrative pronoun in language is the specification of the referent, so it is like placing the hand on the head, and that prevents making the reference of the demonstrative one of the two parts without specification, so that it covers all forms. So let one ponder. His saying: (what has passed) meaning: from being universal, not accidental, nor negative. His saying: (for composed of the thing and its negation is barren) because if you say: it has already been mentioned that the real disjunction is composed of the thing and its negation or the equivalent of its negation, so how does he mention here that it is only from the equivalent? The answer: he mentioned first that the real in terms of composition is true of two types, and here he mentioned in terms of production that one of them is barren, so there is no objection.
Source pages p. 704

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a line of poetry from al-Sullam al-Maroun. The author begins by quoting Ibn 'Arafa's report from al-Farabi regarding the structure of the disjunctive syllogism: the conditional (the major premise) is the disjunctive statement, and the exceptive (the minor premise) is the statement that posits or negates one of the parts. This is opposite to the categorical syllogism where the major is usually the universal and the minor the particular. Then the commentary explains the conditions for a real disjunction (al-munfasila al-haqiqiyya): it must be composed of a thing and the equivalent of its negation, not just the thing and its negation. If it is composed of a thing and its negation (e.g., 'either it is A or it is not A'), then the syllogism is barren (i.e., yields no new knowledge because the conclusion would be the same as one premise). The commentator then discusses a different interpretation by al-Shaykh al-Yusi, who compared the exceptive syllogism to the categorical first and second figures, but clarifies that this is a comparison, not an actual reduction. Finally, there is an objection about the use of the demonstrative pronoun 'this' (dha) in the matn, arguing that it should refer to a specific part, but the response is that it is meant to be general. The passage concludes with a question-answer about the two compositions of the real disjunction, clarifying that there is no contradiction: the real disjunction can be of two types compositionally, but only one type (the one composed of thing and equivalent of its negation) produces conclusions.

Key Terms
الشرطية (al-shartiyya): In this context, it refers to the conditional premise (major premise) in the exceptive syllogism, which can be either conjunctive (mutasila) or disjunctive (munfasila). [Note: In later texts, al-shartiyya may refer specifically to conditional propositions in general, but here it is the major premise in the exceptive syllogism.]
الاستثنائية (al-istithna'iyya): The exceptive premise (minor premise) that posits or negates one part of the conditional. [Note: This term is stable, but sometimes confused with the term 'istithna' in other contexts.]
المنفصلة الحقيقية (al-munfasila al-haqiqiyya): A real disjunction that is exclusive and exhaustive, i.e., the two parts cannot both be true and cannot both be false. Here it is composed of a thing and the equivalent of its negation. [Note: In earlier logic, the real disjunction is defined as excluding both coexistence and void. Here it is composed of a thing and its equivalent to the negation.]
عقيمة (aqima): Barren, i.e., a disjunction that yields no valid conclusion when used in an exceptive syllogism. [Note: This term is used metaphorically; it is not a standard technical term in all texts.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Productive and yields a new conclusion.
B) Barren because the conclusion is the same as the exceptive premise.
C) Equivalent to a categorical syllogism.
D) Only valid if the negation is universal.

Key Takeaway
A real disjunction must be composed of a thing and the equivalent of its negation to produce a valid conclusion; otherwise, it is barren.

The Four Conclusions of a Real Disjunctive Syllogism
Author enumerates the four possible conclusions in a real disjunctive syllogism and warns against tautological disjunctions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state and apply the four possible conclusions of a real disjunctive syllogism using a kalam example.

Core Idea
In a real disjunction, affirming one part negates the other, and negating one affirms the other.

The author presents the four valid inference patterns for a real disjunctive syllogism, using the example of the existent being either eternal or originated. This is a foundational tool for constructing valid arguments in kalam and usul.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disjunction
Find the disjunctive premise in the example: 'Either the existent is eternal or it is originated.' Note that the disjunction is real: both cannot be true and both cannot be false.
2
Trace the four inferences
Apply the rule: positing one part negates the other, and negating one affirms the other. Verify that each of the four cases is logically valid.
3
Test with Islamic example
Construct a similar disjunctive syllogism from fiqh or kalam, e.g., 'Either this act is wajib or it is haram (assuming no other categories).' Derive the four conclusions.
Ensure the disjunction is truly exclusive and exhaustive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الاستثنائي فِي الاسْتِثْنَائِيَّةِ أَنْتَجَ رَفْعَ الآخَرِ؛ لِامْتِنَاعِ اجْتِمَاعِهِمَا عَلَى الصِّدْقِ، وَأَيُّهُمَا رُفِعَ أَنْتَجَ وَضْعَ الآخَرِ؛ لِامْتِنَاعِ ارْتِفَاعِهِمَا، فَلَهُ أَرْبَعُ نَتَائِج؛ كَقَوْلِنَا [٦١/١]: دَائِماً : إِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ المَوْجُودُ قَدِيماً ، وَإِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ حَادِثاً»، فَإِذَا قُلْنَا : لَكِنَّهُ قَدِيمٌ أَنْتَجَ: «لَيْسَ بِحَادِثِ»، وَإِذَا قُلْنَا : «لَكِنَّهُ حَادِثٌ أَنْتَجَ : لَيْسَ بِقَدِيم»، وَإِذَا قُلْنَا : لَكِنَّهُ لَيْسَ بِقَدِيم أَنْتَجَ : فَهُوَ حَادِتٌ»، وَإِذَا قُلْنَا: «لَكِنَّهُ لَيْسَ بِحَادِثٍ، أَنْتَجَ : فَهُوَ قَدِيمٌ»، بِخِلَافِ : «إِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ المَوْجُودُ حَادِثاً ، أَوْ لَيْسَ حَادِثاً فَلَا يُنْتِجُ شَيْئاً ، لأَنَّ النَّتيجةَ حِينَئِذٍ تَكُونُ عَيْنَ الِاسْتِثْنَاناتِيَّةِ، وَتَلْزَمُ المُصَادَرَةُ عَلَى المَظْلُوبِ وَهِيَ فِي العُرْفِ : جَعْلُ المَطْلُوبِ جُزءاً مِنَ الدَّلِيلِ، وَلَا خَفَاءَ فِي فَسَادِ قِيَاسِ اتَّحَدَتْ إِحْدَى مُقَدِّمَتَيْهِ وَنَتِيجَتِهِ . قصارة - قوله : (لِأَنَّ النَّتِيجَةَ حِينَئِذٍ تَكُونُ عَيْنَ الِاسْتِثْنَائِيَّةِ بيانُهُ : أنَّك لو استثنيت عين المقدم وقلت : لكنَّه حادث أنتج نقيض التّالي وهو قولك: «هو حادث»، وهي غير الاستثنائية، وكذا يلزمُ في استثناء عين التّالي أو نقيض كل منهما ، فإذا قلت : «لكنه ليس بحادث أنتج: «ليس بحادث»، ولا خفاء في فساد قياس فساد قياس اتحدت إحدى مقدمتيه ونتيجته؛ لأنَّه إن علم صدق تلك المقدمة لم يحتج لقياس أصلاً، وإن لم يعلم لم يكن التَّوصُّل بالمجهول إلى نفسه .
Chapter on the exceptive syllogism. In the exceptive, when one of the two parts is posited, it produces the negation of the other, due to the impossibility of both being true. And whichever of them is negated produces the affirmation of the other, due to the impossibility of both being false. So it has four conclusions. Like our saying: 'Always: either the existent is eternal or it is originated.' So if we say: 'But it is eternal' it produces: 'It is not originated.' And if we say: 'But it is originated' it produces: 'It is not eternal.' And if we say: 'But it is not eternal' it produces: 'It is originated.' And if we say: 'But it is not originated' it produces: 'It is eternal.' Contrary to: 'Either the existent is originated or it is not originated' which produces nothing, because the conclusion then would be the same as the exceptive statement, and circular reasoning on the desired conclusion follows, and it is conventionally: making the desired conclusion a part of the proof. And there is no hiddenness in the corruption of a syllogism whose one premise and conclusion are identical. Marginal note - His saying: (because the conclusion then would be the same as the exceptive) its explanation: that if you except the exact antecedent and say: 'But it is originated' it produces the negation of the consequent which is your saying: 'It is originated', and it is not the exceptive, and likewise it is necessary in excepting the exact consequent or the negation of each of them. So if you say: 'But it is not originated' it produces: 'It is not originated', and there is no hiddenness in the corruption of a syllogism whose one premise and conclusion are identical; because if the truth of that premise is known, there is no need for a syllogism at all, and if it is not known, then arriving from the unknown to itself is not possible.
Source pages p. 705

Commentary
This passage is a concise exposition of the rules for a real disjunctive syllogism. The author states that in an exceptive syllogism where the disjunction is real (i.e., the two parts are mutually exclusive and collectively exhaustive), there are four possible conclusions: (1) affirming one part yields negation of the other, (2) affirming the other yields negation of the first, (3) negating one part yields affirmation of the other, (4) negating the other yields affirmation of the first. The example uses the philosophical pair 'eternal/originated' to illustrate. The author then contrasts this with a disjunction that is merely between a thing and its negation (e.g., 'either it is originated or it is not originated'), which yields no conclusion because the exceptive premise and the conclusion would be identical, resulting in a circular argument (petitio principii). This is a clear warning against using tautological disjunctions in reasoning. The marginal note adds a detailed explanation: if you take the exact antecedent 'It is originated' and say 'But it is originated', the conclusion 'It is not not-originated' is actually the same as affirming the antecedent, which is not a new conclusion; similarly for other cases. Thus the syllogism is corrupt because it assumes what it is trying to prove.

Key Terms
الاستثنائية
النتائج الأربع

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) It is not originated.
B) It is eternal.
C) It is originated.
D) The syllogism is invalid.

Key Takeaway
A real disjunctive syllogism yields four valid conclusions, each depending on which part is posited or negated.

Why Tautological Disjunctions Lead to Circular Reasoning
Author enumerates the four possible conclusions in a real disjunctive syllogism and warns against tautological disjunctions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a tautological disjunction and explain why it results in the fallacy of musadara (begging the question).

Core Idea
A disjunction like 'A or not A' is tautological and yields no new conclusion because the conclusion would be identical to the premise.

The author warns against using a disjunction that is merely between a thing and its negation, such as 'either it is originated or it is not originated.' Such a disjunction does not allow a valid inference because the conclusion would be the same as the exceptive premise, leading to circular reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the tautological disjunction
Find the example in the passage: 'Either the existent is originated or it is not originated.' Recognize that the two parts are contradictories, making the disjunction tautological.
2
Test each possible exception
Try each of the four possible exceptive premises: e.g., 'But it is originated' leads to the conclusion 'It is not not-originated', which is equivalent to 'It is originated'. Similarly for other cases.
3
Recognize the circularity
Notice that the conclusion is identical to the exceptive premise or its equivalent. This means the argument assumes what it is trying to prove, which is the fallacy of musadara (begging the question).
4
Understand the corruption
The author states that such a syllogism is corrupt (fasid) because if the premise is known, no syllogism is needed; if unknown, it cannot prove itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الاستثنائي فِي الاسْتِثْنَائِيَّةِ أَنْتَجَ رَفْعَ الآخَرِ؛ لِامْتِنَاعِ اجْتِمَاعِهِمَا عَلَى الصِّدْقِ، وَأَيُّهُمَا رُفِعَ أَنْتَجَ وَضْعَ الآخَرِ؛ لِامْتِنَاعِ ارْتِفَاعِهِمَا، فَلَهُ أَرْبَعُ نَتَائِج؛ كَقَوْلِنَا [٦١/١]: دَائِماً : إِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ المَوْجُودُ قَدِيماً ، وَإِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ حَادِثاً»، فَإِذَا قُلْنَا : لَكِنَّهُ قَدِيمٌ أَنْتَجَ: «لَيْسَ بِحَادِثِ»، وَإِذَا قُلْنَا : «لَكِنَّهُ حَادِثٌ أَنْتَجَ : لَيْسَ بِقَدِيم»، وَإِذَا قُلْنَا : لَكِنَّهُ لَيْسَ بِقَدِيم أَنْتَجَ : فَهُوَ حَادِتٌ»، وَإِذَا قُلْنَا: «لَكِنَّهُ لَيْسَ بِحَادِثٍ، أَنْتَجَ : فَهُوَ قَدِيمٌ»، بِخِلَافِ : «إِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ المَوْجُودُ حَادِثاً ، أَوْ لَيْسَ حَادِثاً فَلَا يُنْتِجُ شَيْئاً ، لأَنَّ النَّتيجةَ حِينَئِذٍ تَكُونُ عَيْنَ الِاسْتِثْنَاناتِيَّةِ، وَتَلْزَمُ المُصَادَرَةُ عَلَى المَظْلُوبِ وَهِيَ فِي العُرْفِ : جَعْلُ المَطْلُوبِ جُزءاً مِنَ الدَّلِيلِ، وَلَا خَفَاءَ فِي فَسَادِ قِيَاسِ اتَّحَدَتْ إِحْدَى مُقَدِّمَتَيْهِ وَنَتِيجَتِهِ . قصارة - قوله : (لِأَنَّ النَّتِيجَةَ حِينَئِذٍ تَكُونُ عَيْنَ الِاسْتِثْنَائِيَّةِ بيانُهُ : أنَّك لو استثنيت عين المقدم وقلت : لكنَّه حادث أنتج نقيض التّالي وهو قولك: «هو حادث»، وهي غير الاستثنائية، وكذا يلزمُ في استثناء عين التّالي أو نقيض كل منهما ، فإذا قلت : «لكنه ليس بحادث أنتج: «ليس بحادث»، ولا خفاء في فساد قياس فساد قياس اتحدت إحدى مقدمتيه ونتيجته؛ لأنَّه إن علم صدق تلك المقدمة لم يحتج لقياس أصلاً، وإن لم يعلم لم يكن التَّوصُّل بالمجهول إلى نفسه .
Chapter on the exceptive syllogism. In the exceptive, when one of the two parts is posited, it produces the negation of the other, due to the impossibility of both being true. And whichever of them is negated produces the affirmation of the other, due to the impossibility of both being false. So it has four conclusions. Like our saying: 'Always: either the existent is eternal or it is originated.' So if we say: 'But it is eternal' it produces: 'It is not originated.' And if we say: 'But it is originated' it produces: 'It is not eternal.' And if we say: 'But it is not eternal' it produces: 'It is originated.' And if we say: 'But it is not originated' it produces: 'It is eternal.' Contrary to: 'Either the existent is originated or it is not originated' which produces nothing, because the conclusion then would be the same as the exceptive statement, and circular reasoning on the desired conclusion follows, and it is conventionally: making the desired conclusion a part of the proof. And there is no hiddenness in the corruption of a syllogism whose one premise and conclusion are identical. Marginal note - His saying: (because the conclusion then would be the same as the exceptive) its explanation: that if you except the exact antecedent and say: 'But it is originated' it produces the negation of the consequent which is your saying: 'It is originated', and it is not the exceptive, and likewise it is necessary in excepting the exact consequent or the negation of each of them. So if you say: 'But it is not originated' it produces: 'It is not originated', and there is no hiddenness in the corruption of a syllogism whose one premise and conclusion are identical; because if the truth of that premise is known, there is no need for a syllogism at all, and if it is not known, then arriving from the unknown to itself is not possible.
Source pages p. 705

Commentary
This passage is a concise exposition of the rules for a real disjunctive syllogism. The author states that in an exceptive syllogism where the disjunction is real (i.e., the two parts are mutually exclusive and collectively exhaustive), there are four possible conclusions: (1) affirming one part yields negation of the other, (2) affirming the other yields negation of the first, (3) negating one part yields affirmation of the other, (4) negating the other yields affirmation of the first. The example uses the philosophical pair 'eternal/originated' to illustrate. The author then contrasts this with a disjunction that is merely between a thing and its negation (e.g., 'either it is originated or it is not originated'), which yields no conclusion because the exceptive premise and the conclusion would be identical, resulting in a circular argument (petitio principii). This is a clear warning against using tautological disjunctions in reasoning. The marginal note adds a detailed explanation: if you take the exact antecedent 'It is originated' and say 'But it is originated', the conclusion 'It is not not-originated' is actually the same as affirming the antecedent, which is not a new conclusion; similarly for other cases. Thus the syllogism is corrupt because it assumes what it is trying to prove.

Key Terms
المصادرة
الاستثنائية

C) It yields no conclusion because of circular reasoning.
D) It yields one conclusion.

Key Takeaway
A disjunction that is merely between a thing and its negation leads to circular reasoning (musadara) and must be avoided in sound argumentation.

The Syllogism
beginner

Exclusive vs Exhaustive Disjunctions in Exceptive Syllogisms
Author distinguishes exclusive and exhaustive disjunctions with inference rules.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between exclusive (mani'at al-jam') and exhaustive (mani'at al-khuluw) disjunctions, and identify which exceptive premise yields a valid conclusion for each type.

Core Idea
Exclusive prevents both true; exhaustive prevents both false.

In this passage, the author explains two fundamental types of disjunction used in conditional syllogisms. Understanding the difference between exclusive (mani'at al-jam') and exhaustive (mani'at al-khuluw) is crucial for correctly applying exceptive syllogisms. The rules for what you can conclude depend entirely on which type of disjunction you have.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disjunction type
Determine if the conditional is exclusive (mani'at al-jam') – meaning both parts cannot be true – or exhaustive (mani'at al-khuluw) – meaning both parts cannot be false.
Exclusive: 'Either A or B' where A and B cannot both be true. Exhaustive: 'Either A or B' where A and B cannot both be false.
2
Apply rule for exclusive disjunction
If the exceptive premise affirms one part, you can conclude the negation of the other. If it negates one part, no conclusion follows because both could be false.
Example: 'Either it is a body or an accident.' If you affirm 'it is a body', conclude 'it is not an accident.' If you negate 'it is not a body', no conclusion.
3
Apply rule for exhaustive disjunction
If the exceptive premise negates one part, you can conclude the affirmation of the other. If it affirms one part, no conclusion follows because both could be true.
Example: 'Either it is non-body or non-eternal.' If you negate 'it is non-body' (i.e., affirm it is a body), conclude 'it is non-eternal.' If you affirm 'it is non-body', no conclusion.
4
Practise with Islamic examples
Test your understanding by applying the rules to examples from fiqh or kalam, checking whether the conclusion follows according to the disjunction type.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِذَا كَانَتْ شَرْطِيَّتُهُ مَانِعَةَ جَمْع شُرِطَ فِي الِاسْتِثْنَائِيَّةِ أَنْ تَكُونَ وَاضِعَةٌ لِأَحَدِ الطَّرَفَيْنِ، فَإِنْ وَضَعْتَ المُقَدَّمَ أَنْتَجَ نَقِيضَ التَّالِي، وَإِنْ وَضَعْتَ التَّالِي أَنْتَجَ نَقِيضَ المُقَدَّمِ؛ لِامْتِنَاعِ اجْتِمَاعِهِمَا ؛ كَقَوْلِكَ : «إِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ المَوْجُودُ جِرْماً ، وَإِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ عَرَضاً»، فَإِذَا قُلْتَ: لَكِنَّهُ جِرْمٌ أَنْتَجَ : «لَيْسَ بِعَرَضِ»، أَوْ قُلْتَ : لَكِنَّهُ عَرَضٌ أَنْتَجَ : «لَيْسَ بِجِرْم»، وَلَا يَلْزَمُ نْ رَفْعِ أَحَدٍ طَرَفَيْهَا وَضْعُ الآخَرِ ؛ لِجَوَازِ الخُلُوِّ عَنْهُمَا ، فَلَوْ قُلْتَ : لَكِنَّهُ لَيْسَ بِجِرْمٍ» أَوْ لَيْسَ بِعَرَضِ» لَمْ يُنْتِجُ شَيْئاً. وَإِذَا كَانَتْ شَرْطِيَّتُهُ مَانِعَةَ خُلُو اشْتُرِطَ فِي الاسْتِثْنَائِيَّةِ أَنْ تَكُونَ رَافِعَةٌ لِأَحَدٍ طَرَفَي الشَّرْطِيَّةِ ، فَإِذَا رَفَعْتَ عَيْنَ المُقَدَّم أَنْتَجَ وَضْعَ التَّالِي ، وَإِذَا رَفَعْتَ عَيْنَ التَّالِي أَنْتَجَ وَضْعَ المُقَدَّمِ ؛ لامْتِنَاعِ الخُلُوِّ ، وَلَا يَلْزَمُ مِنْ وَضْعِ أَحَدِ الطَّرَفَيْنِ رَفْعَ الآخَرِ ؛ لِجَوَازِ الاجْتِمَاعِ ؛ كَقَوْلِنَا : «إِمَّا أَنَّ يَكُونَ المَوْجُودُ غَيْرَ جِرْمٍ، وَإِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ غَيْرَ قَدِيمٍ، فَإِذَا قُلْنَا : لَكِنَّهُ قَدِيمٌ أَنْتَجَ : فَهُوَ غَيْرُ جِرْمٍ، وَإِذَا قُلْنَا : لَكِنَّهُ جِرْمٌ أَنْتَجَ: «فَهُوَ غَيْرُ قَدِيمٍ، وَلَوْ قُلْتَ: لَكِنَّهُ غَيْرُ قَدِيمٍ» أَوْ قُلْتَ : لَكِنَّهُ غَيْرُ جِرْمٍ لَمْ يُنْتِجُ شَيْئاً لِمَا مَرَّ.
And if its conditional is exclusive (mani'at jam'), it is stipulated in the exceptive that it be positing one of the two parts. For if you posit the antecedent, it produces the negation of the consequent; and if you posit the consequent, it produces the negation of the antecedent, due to the impossibility of their coexistence. Like your saying: 'Either the existent is a body or it is an accident.' So if you say: 'But it is a body' it produces: 'It is not an accident.' Or you say: 'But it is an accident' it produces: 'It is not a body.' And it does not follow from the negation of one of its two parts the affirmation of the other, due to the possibility of void from both. So if you say: 'But it is not a body' or 'It is not an accident', it produces nothing. And if its conditional is exhaustive (mani'at khulu), it is stipulated in the exceptive that it be negating one of the two parts of the conditional. For if you negate the exact antecedent, it produces the affirmation of the consequent; and if you negate the exact consequent, it produces the affirmation of the antecedent, due to the impossibility of void. And it does not follow from the affirmation of one of the two parts the negation of the other, due to the possibility of coexistence. Like our saying: 'Either the existent is non-body or it is non-eternal.' So if we say: 'But it is eternal' it produces: 'It is non-body.' And if we say: 'But it is a body' it produces: 'It is non-eternal.' And if you say: 'But it is non-eternal' or 'But it is non-body', it produces nothing for what has passed.
Source pages p. 705

Commentary
This passage defines two types of disjunction: exclusive (mani'at al-jam') and exhaustive (mani'at al-khuluw). For an exclusive disjunction, the two parts cannot both be true. Thus, if you affirm one part (in the exceptive premise), you can conclude the negation of the other. However, negating one part does not allow you to affirm the other, because both could be false (the void possibility). The example uses 'body or accident' which are categories of substance and accidents; they cannot both be true of the same thing, but it is possible for a thing to be neither (e.g., a vacuum). Therefore, only positing a part yields a conclusion. For an exhaustive disjunction, the two parts cannot both be false; they are collectively exhaustive. Thus, if you negate one part, you can conclude the affirmation of the other. But affirming one part does not allow you to negate the other, because both could be true (the coexistence possibility). The example uses 'non-body or non-eternal'; these could both be true for something that is neither body nor eternal (e.g., a soul). Therefore, only negating a part yields a conclusion. This classification is crucial for correctly applying exceptive syllogisms.

Key Terms
مانعة الجمع
مانعة الخلو
الاستثنائية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In an exclusive disjunction (mani'at al-jam') like 'Either this is a body or it is an accident', which exceptive premise yields a valid conclusion?
A) But it is not a body.
B) But it is not an accident.
C) But it is a body.
D) Both A and B yield valid conclusions.

Key Takeaway
The validity of an exceptive syllogism depends on the type of disjunction: exclusive allows conclusion only from affirmation; exhaustive allows conclusion only from negation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

The Syllogism

The Real Conditional and Its Four Valid Inferences
Classifies three types of conditional syllogism and their valid conclusions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four valid inferences in a real conditional syllogism and distinguish it from a tautology.

Core Idea
A real conditional has four valid conclusions: affirming one term negates the other, and negating one affirms the other.

In this lesson, we examine the first type of conditional syllogism from our passage: the 'real conditional' (al-shartiyya al-haqiqiyya). This type is equivalent to a logical biconditional, where the two disjuncts are both mutually exclusive and jointly exhaustive. Understanding its valid inference patterns is crucial for constructing sound arguments in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional disjunction
The real conditional has two terms that cannot be true together and cannot be false together. For example: 'Either the existent is eternal, or it is originated.'
2
Recognize the four possible exceptive premises
You can affirm either term or negate either term. These are the four 'istithna'iyya' premises.
3
Derive the four conclusions
Affirm eternal => not originated; affirm originated => not eternal; negate eternal => originated; negate originated => eternal.
Each conclusion follows necessarily due to the mutual exclusivity and exhaustiveness.
4
Spot the circular case
If the disjunction is a tautology, like 'either originated or not originated', the conclusion merely repeats the premise, leading to circular reasoning (musadara).
Such a syllogism is logically useless because the conclusion is already assumed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الاستثنائي. في الاستثنائية أنتج رفع الآخر؛ لامتناع اجتماعهما على الصدق، وأيهما رفع أنتج وضع الآخر؛ لامتناع ارتفاعهما، فله أربع نتائج؛ كقولنا: دائماً: إما أن يكون الموجود قديماً، وإما أن يكون حادثاً، فإذا قلنا: لكنه قديم أنتج: ليس بحادث، وإذا قلنا: لكنه حادث أنتج: ليس بقديم، وإذا قلنا: لكنه ليس بقديم أنتج: فهو حادث، وإذا قلنا: لكنه ليس بحادث، أنتج: فهو قديم، بخلاف: إما أن يكون الموجود حادثاً، أو ليس حادثاً فلا ينتج شيئاً، لأن النتيجة حينئذ تكون عين الاستثنائية، وتلزم المصادرة على المطلوب وهي في العرف: جعل المطلوب جزءاً من الدليل، ولا خفاء في فساد قياس اتحدت إحدى مقدمتيه ونتيجته. وإذا كانت شرطيته مانعة جمع اشترط في الاستثنائية أن تكون واضعة لأحد الطرفين، فإن وضعت المقدم أنتج نقيض التالي، وإن وضعت التالي أنتج نقيض المقدم؛ لامتناع اجتماعهما؛ كقولك: إما أن يكون الموجود جرماً، وإما أن يكون عرضاً، فإذا قلت: لكنه جرم أنتج: ليس بعرض، أو قلت: لكنه عرض أنتج: ليس بجرم، ولا يلزم من رفع أحد طرفيها وضع الآخر؛ لجواز الخلو عنهما، فلو قلت: لكنه ليس بجرم أو ليس بعرض لم ينتج شيئاً. وإذا كانت شرطيته مانعة خلوا اشترط في الاستثنائية أن تكون رافعة لأحد طرفي الشرطية، فإذا رفعت عين المقدم أنتج وضع التالي، وإذا رفعت عين التالي أنتج وضع المقدم؛ لامتناع الخلو، ولا يلزم من وضع أحد الطرفين رفع الآخر؛ لجواز الاجتماع؛ كقولنا: إما أن يكون الموجود غير جرم، وإما أن يكون غير قديم، فإذا قلنا: لكنه قديم أنتج: فهو غير جرم، وإذا قلنا: لكنه جرم أنتج: فهو غير قديم، ولو قلت: لكنه غير قديم أو قلت: لكنه غير جرم لم ينتج شيئاً لما مر. قوله: (لأن النتيجة حينئذ تكون عين الاستثنائية) بيانه: أنك لو استثنيت عين المقدم وقلت: لكنه حادث أنتج نقيض التالي وهو قولك: «هو حادث»، وهي غير الاستثنائية، وكذا يلزم في استثناء عين التالي أو نقيض كل منهما، فإذا قلت: «لكنه ليس بحادث» أنتج: «ليس بحادث»، ولا خفاء في فساد قياس اتحدت إحدى مقدمتيه ونتيجته؛ لأنه إن علم صدق تلك المقدمة لم يحتج لقياس أصلاً، وإن لم يعلم لم يكن التوصُّل بالمجهول إلى نفسه. فتحصل أن الذي شرطيته حقيقية ينتج أربع نتائج: ثنتان في وضع أحد الطرفين وثنتان في رفعه، والذي شرطيته مانعة جمع ينتج الأولين من نتائج الحقيقية، والذي شرطيته مانعة خلوا ينتج الأخيرتين، والله تعالى الموفق.
Chapter on the conditional syllogism. In the [real] conditional, raising the other [term] produces [a conclusion] due to the impossibility of both being true together, and whichever is raised produces placing the other due to the impossibility of their both being false. So it has four results. Like our saying: 'Always: either the existent is eternal, or it is originated.' So if we say: 'But it is eternal,' it produces: 'It is not originated.' And if we say: 'But it is originated,' it produces: 'It is not eternal.' And if we say: 'But it is not eternal,' it produces: 'It is originated.' And if we say: 'But it is not originated,' it produces: 'It is eternal.' Contrary to: 'Either the existent is originated, or it is not originated' — this produces nothing because the result then becomes the same as the conditional, and there occurs begging the question, which in customary usage is making the desired [conclusion] a part of the proof. There is no hiddenness in the corruption of a syllogism whose one premise and conclusion are identical. And if its conditional is exclusive (mani'at al-jam'), then it is required in the exceptive [syllogism] that it be placing one of the two terms. If you place the antecedent, it produces the contradictory of the consequent; if you place the consequent, it produces the contradictory of the antecedent, due to the impossibility of both being together. Like your saying: 'Either the existent is a substance, or it is an accident.' So if you say: 'But it is a substance,' it produces: 'It is not an accident.' Or if you say: 'But it is an accident,' it produces: 'It is not a substance.' And it does not follow from the removal of one of its terms the placing of the other, due to the possibility of both being absent. So if you say: 'But it is not a substance' or 'not an accident,' it produces nothing. And if its conditional is exhaustive (mani'at al-khuluw), it is required in the exceptive [syllogism] that it be removing one of the two terms of the conditional. So if you remove the exact antecedent, it produces placing the consequent; if you remove the exact consequent, it produces placing the antecedent, due to the impossibility of both being absent. And it does not follow from placing one of the two terms the removal of the other, due to the possibility of both being together. Like our saying: 'Either the existent is not a substance, or it is not eternal.' So if we say: 'But it is eternal,' it produces: 'It is not a substance.' And if we say: 'But it is a substance,' it produces: 'It is not eternal.' And if you said: 'But it is not eternal' or 'But it is not a substance,' it would produce nothing, due to what has passed. His saying: ('Because the result then becomes the same as the conditional') its explanation: That if you except the exact antecedent and say: 'But it is originated,' it produces the contradictory of the consequent, which is your saying: 'It is originated' — that is not the same as the conditional. Likewise it follows in excepting the exact consequent or the contradictory of each. So if you say: 'But it is not originated,' it produces: 'It is not originated.' There is no hiddenness in the corruption of a syllogism whose one premise and conclusion are identical, because if the truth of that premise is known, there is no need for a syllogism at all; and if it is not known, one cannot arrive from the unknown to itself. So it is concluded that the one whose conditional is real produces four results: two from placing one of the two terms and two from removing it. The one whose conditional is exclusive produces the first two of the real results. The one whose conditional is exhaustive produces the last two. And Allah Almighty is the granter of success.
Source pages p. 705

Commentary
This passage defines three types of conditional syllogisms (qiyas istithna'i) and specifies their valid conclusions. The first type is the 'real' conditional (shartiyya haqiqiyya), which is equivalent to the logical material biconditional: both terms cannot be true together and cannot be false together. From this, four valid inferences arise: affirming one term negates the other; negating one affirms the other. The example given uses 'the existent is either eternal or originated': affirming eternity negates origination, and vice versa; negating eternity affirms origination, and vice versa. The passage then warns against using an exclusive disjunction like 'either originated or not originated' because the conclusion would be a mere repetition (the result becomes the same as the conditional), which is circular reasoning (musadara). The second type is the exclusive conditional (mani'at al-jam'), where both terms cannot be true together but can be false together. Here, the only valid inferences are affirming one term to negate the other; negating one term does not affirm the other. The example uses 'either a substance or an accident': affirming substance negates accident, but negating substance does not affirm accident (since it could be neither). The third type is the exhaustive conditional (mani'at al-khuluw), where both terms cannot be false together but can be true together. Here, the valid inferences are negating one term to affirm the other; affirming one does not negate the other. The example uses 'either not a substance or not eternal': negating 'not a substance' (i.e., affirming substance) affirms 'not eternal' (i.e., denies eternity), but affirming 'not a substance' (negating substance) does not affirm 'not eternal' (since both could be true). The commentary then clarifies that if the conclusion equals the conditional, it's a circular argument. The summary restates: real yields four results, exclusive yields the first two (affirmative inferences), exhaustive yields the last two (negative inferences). All examples are from Islamic theology and ontology: eternal vs. originated, substance vs. accident.

Key Terms
الشرطية الحقيقية
استثنائية
مصادرة

A) Affirming one term allows you to conclude the other term.
B) Negating one term allows you to conclude the negation of the other.
C) Negating one term allows you to conclude the other term.
D) None of the above.

Key Takeaway
A real conditional yields four valid inferences: affirming either term negates the other, and negating either term affirms the other.

Construct a real conditional syllogism on an Islamic topic (e.g., 'Either the Quran is created or uncreated'). Then list all four valid conclusions assuming the disjunction is real.
?
Identify whether the following disjunction is a real conditional or a tautology: 'Either Zakat is obligatory or not obligatory.' Explain why the latter is useless.

The Syllogism
intermediate
7 min
The Exclusive Conditional and Its Valid Inference
Explains the valid inference pattern for exclusive conditional syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the valid inference in an exclusive conditional (mani'at al-jam') and recognize the fallacy of negating one term to affirm the other.

Core Idea
In an exclusive conditional, only affirming one term allows you to negate the other; negating one does not guarantee the other.

The second type of conditional syllogism is the exclusive conditional (mani'at al-jam'), where the two disjuncts cannot both be true but can both be false. This lesson focuses on its specific valid inference pattern and common pitfall, illustrated with the ontological example of substance and accident.

Guided Path
1
Identify the exclusive disjunction
In an exclusive conditional, the two terms cannot be true together, but they may both be false. Example: 'Either the existent is a substance or an accident.'
2
Recognize the valid exceptive premise
The only valid exceptive premise is one that affirms (places) one of the two terms. For example: 'But it is a substance.'
The exceptive must be a placing (واضعة) of one term, not a removal.
3
Derive the valid conclusion
If you affirm one term, you can conclude the negation of the other. For substance/accident: affirm substance => not accident; affirm accident => not substance.
This is because both cannot be true simultaneously.
4
Recognize the invalid inference
If you negate (remove) one term, you cannot conclude the other, because both might be false. For example, 'but it is not a substance' does not imply 'it is an accident'.
The passage explicitly warns: 'it does not follow from the removal of one of its terms the placing of the other'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الاستثنائي. في الاستثنائية أنتج رفع الآخر؛ لامتناع اجتماعهما على الصدق، وأيهما رفع أنتج وضع الآخر؛ لامتناع ارتفاعهما، فله أربع نتائج؛ كقولنا: دائماً: إما أن يكون الموجود قديماً، وإما أن يكون حادثاً، فإذا قلنا: لكنه قديم أنتج: ليس بحادث، وإذا قلنا: لكنه حادث أنتج: ليس بقديم، وإذا قلنا: لكنه ليس بقديم أنتج: فهو حادث، وإذا قلنا: لكنه ليس بحادث، أنتج: فهو قديم، بخلاف: إما أن يكون الموجود حادثاً، أو ليس حادثاً فلا ينتج شيئاً، لأن النتيجة حينئذ تكون عين الاستثنائية، وتلزم المصادرة على المطلوب وهي في العرف: جعل المطلوب جزءاً من الدليل، ولا خفاء في فساد قياس اتحدت إحدى مقدمتيه ونتيجته. وإذا كانت شرطيته مانعة جمع اشترط في الاستثنائية أن تكون واضعة لأحد الطرفين، فإن وضعت المقدم أنتج نقيض التالي، وإن وضعت التالي أنتج نقيض المقدم؛ لامتناع اجتماعهما؛ كقولك: إما أن يكون الموجود جرماً، وإما أن يكون عرضاً، فإذا قلت: لكنه جرم أنتج: ليس بعرض، أو قلت: لكنه عرض أنتج: ليس بجرم، ولا يلزم من رفع أحد طرفيها وضع الآخر؛ لجواز الخلو عنهما، فلو قلت: لكنه ليس بجرم أو ليس بعرض لم ينتج شيئاً. وإذا كانت شرطيته مانعة خلوا اشترط في الاستثنائية أن تكون رافعة لأحد طرفي الشرطية، فإذا رفعت عين المقدم أنتج وضع التالي، وإذا رفعت عين التالي أنتج وضع المقدم؛ لامتناع الخلو، ولا يلزم من وضع أحد الطرفين رفع الآخر؛ لجواز الاجتماع؛ كقولنا: إما أن يكون الموجود غير جرم، وإما أن يكون غير قديم، فإذا قلنا: لكنه قديم أنتج: فهو غير جرم، وإذا قلنا: لكنه جرم أنتج: فهو غير قديم، ولو قلت: لكنه غير قديم أو قلت: لكنه غير جرم لم ينتج شيئاً لما مر. قوله: (لأن النتيجة حينئذ تكون عين الاستثنائية) بيانه: أنك لو استثنيت عين المقدم وقلت: لكنه حادث أنتج نقيض التالي وهو قولك: «هو حادث»، وهي غير الاستثنائية، وكذا يلزم في استثناء عين التالي أو نقيض كل منهما، فإذا قلت: «لكنه ليس بحادث» أنتج: «ليس بحادث»، ولا خفاء في فساد قياس اتحدت إحدى مقدمتيه ونتيجته؛ لأنه إن علم صدق تلك المقدمة لم يحتج لقياس أصلاً، وإن لم يعلم لم يكن التوصُّل بالمجهول إلى نفسه. فتحصل أن الذي شرطيته حقيقية ينتج أربع نتائج: ثنتان في وضع أحد الطرفين وثنتان في رفعه، والذي شرطيته مانعة جمع ينتج الأولين من نتائج الحقيقية، والذي شرطيته مانعة خلوا ينتج الأخيرتين، والله تعالى الموفق.
Chapter on the conditional syllogism. In the [real] conditional, raising the other [term] produces [a conclusion] due to the impossibility of both being true together, and whichever is raised produces placing the other due to the impossibility of their both being false. So it has four results. Like our saying: 'Always: either the existent is eternal, or it is originated.' So if we say: 'But it is eternal,' it produces: 'It is not originated.' And if we say: 'But it is originated,' it produces: 'It is not eternal.' And if we say: 'But it is not eternal,' it produces: 'It is originated.' And if we say: 'But it is not originated,' it produces: 'It is eternal.' Contrary to: 'Either the existent is originated, or it is not originated' — this produces nothing because the result then becomes the same as the conditional, and there occurs begging the question, which in customary usage is making the desired [conclusion] a part of the proof. There is no hiddenness in the corruption of a syllogism whose one premise and conclusion are identical. And if its conditional is exclusive (mani'at al-jam'), then it is required in the exceptive [syllogism] that it be placing one of the two terms. If you place the antecedent, it produces the contradictory of the consequent; if you place the consequent, it produces the contradictory of the antecedent, due to the impossibility of both being together. Like your saying: 'Either the existent is a substance, or it is an accident.' So if you say: 'But it is a substance,' it produces: 'It is not an accident.' Or if you say: 'But it is an accident,' it produces: 'It is not a substance.' And it does not follow from the removal of one of its terms the placing of the other, due to the possibility of both being absent. So if you say: 'But it is not a substance' or 'not an accident,' it produces nothing. And if its conditional is exhaustive (mani'at al-khuluw), it is required in the exceptive [syllogism] that it be removing one of the two terms of the conditional. So if you remove the exact antecedent, it produces placing the consequent; if you remove the exact consequent, it produces placing the antecedent, due to the impossibility of both being absent. And it does not follow from placing one of the two terms the removal of the other, due to the possibility of both being together. Like our saying: 'Either the existent is not a substance, or it is not eternal.' So if we say: 'But it is eternal,' it produces: 'It is not a substance.' And if we say: 'But it is a substance,' it produces: 'It is not eternal.' And if you said: 'But it is not eternal' or 'But it is not a substance,' it would produce nothing, due to what has passed. His saying: ('Because the result then becomes the same as the conditional') its explanation: That if you except the exact antecedent and say: 'But it is originated,' it produces the contradictory of the consequent, which is your saying: 'It is originated' — that is not the same as the conditional. Likewise it follows in excepting the exact consequent or the contradictory of each. So if you say: 'But it is not originated,' it produces: 'It is not originated.' There is no hiddenness in the corruption of a syllogism whose one premise and conclusion are identical, because if the truth of that premise is known, there is no need for a syllogism at all; and if it is not known, one cannot arrive from the unknown to itself. So it is concluded that the one whose conditional is real produces four results: two from placing one of the two terms and two from removing it. The one whose conditional is exclusive produces the first two of the real results. The one whose conditional is exhaustive produces the last two. And Allah Almighty is the granter of success.

The Exhaustive Conditional and Its Valid Inference
Explains the valid inference pattern for exhaustive conditional syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the valid inference in an exhaustive conditional (mani'at al-khuluw) and recognize the fallacy of affirming one term to negate the other.

Core Idea
In an exhaustive conditional, only negating one term allows you to affirm the other; affirming one does not negate the other.

The third type of conditional syllogism is the exhaustive conditional (mani'at al-khuluw), where the two disjuncts cannot both be false but can both be true. This lesson explains its unique valid inference pattern, using the example 'either not a substance or not eternal' from the passage.

Guided Path
1
Identify the exhaustive disjunction
In an exhaustive conditional, the two terms cannot be false together, but they may both be true. Example: 'Either the existent is not a substance or it is not eternal.'
2
Recognize the valid exceptive premise
The valid exceptive premise must remove (raf') one of the two disjuncts. For example: 'But it is eternal' which removes the disjunct 'not eternal'? Wait: The example says 'If we say: But it is eternal, it produces: it is not a substance.' Here, 'it is eternal' is the negation of the second disjunct 'not eternal'? Actually, the disjuncts are: 'not a substance' and 'not eternal'. So 'it is eternal' is a removal of the second disjunct (since it denies 'not eternal'). So the exceptive premise is a removal of one disjunct.
The exceptive must be a removal (رافعة) of one term.
3
Derive the valid conclusion
If you remove one disjunct, you can conclude the other. For example: remove 'not eternal' (by saying 'it is eternal') => conclude 'not a substance' (the other disjunct).
This is because both cannot be false; if one is false, the other must be true.
4
Recognize the invalid inference
If you affirm (place) one disjunct, you cannot conclude the removal of the other, because both might be true. For example, affirming 'not a substance' (by saying 'but it is not a substance') does not imply 'eternal' (since both could be false? Actually, if both are true, then both are true, so affirming one leaves the other undetermined). The passage warns: 'it does not follow from placing one of the two terms the removal of the other'.

Here, the only valid inferences are affirming one term to negate the other; negating one term does not affirm the other. The example uses 'either a substance or an accident': affirming substance negates accident, but negating substance does not affirm accident (since it could be neither). The third type is the exhaustive conditional (mani'at al-khuluw), where both terms cannot be false together but can be true together. Here, the valid inferences are negating one term to affirm the other; affirming one does not negate the other. The example uses 'either not a substance or not eternal': negating 'not a substance' (i.e., affirming substance) affirms 'not eternal' (i.e., denies eternity), but affirming 'not a substance' (negating substance) does not affirm 'not eternal' (since both could be true). The commentary then clarifies that if the conclusion equals the conditional, it's a circular argument. The summary restates: real yields four results, exclusive yields the first two (affirmative inferences), exhaustive yields the last two (negative inferences). All examples are from Islamic theology and ontology: eternal vs. originated, substance vs. accident.

Key Terms
مانعة الخلو
استثنائية
رافعة

Key Takeaway
In an exhaustive conditional, the only valid inference is negating one term to affirm the other; affirming one term yields no conclusion.
