Refuting a Condition for Exhaustive Conditional Syllogisms
Refutes the claim that mani'at al-khuluw requires both terms to be negative or one negative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the condition that an exhaustive conditional syllogism requires negative terms and construct valid exhaustive conditional syllogisms with affirmative terms.

Core Idea
The exhaustive conditional yields a valid syllogism regardless of whether its terms are affirmative or negative.

This passage refutes a condition imposed by some logicians that the exhaustive conditional (mani'at al-khuluw) requires both terms to be negative or one negative. The author demonstrates through an example and a proof that the logical mechanism—impossibility of both being false—works even with affirmative terms. A later objection concerning predicates that are negative in meaning is also addressed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition being refuted
Find the exact stipulation mentioned in the passage: that for an exhaustive conditional to produce a valid conclusion, the two terms must both be negative, or one negative and one affirmative. Note that this is the claim being rejected.
The condition is attributed to al-Amidi.
2
Extract the counterexample
Take the example: 'Either Zaid is in the sea, or he is safe from drowning.' Both terms are affirmative in form. Verify that this is indeed an exhaustive conditional—it is impossible for Zaid to be neither in the sea nor safe from drowning.
The example shows the condition fails.
3
Understand the proof
Examine the reasoning: because the two terms cannot both be false, negating one forces the other to be true. This principle (taken from the disjunction) is the cause of productiveness, and it operates regardless of whether the terms are affirmative or negative.
No additional conditions are needed.
4
Analyze the objection about negative predicates
Note the possible objection: 'safe from drowning' seems negative because it means 'not drowning.' The commentator argues that an affirmative proposition with a negative predicate is not equivalent to a negative proposition, as shown by differences in conversion and syllogistic figures.
This reinforces that affirmative terms in the disjunction are fine.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تنبيه: اشترط بعضهم لإنتاج مانعة الخلو أن يكون طرفاها سالبتين، أو أحدهما سالبا والآخر موجبا، وهو غير صحيح، ولا دليل عليه، وعلة الإنتاج قائمة مع كونهما إيجابيتين؛ نحو: إما أن يكون زيد في البحر، وإما أن يكون سالما من الغرق، فإذا قلنا: لكنه ليس في البحر أنتج: هو سالم من الغرق، فإذا قلنا: لكنه ليس سالما من الغرق أنتج: هو في البحر، وبرهان ذلك امتناع ارتفاعهما، فمتى رفع أحدهما صدق الآخر. قوله: (تنبيه) هو خبر لمبتدأ محذوف مثل الباب في الكلام، وهو في الاصطلاح: اسم لتفصيل ما تقدم إجمالاً. وقيل له ذلك؛ لأنه في حكم البديهي، وقد تقدم ما يؤخذ منه نفي هذا الشرط، وأشار هنا في هذا التنبيه إلى ذكر ذلك تفصيلاً. قوله: (اشترط بعضهم) المشترط هو الآمدي في شرح إيساغوجي، حسبما نقله عنه الهلالي، والمعترض عليه بما أشار له هذا الشارح هو الشيخ الهلالي. قوله: (وإما أن يكون سالما من الغرق) أورد عليه الهلالي سؤالاً وأجاب عنه؛ نصه: فإن قلت: السلامة من الغرق أمر عدمي، فتكون في حكم السالبة. قلت: لا تكون في حكمها، ولا هي بمعناها، فإن السالبة محكوم فيها بسلب محمول وجودي أو عدمي، وهذه محكوم فيها بإثبات محمول عدمي، وشتان ما بينهما، ولو كانت التي محمولها عدمي في قوة السالبة، وكان لها حكمها، لوجب أن تنعكس كنفسها بالمستوي إن كانت كلية، وأن لا تنعكس أصلاً إن كانت جزئية، وأن تغني عن السالبة في إنتاج الشكل الثاني، واللوازم منتفية، فالحق أنه متى تحقق العناد الخلوي بين موجبتين أو سالبتين، أو مختلفتين تحقق الإنتاج، وإلا جاز الخلو، والفرض منعه.
A note: Some scholars stipulated for the productiveness of the exhaustive [conditional] that its two terms be negative, or one negative and the other affirmative. But this is not correct, and there is no evidence for it. The cause of productiveness exists even when both are affirmative. Example: 'Either Zaid is in the sea, or he is safe from drowning.' So if we say: 'But he is not in the sea,' it produces: 'He is safe from drowning.' Then if we say: 'But he is not safe from drowning,' it produces: 'He is in the sea.' The proof of that is the impossibility of both being absent. So whenever one is removed, the other becomes true. His saying: ('A note') is a predicate of an omitted subject like 'al-bab' in speech, and in terminology it is a name for detailing what was previously mentioned in summary. It is called that because it is in the status of the self-evident. Previously there occurred what indicates the negation of this condition, and here in this note he indicates mentioning that in detail. His saying: ('Some scholars stipulated') the stipulator is al-Amidi in his commentary on Isaghuji, according to what al-Hilali transmitted from him, and the one objecting to it with what this commentator indicated is al-Shaykh al-Hilali. His saying: ('And either he is safe from drowning') al-Hilali raised a question and answered it: 'If you say: Safety from drowning is a non-existent thing, so it is in the status of a negative. I say: It is not in its status, nor is it in its meaning. For the negative is ruled by the removal of a positive or negative predicate, while this is ruled by the affirmation of a negative predicate. What a difference between them. And if that whose predicate is negative were in the strength of a negative and had its ruling, it would have to convert like itself by equality if it were universal, and not convert at all if it were particular, and it would suffice for the negative in producing the second figure. But these corollaries are false. So the truth is that whenever the exhaustive opposition is verified between two affirmatives, two negatives, or two different types, the production is verified. Otherwise, absence is possible, but the hypothesis prevents it.'
Source pages p. 706

Commentary
This passage is a critical note (tanbih) refuting a condition imposed by some scholars regarding the exhaustive conditional syllogism (mani'at al-khuluw). The condition claimed that for such a syllogism to produce a valid conclusion, the two terms of the disjunction must both be negative, or one negative and one affirmative. The author declares this condition incorrect and without evidence, demonstrating that the logical mechanism (the impossibility of both being false) operates even when both terms are affirmative. He provides an example from everyday life: 'Either Zaid is in the sea, or he is safe from drowning.' Both terms are affirmative in form, yet the exhaustive condition holds because it is impossible for Zaid to be neither in the sea nor safe from drowning (if he were not in the sea, he would necessarily be safe from drowning). Thus, negating one term ('he is not in the sea') produces the other ('he is safe from drowning'), and vice versa. The accompanying commentary (hashiya) clarifies that the stipulator was al-Amidi, as transmitted by al-Hilali, and the objector is al-Shaykh al-Hilali. It then addresses a potential objection: that 'safe from drowning' is a negative concept (absence of drowning) and therefore effectively negative. The response argues that a negative concept as a predicate is not equivalent to a negative proposition; it is an affirmative proposition with a negative predicate, which behaves differently in logical conversion and syllogistic figures. For instance, if such propositions were equivalent to negatives, they would convert straightforwardly as universal affirmatives (which they do not) and would suffice for the second figure (which they do not). Therefore, the correct position is that exhaustive opposition—whether between two affirmatives, two negatives, or mixed—yields productive syllogisms. The condition is thus refuted.

Types of Conditional Disjunctions and Their Conclusions
Author classifies conditional types by their conclusions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a conditional premise is shartiyya haqiqiyya, man'at al-jam', or man'at al-khuluw, and determine which conclusions it yields in a conditional syllogism.

Core Idea
Not all exclusive disjunctions are equal: each type yields a specific subset of conclusions.

In conditional syllogisms (qiyas istithna'i), the type of conditional premise determines which conclusions logically follow. The author classifies three types: the real conditional, the exclusive disjunction that prevents conjunction, and the exclusive disjunction that prevents void. Understanding this classification is crucial for correctly applying conditional reasoning in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the Three Types
Review the definitions: real conditional (shartiyya haqiqiyya) is true when exactly one part is true; man'at al-jam' prevents conjunction (cannot both be true, may both be false); man'at al-khuluw prevents void (cannot both be false, may both be true).
Memorize the truth conditions for each type.
2
Understand the Four Possible Conclusions
In a conditional syllogism, there are four conclusions: (1) posit A → negate B, (2) posit B → negate A, (3) negate A → posit B, (4) negate B → posit A.
These correspond to affirming one part or denying one part.
3
Map Conclusions to Types
Real conditional yields all four. Man'at al-jam' yields only the first two (positing one negates the other). Man'at al-khuluw yields only the last two (negating one posits the other).
Why? Because man'at al-jam' allows both false, so negating one does not imply the other; man'at al-khuluw allows both true, so positing one does not negate the other.
4
Apply with a Fiqh Example
Consider the conditional 'Either this transaction is valid or it is invalid.' Since validity and invalidity cannot both be true nor both false, this is a real conditional. Therefore all four conclusions are valid. Test with positing validity → infer invalidity, etc.
Use concrete Islamic examples to internalize.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَتَحَصَّلَ أَنَّ الَّذِي شَرْطِيَّتُهُ حَقِيقِيَّةٌ يُنْتِجُ أَرْبَعَ نَتَائِجَ ثِنْتَانِ فِي وَضْعِ أَحَدِ الطَّرَفَيْنِ وَثِنْتَانِ فِي رَفْعِهِ، وَالَّذِي شَرْطِيَّتُهُ مَانِعَةُ جَمْعِ يُنْتِجُ الأَوَّلَيْنِ مِنْ نَتَائِحِ الحَقِيقِيَّةِ، وَالَّذِي شَرْطِيَّتُهُ مَانِعَةُ خُلُوِّ يُنْتِجُ الأَخِيرَتَيْنِ، وَاللهُ تَعَالَى المُوَفِّقُ .
It is established that the conditional which is real produces four conclusions: two in positing one of the two parts and two in removing it. The conditional which is man'at al-jam' produces the first two of the conclusions of the real one. The conditional which is man'at al-khuluw produces the last two. And Allah Almighty is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 706

Commentary
This passage is a classification of conditional propositions according to their type and the conclusions they yield in a conditional syllogism (qiyas istithna'i). The author states that a real conditional (shartiyya haqiqiyya) — which is true when not both parts are true together nor both false together but allows one true and one false — produces four possible conclusions: affirmation of one part when the other is posited versus removed, and negation of one part when the other is posited versus removed. The exclusive disjunction that prevents conjunction (man'at al-jam') yields only the first two conclusions (positing one part to infer the negation of the other), because it allows both to be false simultaneously. The exclusive disjunction that prevents void (man'at al-khuluw) yields only the last two conclusions (negating one part to infer the affirmation of the other), because it allows both to be true simultaneously. This classification is foundational for determining the validity of conclusions in conditional syllogisms. For example, in a real disjunction like "Either Zaid is at home or he is at the market," if we posit one we negate the other, etc.

Refuting a Condition on Man'at al-Khuluw Production
Author refutes a condition that both parts must be negative for man'at al-khuluw to produce.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate whether a condition on the parts of man'at al-khuluw is necessary for its syllogistic production.

Core Idea
Man'at al-khuluw produces regardless of affirmative or negative parts.

Some logicians imposed a condition that the two disjuncts of man'at al-khuluw must be negative or mixed for the syllogism to be productive. The author rejects this as neither correct nor provable. He argues that the true cause of production – the impossibility of both parts being false – remains even when both disjuncts are affirmative, and provides a concrete example to demonstrate that the syllogism works identically.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition being refuted
Find the condition that some scholars placed on man'at al-khuluw for it to be productive. It is mentioned at the beginning of the passage: the two parts must be both negative, or one negative and the other affirmative.
The author says this condition is without proof.
2
Extract the author's counterexample
The author gives the example 'Either Zaid is in the sea or he is safe from drowning.' Note that both disjuncts are affirmative propositions. Check that this is indeed a man'at al-khuluw – it is impossible for both to be false because if Zaid is not in the sea he drowns, so he must be safe otherwise? Actually the example: if not in sea then safe? Wait, the logical structure: it's impossible for both 'Zaid is in the sea' and 'Zaid is safe from drowning' to be false because if he's not in the sea, he's safe? That's not logically necessary. But the author assumes the impossibility of both false. Accept the example as given for the sake of the argument.
The example shows two affirmatives, violating the condition.
3
Analyze the syllogistic production
The author shows that if we negate one part, we can conclude the other. 'If we say: But he is not in the sea, it produces: He is safe from drowning.' Similarly, if we negate the other, we conclude the first. This is the standard inference for man'at al-khuluw: deny one, assert the other.
Thus the condition is unnecessary because the production works even with both affirmatives.
4
Identify the cause of production
The author explicitly states the proof: 'the impossibility of both being false; so whenever one is negated the other is true.' This is the 'illat al-intaj – the reason that makes the inference valid.
This cause does not depend on the quality (affirmative/negative) of the parts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تنبية: اشْتَرَطَ بَعْضُهُمْ لِإِنْتاجِ مَانِعَةِ الخُلُوِّ أَنْ يَكُونَ طَرَفَاهَا سَالِبَتَيْنِ، أَوْ أَحَدُهُمَا سَالِباً وَالآخَرُ مُوجَباً، وَهُوَ غَيْرُ صَحِيحٍ، وَلَا دَلِيلَ عَلَيْهِ، وَعِلَّةُ الإِنْتَاجِ قَائِمَةٌ مَعَ كَوْنِهِمَا إِيْجَابِتَيْنِ؛ نَحْوُ : «إِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ زَيْدٌ فِي البَحْرِ، وَإِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ سَالِماً مِنَ الغَرَقِ»، فَإِذَا قُلْنَا : لَكِنَّهُ لَيْسَ فِي البَحْرِ أَنْتَجَ : هُوَ سَالِمٌ مِنَ الغَرَقِ»، فَإِذَا قُلْنَا : «لَكِنَّهُ لَيْسَ سَالِماً مِنَ الغَرَقِ» أَنْتَجَ : هُوَ فِي البَحْرِ، وَبُرْهَانُ ذَلِكَ امْتِنَاعُ ارْتِفَاعِهِمَا، فَمَتَى رُفِعَ أَحَدُهُمَا صَدَقَ الآخَرُ .
A note: Some have conditioned for the production of man'at al-khuluw that its two parts be both negative, or one negative and the other affirmative. That is not correct, and there is no proof for it. The cause of production exists even when they are both affirmative, such as 'Either Zaid is in the sea or he is safe from drowning.' If we say: 'But he is not in the sea,' it produces: 'He is safe from drowning.' If we say: 'But he is not safe from drowning,' it produces: 'He is in the sea.' The proof of that is the impossibility of both being false; so whenever one is negated the other is true.
Source pages p. 706

Commentary
This passage is a critical note (tanbih) refuting a condition imposed by some scholars on the exclusive disjunction that prevents void (man'at al-khuluw). The condition is that both parts must be negative or one negative and the other affirmative for the syllogism to produce a conclusion. The author declares this incorrect and without proof. He argues that the cause of production—the impossibility of both parts being false—remains even when both parts are affirmative. He provides an example: 'Either Zaid is in the sea or he is safe from drowning.' Both parts are affirmative. If we negate one (e.g., 'He is not in the sea'), we conclude the other ('He is safe from drowning'). If we negate the other, we conclude the first. This works because in man'at al-khuluw, the only impossibility is that both are false; thus if one is false, the other must be true. Hence the condition is unnecessary.

Condition for Particular Exclusive Disjunction
Author states that a particular exclusive disjunction requires an added condition for production.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a particular exclusive disjunction requires an added condition and construct the correct conclusion that preserves that condition.

Core Idea
A particular exclusive disjunction yields a conclusion only if its condition is preserved in the exception.

Exclusive disjunctions in logic can be universal or particular. A particular exclusive disjunction is restricted by a condition, and that condition must be carried into the conclusion for the inference to be valid. This lesson examines a note from the author on this subtle but crucial requirement, using a concrete example.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disjunction type
Notice that the disjunction is introduced with 'إما أن يكون هذا الجسم وهو حي عالماً أو جاهلاً' — it is a particular exclusive disjunction because it only applies to a body while it is alive. The phrase 'وهو حي' (while it is alive) is the condition (qayd) that restricts the scope.
Compare this to a universal exclusive disjunction that would apply to all bodies regardless of state.
2
Extract the disjuncts and the condition
The two disjuncts are 'knowing' ('ālim) and 'ignorant' (jāhil). The condition is 'alive' (ḥayy). The disjunction holds only for something that is alive; a dead body does not fall under either disjunct.
The condition is part of the subject term: 'this body, while it is alive'.
3
Test the inference without the condition
If we try to conclude simply 'knowing or ignorant' without including the condition, we commit an error. The author explains that a body as such (a dead body) may possess neither attribute, so the conclusion would be false.
The inference fails because the disjunction's scope is narrower than the conclusion's claim.
4
Construct the correct inference with the condition
To produce a valid conclusion, we must retain the condition in the exception (istithnā'). The correct form is: 'But it is a living knowing one' or 'But it is a living ignorant one'. The condition 'living' must appear in the predicate of the conclusion.
The condition modifies both disjuncts, so it must be repeated in the conclusion's predicate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تنبیهان الأوّل: المنفصلة المخصوصة لا تنتج إلا مع قيد استثنائيتها بما قيدت به؛ نحو: «إما أن يكون هذا الجسم وهو حي عالماً أو جاهلاً» فلا ينتج «عالماً أو جاهلاً» إلَّا مع قيد: «أَنَّه حي» فيقال مثلاً: «لكنه حي عالمٌ»، أو: «حيٌ جاهل»؛ لاحتمال أن لا يتّصف الجسم من حيث هو جسم بتلك الأوصاف.
Two notes: The first: The particular exclusive disjunction produces only with a condition on its exception according to what it was conditioned by. For example: 'Either this body, while it is alive, is knowing or ignorant' — so it does not produce 'knowing or ignorant' except with the condition 'that it is alive', so one says for instance: 'But it is a living knowing one' or 'a living ignorant one' because of the possibility that the body as such does not possess those attributes.
Source pages p. 707

Commentary
This passage is a note (tanbih) about a special case of exclusive disjunction — the 'particular exclusive disjunction' (al-munfasila al-mukhtaṣṣa). This is a disjunction that is not universal; it applies only under a specified condition. The author explains that such a disjunction does not yield a conclusion unless the condition (qayd) is included in the exception (istithna'). For example, if we say 'Either this body, while it is alive, is knowing or ignorant,' the disjunction holds only for a living body. Therefore, if we try to conclude solely 'knowing or ignorant' without the condition 'alive', the inference fails because a dead body may possess neither attribute. The correct inference is 'But it is a living knowing one' or 'But it is a living ignorant one,' thus preserving the condition. This illustrates that the scope of the disjuncts must be precisely maintained in the conclusion.

Key Terms
منفصلة مخصوصة
قيد

C) Because the exception must be negative
D) Because it is a real disjunction

Key Takeaway
A particular exclusive disjunction requires that its condition be included in the exception to yield a valid conclusion.

The Syllogism
intermediate
7 min
Conditions of Contradiction in Conditional Syllogisms
Author emphasizes that when negating a part, conditions of true contradiction must be met.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether an exception in a conditional syllogism is a true contradictory and assess the validity of the inference.

Core Idea
The exception must be the exact contradictory, including the same modality.

In conditional syllogisms (qiyas istithna'i), a valid inference requires that the exception (istithna') be the true contradictory of the part it negates. A mismatch in modality—such as using a possible negation when the part is absolute—destroys the inference. This lesson unpacks the logic and shows how to avoid this pitfall.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional and its parts
Start with the conditional statement (shartiyya). Separate the antecedent (muqaddam) and consequent (tali). In the example: 'Whenever man is an animal, he is mortal absolutely.' The consequent is 'man is mortal absolutely'.
The conditional here is a hypothetical universal (kullama).
2
Determine the modality of the part to be negated
Check the modality (jiha) of the part you want to negate. In the example, the consequent has absolute modality (بالإطلاق), meaning it is necessary and universal. The part is 'man is mortal' with absolute necessity.
Modality can be absolute, necessary, possible, etc.
3
State the required contradictory
The true contradictory of the part must have the opposite truth value and the same modality. So for 'man is mortal absolutely', the contradictory is 'man is not mortal absolutely' (not just 'possibly not').
This ensures the exception refers precisely to that part (rajul ila dhalik al-taraf).
4
Check the given exception against the required contradictory
If the exception given is 'not mortal possibly' (بالامكان), it is not the contradictory. It is compatible with the absolute statement (since absolute does not exclude possibility). Therefore, no conclusion follows.
The condition of contradiction is violated in modality.
5
Distinguish formal validity from material truth
If the exception were the correct contradictory (e.g., 'not mortal absolutely'), the syllogistic form would be valid. A false conclusion would then be due to material falsity (the exception being false in reality), not a formal error.
Formal validity does not guarantee truth of premises.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثَّاني: يجب عند استثناء نقيض طرف الشَّرطيَّة أن تعتبر شروط التناقض؛ ليتحقق كون الاستثنائية راجعة لذلك الطَّرف، حتّى إذا كانت جهة الطرف الإطلاق، وأريد رفعه، فلا بد أن تكون الاستثنائية دائمة، وإلا لم يحصل الإنتاج؛ كقولنا: كلَّما كان الإنسان حيواناً كان ميتاً بالإطلاق، لكنَّه ليس بميت بالإطلاق أو بالإمكان فالقضيَّتان صادقتان، والنتيجة وهي: اليس الإنسان بحيوان كاذبة، فلو أخذنا الاستثنائية دائمة، كانت صورة القياس صحيحة، وإنما تكذب النتيجة معها؛ لفساد المادة بكذب الاستثنائية .
The second: It is necessary when excluding the contradictory of a part of the conditional to consider the conditions of contradiction, so that it is verified that the exception refers to that part. For example, if the modality of the part is absolute, and one intends to negate it, then the exception must be necessary (always), otherwise production does not occur. Like our saying: 'Whenever man is an animal, he is mortal absolutely,' but 'He is not mortal absolutely or possibly' — the two propositions are true, and the result, namely 'Man is not an animal,' is false. If we took the exception as necessary, the form of the syllogism would be correct, but the result is false only due to corruption of matter due to the falsity of the exception.
Source pages p. 707

Commentary
This passage is a second note (tanbih) concerning the necessity of observing the conditions of true contradiction (shurut al-tanaquḍ) when one negates a part in a conditional syllogism. The author explains that the exception (istithna') must truly be the contradictory of the part, meaning it must have the opposite truth value in all cases. Specifically, if the part is absolute (unmodalized), its negation must also be absolute to maintain the contradictory relationship. If the exception is only modal (e.g., 'possibly not'), then it is not a true contradictory, and the syllogism fails. An example is given: Suppose we have the true conditional 'Whenever man is an animal, he is mortal with absolute necessity.' If we then say 'but he is not mortal absolutely' (i.e., a necessary negation), that is false because it contradicts the conditional. But if we take the exception as 'not mortal possibly,' that does not conflict with the conditional's absolute necessity; therefore no conclusion follows. To get a valid inference, the exception must be the exact contradictory (i.e., 'always not'). The author notes that if we use a necessary (always) exception, the syllogistic form is correct, but if the conclusion is false it is due to a material error (the exception being false), not formal fallacy.

Key Terms
شروط التناقض
جهة الإطلاق
استثنائية دائمة
فساد المادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the exception is false
B) Because it is not a true contradictory of the part
C) Because the conditional is false
D) Because the exception must be particular

Key Takeaway
In conditional syllogisms, the exception must be the exact contradictory of the part, including modality, for the inference to be valid.

First Warning: Restriction in Exclusive Disjunctive
Author warns that the exclusive disjunctive needs explicit restriction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when an exclusive disjunctive syllogism requires an explicit restriction and apply the condition correctly.

Core Idea
An exclusive disjunctive without its restriction yields no logical conclusion.

In this lesson, we examine the first of two warnings from the author about the proper use of the exclusive disjunctive syllogism (al-munfasila al-makhsusa). The key insight is that the disjunction must be explicitly restricted to a specific context; otherwise, the conclusion does not follow. This warning prevents premature inference when the terms are not truly exhaustive under all conditions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the exclusive disjunctive proposition
Find the 'either...or' statement in the passage. Note that it includes a restriction: 'while it is living' (وهو حي).
2
Check if the restriction is carried into the conclusion
The author says that without the restriction, the conclusion 'knowing or ignorant' does not follow, because the body as such might not have those attributes.
3
Apply the test: try to infer without restriction
Ask: if we remove 'while it is living', does the conclusion still hold? The answer is no, because a dead body is not knowing or ignorant.
4
Formulate the correct syllogism
The valid form includes the restriction in both premises and conclusion: e.g., 'But it is a living knower' or 'a living ignorant'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الاستثنائي
تنبیهان
الأوّل: المنفصلة المخصوصة لا تنتج إلا مع قيد استثنائيتها بما قيدت به؛ نحو: «إما أن يكون هذا الجسم وهو حي عالماً أو جاهلاً»، فلا ينتج: «عالماً أو جاهلاً»، إلا مع قيد: «أنه حي»، فيقال مثلاً: «لكنه حي عالم»، أو: «حي جاهل»؛ لاحتمال أن لا يتصف الجسم من حيث هو جسم بتلك الأوصاف.
الثاني: يجب عند استثناء نقيض طرف الشرطية أن تعتبر شروط التناقض؛ ليتحقق كون الاستثنائية راجعة لذلك الطرف، حتى إذا كانت جهة الطرف الإطلاق، وأريد رفعه، فلا بد أن تكون الاستثنائية دائمة، وإلا لم يحصل الإنتاج؛ كقولنا: «كلما كان الإنسان حيواناً كان ميتاً بالإطلاق، لكنه ليس بميت بالإطلاق أو بالإمكان» فالقضيتان صادقتان، والنتيجة وهي: «ليس الإنسان بحيوان» كاذبة، فلو أخذنا الاستثنائية دائمة، كانت صورة القياس صحيحة، وإنما تكذب النتيجة معها؛ لفساد المادة بكذب الاستثنائية.
Section on the conditional [syllogism]. Two warnings. The first: The exclusive disjunctive [proposition] does not yield a conclusion except with the restriction of its being exclusive by what it was restricted by; for example: 'Either this body, while it is living, is knowing or ignorant', so it does not yield 'knowing or ignorant' except with the restriction 'that it is living', so one says for example: 'But it is a living knower' or 'a living ignorant'; because of the possibility that the body as such is not described by those attributes. The second: When excepting the contradictory of a part of the conditional [proposition], it is necessary to consider the conditions of contradiction, so that it is verified that the exceptive [proposition] refers back to that part. So if the modality of the part is absolute, and one intends to negate it, then the exceptive [proposition] must be necessary, otherwise production does not occur; as in our saying: 'Whenever man is an animal, he is mortal absolutely, but he is not mortal absolutely or possibly', so the two propositions are true, and the conclusion, which is 'Man is not an animal' is false. If we take the exceptive as necessary, then the form of the syllogism would be correct, but the conclusion is false with it only because of the corruption of the matter by the falsity of the exceptive.
Source pages p. 707

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the exclusive disjunctive syllogism (al-munfasila al-makhsusa). It presents two warnings (tanbihat) about its proper use. The first warning: An exclusive disjunctive does not yield a conclusion unless the exclusive condition is explicitly stated in the premise. For example, if we say 'Either this body, while it is living, is knowing or ignorant', we cannot directly conclude 'It is knowing or ignorant' without specifying the restriction 'while it is living', because the body itself, without the qualification, might not have those attributes. The second warning: When we perform an exceptive syllogism (istithna') by negating the contradictory of one part of a conditional, we must ensure that the conditions of contradiction are satisfied. Specifically, if the original part has an absolute modality, the negation must be necessary (daima) to produce the contrapositive; otherwise, the conclusion may be false. He illustrates with an example: 'Whenever man is an animal, he is mortal absolutely' – if we try to negate the consequent absolutely, we get a false conclusion because the premise is not necessary. But if we make the exceptive necessary, the form is valid but the matter is false. These warnings guard against mistaking the scope and modality of conditional syllogisms.

Key Terms
منفصلة مخصوصة
استثنائية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the body as such is always knowing or ignorant.
B) Because the restriction 'while it is living' is necessary to ensure the attributes apply to this particular body.
C) Because the disjunctive is not exclusive.
D) Because the conclusion is already known.

Key Takeaway
An exclusive disjunctive syllogism is only valid when the restriction is explicitly preserved in the conclusion.

Second Warning: Conditions of Contradiction and Modality
Author warns about modality conditions in exceptive syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine when an exceptive syllogism requires the exceptive premise to be necessary rather than absolute, given the modality of the conditional parts.

Core Idea
Modality matters: negating an absolute statement needs a necessary negation to avoid fallacious conclusions.

The second warning addresses a subtle point: when we use an exceptive syllogism (istithna') to negate one part of a conditional, we must ensure that the negation satisfies the conditions of contradiction. In particular, if the original conditional part has an absolute modality, the exceptive must be necessary (daimah) to produce a valid conclusion. Otherwise, the conclusion may be false even if the premises seem true.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional premise and its modality
In the example: 'Whenever man is an animal, he is mortal absolutely' – note the absolute modality (بالإطلاق).
2
Formulate the exceptive premise with absolute negation
The exceptive says: 'But he is not mortal absolutely or possibly' – this is not a necessary negation; it is absolute or possible.
3
Observe the false conclusion
The conclusion 'Man is not an animal' is false, even though both premises are true. This shows the syllogism is invalid.
4
Apply the correction: make exceptive necessary
If we change the exceptive to 'But he is not mortal necessarily' (i.e., necessary negation), the form becomes valid, but the matter is false because the exceptive would be false. The warning is about modal mismatch.

The Syllogism

Understanding the Compound Syllogism as a Chain of Arguments
Author defines the compound syllogism as a chain of syllogisms linked by common conclusions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the structure of a compound syllogism and distinguish it from a simple syllogism.

Core Idea
A compound syllogism is a chain of linked arguments, not a single inference.

The compound syllogism (al-qiyas al-murakkab) is one of the appendices of the syllogism, mentioned after the simple syllogism because it builds upon it. While a simple syllogism reaches a conclusion from two premises, a compound syllogism uses multiple premises arranged in a sequence: the conclusion of one syllogism becomes a premise for the next, forming a chain that ends with the desired conclusion. This passage from al-Bajuri's commentary on al-Sullam defines the compound syllogism and explains why this structure is necessary—because premises often require further proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the genus and differentia
The compound syllogism is a type of syllogism (genus). Its differentia is that it is composed of multiple simple syllogisms linked together, where the conclusion of one becomes a premise of the next. Find how the commentary describes this chain.
The definition explicitly states: 'two premises produce a conclusion, then that conclusion with another premise produces another conclusion, and so on.'
2
Recognize the linking mechanism
Notice the role of intermediate conclusions: they are both conclusions of earlier syllogisms and premises for later ones. This creates a series of inferences, not just one.
The poem says: 'waqlib natijatan bihi muqaddimahu' – make a conclusion into a premise.
3
Understand the need for a chain
The commentary explains that two premises (or one of them) require proof (kabs) from what follows. This means that in a compound syllogism, the premises themselves are not self-evident but are established through previous syllogisms.
This explains why we cannot always use a simple syllogism; sometimes we need a sequence of proofs.
4
Distinguish simple vs. compound
A simple syllogism has exactly two premises leading to one conclusion. A compound syllogism has more than two premises arranged in a chain. The key distinction is whether the conclusion of the first serves as a premise in the second.
The passage mentions 'lawahiq al-qiyas' – appendices – indicating the compound syllogism is an extension, not the core.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمِنْهُ مَا يَدْعُونَهُ مُرَكَّبَا * لِكَوْنِهِ مِنْ حُجَجٍ قَدْ رُكَّبَا
فَرَكِّبَنْهُ إِنْ تُرِدْ أَنْ تَعْلَمَهُ * وَاقْلِبْ نَتِيجَةً بِهِ مُقَدِّمَهُ
يَلْزَمُ مِنْ تَرْكِيبِهَا بِأُخْرَى * نَتِيجَةٌ إِلَى هَلُمَّ جَرَا
مُتَّصِلَ النَّتَائِجِ الَّذِي حَوَى * يَكُونُ أَوْ مَنْصُولَهَا كُلَّ سَوَا
اعْلَمْ أَنَّ لَوَاحِقَ القِيَاسِ هِيَ الأُمُورُ الَّتِي تَلْحَقُ بِهِ وَتُذْكَرُ بَعْدَهُ لِمُنَاسَبَةٍ بَيْنَهُ وَبَيْنَهَا وَهِيَ هُنَا ثَلَاثَةٌ: الأَوَّلُ: القِيَاسُ المُرَكَّبُ، وَهُوَ قِيَاسٌ مُؤَلَّفٌ مِنْ مُقَدِّمَاتٍ تَنْتُجُ مُقَدِّمَتَانِ مِنْهَا نَتِيجَةً، وَتِلْكَ النَّتِيجَةُ مَعَ مُقَدِّمَةٍ أُخْرَى تُنْتِجُ نَتِيجَةً أُخْرَى، وَهَكَذَا إِلَى أَنْ يَحْصَلَ المَطْلُوبُ، وَذَلِكَ لِافْتِقَارِ مُقَدِّمَتَيْ كُلِّ قِيَاسٍ أَوْ إِحْدَاهُمَا إِلَى الكَسْبِ بِمَا بَعْدَهُ.
And among it is what they call 'compound' because it is composed of arguments that are composed. So compose it if you wish to know it, and make its premise into a conclusion. From combining it with another, a conclusion follows, and so on. It is connected conclusions that contain [the series] and it is either their compound entirely. Know that the appendices of the syllogism are the matters that attach to it and are mentioned after it due to a connection between it and them. They here are three: the first is the compound syllogism, and it is a syllogism composed of premises, two of which produce a conclusion, and that conclusion with another premise produces another conclusion, and so on until the desired [conclusion] is obtained. That is because the two premises of each syllogism, or one of them, are in need of being acquired by what follows it.
Source pages p. 708

Commentary
This passage defines the compound syllogism (al-qiyas al-murakkab), which is one of the appendices of the syllogism (lawahiq al-qiyas). The poem (matn) introduces it concisely: it is a composition of multiple arguments (hujaj) linked together. To construct it, one takes a proposition and treats it as a conclusion from one syllogism, then uses that conclusion as a premise in another, and so on, building a chain. The commentary expands: the appendices are matters that attach to the syllogism due to a relation—here the compound syllogism is appended because it is composed of simple syllogisms. The compound syllogism is defined as a series of syllogisms: the first two premises yield a conclusion, that conclusion is combined with a new premise to yield another conclusion, and so on until the final goal is reached. This need arises because the premises of each simple syllogism, or at least one of them, require a proof (kabs) from another syllogism. The definition distinguishes the compound syllogism from a simple one: it is not a single inference but a chain of inferences.

Key Terms
القياس المركب

Checkpoint
What is the defining characteristic of a compound syllogism according to this passage?
A) It has more than two premises in one inference.
B) Its conclusion is the negation of the antecedent.
C) It consists of a chain of syllogisms where each conclusion serves as a premise for the next.
D) It is a valid syllogism in the second figure.

Key Takeaway
The compound syllogism is a chain of simple syllogisms where intermediate conclusions serve as premises for subsequent inferences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the following statements and arrange them into a compound syllogism: (a) All intoxicants are forbidden (haram). (b) Nabidh is an intoxicant. (c) Therefore nabidh is forbidden. This is a simple syllogism. Now add: (d) Every drink that causes intoxication is an intoxicant. (e) This new drink causes intoxication. Construct a chain that ends with the conclusion 'This new drink is forbidden.' Identify which statement serves as an intermediate conclusion.
?
Read the following proof for the createdness of the Quran: (1) The Quran is composed of speech sounds and letters. (2) Everything composed of speech sounds and letters is temporally originated. (3) Therefore the Quran is temporally originated. (4) Everything temporally originated is created. (5) Therefore the Quran is created. Is this a compound syllogism? Explain why or why not, identifying the intermediate conclusion(s).

The Syllogism
beginner
8 min
What is a Composite Syllogism?
Author defines composite syllogism as one of the appendices of syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define a composite syllogism and identify its chain structure in a given argument.

Core Idea
A chain of multiple two-premise syllogisms leading to one final conclusion.

The composite syllogism is not a new type of inference but a tool to chain multiple syllogisms together. The passage defines it as an appendix of syllogism because it extends the basic rules. Understanding its structure helps you break down complex theological proofs into manageable steps.

Guided Path
1
Identify the final conclusion (al-maṭlūb).
Look at the end of the argument; the last statement is what the whole chain aims to prove. In the example, it is 'The world, its creator is Allah alone.'
2
Work backwards to find premises.
Each conclusion in the chain comes from two premises. Identify the immediate premises that directly lead to the final conclusion. Continue backwards until you reach premises that are accepted as true (e.g., self-evident or from the text).
3
Recognize each individual syllogism.
Each pair of premises + their conclusion forms a complete categorical syllogism. For example: 'The world is changing; every changing thing is originated; therefore the world is originated.' That is one syllogism.
4
Trace the progression chain.
Observe how the conclusion of the first syllogism becomes a premise in the second, and so on. This linking is what makes it composite (murakkab).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لَوَاحِقُ القِياسِ
(١٠٧) وَمِنْهُ مَا يَدْعُونَهُ: «مُرَكَّبَا لِكَوْنِهِ مِنْ حُجَجٍ قَدْ رُكَّبَا (١٠٨) فَرَكْبَنْهُ إِنْ تُرِدْ أَنْ تَعْلَمَهُ وَاقْلِبْ نَتِ وَاقْلِبْ نَتِيجَةٌ بِهِ مُقَدِّمَهُ (١٠٩) يَلْزَمُ مِنْ تَرْكِيبِهَا بِأُخْرَى نَتِيجَةٌ إِلَى هَلُمَّ جَرًا (١١٠) مُتَّصِلَ النَّتَائِج الَّذِي حَوَى يَكُونُ، أَوْ مَنْصُولَها كُلِّ سَوَا
اعْلَمْ أَنَّ لَوَاحِقَ القِيَاسِ هِيَ : الأُمُورُ الَّتِي تَلْحَقُ بِهِ، وَتُذْكَرُ بَعْدَهُ؛ لِمُنَاسَبَةٍ بَيْنَهُ وَبَيْنَهَا ، وَهِيَ هُنَا ثَلَاثَةٌ :
(١) - الأَوَّلُ : القِيَاسُ المُرَكَّبُ، وَهُوَ: قِيَاسُ مُؤلَّفٌ مِنْ مُقَدِّمَاتٍ تَنْتُجُ مُقَدِّمَتَانِ مِنْهَا نَتِيجَةٌ ، وَتِلْكَ النَّتِيجَةُ مَعَ مُقَدِّمَةٍ أُخْرَى تُنْتِجُ نَتِيجَةً أُخْرَى ، ... وَهَكَذَا إِلَى أَنْ يَحْصَلَ المَطْلُوبُ، وَذَلِكَ لِافْتِقَارِ مُقَدِّمَتَي كُلِّ قِيَاسٍ أَوْ إِحْدَاهُمَا إِلَى الكَسْبِ بِمَا بَعْدَهُ إِلَى أَنْ يَنْتَهِيَ إِلَى الضَّرُورَةِ كَمَا تَقَدَّمَ فِي قَوْلِهِ : وَتَنْتَهِي إِلَى ضَرُورَةٍ . . . البَيْتَ ، فَتُكُونُ هُنَا حُجَجُ ، أَيْ: أَفْيسَةٌ مُتَرَتِّبَةٌ مُحَصَّلَةٌ لِلقِيَاسِ المُنتِج لِلمَطْلُوبِ.
وَسَمُّوا مَجْمُوعَ تِلْكَ الأَقْيسَةِ : قياساً مُرَكَّباً، وَجَعَلُوهُ مِنْ لَوَاحِقِ القِيَاسِ .
ثُمَّ إِنْ صُرِّحَ فِيهِ بِنَتَائِجِ تِلْكَ الأَقْسِسَةِ سُمِّيَ : مُتَّصِلَ النَّتَائِجِ؛ لِوَصْلِهَا بِالمُقَدِّمَاتِ، وَإِنْ لَمْ يُصَرَّحْ بِهَا سُمِّيَ : مَفْصُولَ النَّتَائِجِ؛ لِفَصْلِهَا عَنِ المُقَدِّمَاتِ فِي الذِّكْرِ، وَإِنْ كَانَتْ مُرَادَةً فِي المَعْنَى. مِثَالُ الأَوَّلِ : العَالَمُ مُتَغَيِّرُ [٦٢/١]، وَكُلُّ مُتَغَيِّرٍ حَادِثٌ، فَ: «العَالَمُ حَادِثُ»، ثُمَّ العَالَمُ حَادِثٌ، وَكُلُّ حَادِثٍ مُفْتَقِرٌ إِلَى مُحْدِثٍ فَ: «العَالَمُ مُفْتَقِرٌ إِلَى مُحْدِثٍ»، ثُمَّ العَالَمُ مُفْتَقِرٌ إِلَى مُحْدِثٍ، وَكُلُّ مُفْتَقِرٍ إِلَى مُحْدِثٍ فَخَالِقُهُ اللَّهُ تَعَالَى وَحْدَهُ [ج/ ٤٠] فَ : «العَالَمُ خَالِقُهُ اللهُ تَعَالَى وَحْدَهُ»، وَهُوَ المَطْلُوبُ.
وَمِثَالُ الثَّانِي: قَوْلُنَا فِي هَذَا المِثَالِ : «العَالَمُ مُتَغَيِّرٌ، وَكُلُّ مُتَغَيِّرٍ حَادِثٌ، وَكُلُّ حَادِثٍ يَفْتَقِرُ إِلَى مُحْدِثٍ، وَكُلُّ مُفْتَقِرٍ إِلَى مُحْدِثٍ فَخَالِقُهُ اللهُ تَعَالَى وَحْدَهُ»، يُنْتِجُ : العَالَمُ خَالِقُهُ اللهُ تَعَالَى وَحْدَهُ، وَهُوَ المَطْلُوبُ.
Appendices of the Syllogism. (107) And among it is what they call 'composite' because it is composed of arguments that have been combined. (108) So combine it if you wish to know it, and convert the conclusion and convert the conclusion with its premise. (109) From combining it with another, a conclusion necessarily follows, and so on. (110) What contains connected results is called 'connected results', or 'disconnected results' all the same. Know that the appendices of the syllogism are: the matters that attach to it and are mentioned after it due to a suitability between it and them. They are three here: (1) The first: the composite syllogism. It is a syllogism composed of premises, such that two premises from them produce a conclusion, and that conclusion with another premise produces another conclusion, and so on until the desired conclusion is obtained. This is because the two premises of each syllogism or one of them need acquisition by what comes after it until it ends at necessity, as previously stated in his saying 'and it ends at necessity...' the verse. So here there are arguments, i.e., ordered syllogisms that achieve the productive syllogism for the desired [conclusion]. They called the total of those syllogisms 'composite syllogism', and they made it from among the appendices of the syllogism. Then if its conclusions are explicitly mentioned in it, it is called 'connected results' due to connecting them with the premises; and if they are not explicitly mentioned, it is called 'disconnected results' due to separating them from the premises in mention, even though they are intended in meaning. Example of the first: The world is changing, and every changing thing is originated, so 'the world is originated'; then the world is originated, and every originated thing is in need of an originator, so 'the world is in need of an originator'; then the world is in need of an originator, and everything in need of an originator – its creator is Allah alone, so 'the world, its creator is Allah alone', which is the desired [conclusion]. Example of the second: Our saying in this example: 'The world is changing, and every changing thing is originated, and every originated thing is in need of an originator, and everything in need of an originator – its creator is Allah alone, produces: The world, its creator is Allah alone, which is the desired [conclusion].
Source pages p. 708

Commentary
This passage defines and classifies the composite syllogism (al-qiyās al-murakkab) as one of the appendices of syllogism. The author begins with the poetic matn (lines 107-110) stating that a composite syllogism is made of multiple arguments. The commentary then explains: the appendices of syllogism are matters that are attached to it and discussed after it due to a suitability; here there are three, and the first is the composite syllogism. The definition is given: it is a syllogism composed of premises such that two premises yield a conclusion, that conclusion then combined with another premise yields another conclusion, and so on until the desired conclusion is reached. The reason for composition is that the premises of each syllogism (or one of them) require acquisition from earlier premises until one reaches self-evident truths (ḍarūra). The total of these syllogisms is called a composite syllogism. Then follows a division: if intermediate conclusions are explicitly stated, it is called 'connected results' (muttasil al-natā'ij); if not, 'disconnected results' (mafṣūl al-natā'ij), though the intermediate conclusions are still intended implicitly. Two examples from kalām are given, both proving the existence of God from the contingency of the world. The first example explicitly states the intermediate conclusions ('the world is originated', 'the world is in need of an originator') in sequence; the second example omits them but the sequence is implied. This classification is important because it demonstrates how multiple syllogisms can be chained to reach a complex conclusion, and how the explicit mention of intermediate steps affects the structure.

Key Terms
القياس المركب
لواحق القياس
حُجَج مترتبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the primary reason a composite syllogism is needed?
A) To make arguments longer and more impressive.
B) Because the premises of each syllogism (or one of them) need acquisition from earlier steps until reaching self-evident truths.
C) Because a simple syllogism cannot produce certain conclusions.
D) To satisfy the rules of the first figure.

Key Takeaway
A composite syllogism is a chain of multiple two-premise syllogisms where intermediate conclusions link the steps from premises to final conclusion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a composite syllogism of at least two steps to prove that 'The Quran is the word of Allah'. Use premises from Islamic theology (e.g., miracles of inimitability, prophethood, etc.).
?
Take the following argument and break it into its component syllogisms: 'Alcohol is an intoxicant; every intoxicant is prohibited; therefore alcohol is prohibited. Every prohibited substance is sinful to consume; alcohol is prohibited; therefore alcohol is sinful to consume.'

The Syllogism
beginner
8 min
Two Types: Connected and Disconnected Results
Author divides composite syllogism into connected and disconnected results and gives examples from kalām.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between muttasil al-natā'ij and mafṣūl al-natā'ij composite syllogisms and transform one into the other.

Core Idea
The same chain of reasoning can be expressed with or without explicit intermediate conclusions.

Having understood the composite syllogism as a chain, the passage now divides it based on whether the intermediate conclusions are explicitly stated. This is a practical distinction: sometimes clarity demands that every step be spelled out; other times brevity is acceptable as long as the logical connection is understood. The same kalām proof is given in both forms, showing their equivalence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the intermediate conclusions in a composite syllogism.
In a chain, each step produces a conclusion that becomes a premise for the next. For the kalām proof, the intermediate conclusions are: 'The world is originated' and 'The world is in need of an originator'.
2
Check if those intermediate conclusions are written out between the premises.
In the muttasil form, they appear: e.g., after 'The world is changing; every changing thing is originated' we write 'so the world is originated' before moving to the next step. In the mafṣūl form, they are omitted: the premises are listed without the intermediate conclusions, but they are still intended.
3
Convert one form to the other.
To go from muttasil to mafṣūl, remove the explicit intermediate conclusion statements, keeping only the premises and the final conclusion. To go from mafṣūl to muttasil, insert the intermediate conclusions as separate sentences after each inference.
The logical structure remains identical; only the presentation changes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Commentary
This passage defines and classifies the composite syllogism (al-qiyās al-murakkab) as one of the appendices of syllogism. The author begins with the poetic matn (lines 107-110) stating that a composite syllogism is made of multiple arguments. The commentary then explains: the appendices of syllogism are matters that are attached to it and discussed after it due to a suitability; here there are three, and the first is the composite syllogism. The definition is given: it is a syllogism composed of premises such that two premises yield a conclusion, that conclusion then combined with another premise yields another conclusion, and so on until the desired conclusion is reached. The reason for composition is that the premises of each syllogism (or one of them) require acquisition from earlier premises until one reaches self-evident truths (ḍarūra). The total of these syllogisms is called a composite syllogism. Then follows a division: if intermediate conclusions are explicitly stated, it is called 'connected results' (muttasil al-natā'ij); if not, 'disconnected results' (mafṣūl al-natā'ij), though the intermediate conclusions are still intended implicitly. Two examples from kalām are given, both proving the existence of God from the contingency of the world. The first example explicitly states the intermediate conclusions ('the world is originated', 'the world is in need of an originator') in sequence; the second example omits them but the sequence is implied. This classification is important because it demonstrates how multiple syllogisms can be chained to reach a complex conclusion, and how the explicit mention of intermediate steps affects the structure.

Key Terms
متصل النتائج
مفصول النتائج

C) Muttasil has more premises than mafṣūl.

A composite syllogism can be expressed either with explicit intermediate conclusions (connected) or without them (disconnected), preserving the same logical structure.

The Syllogism
intermediate
10 min
Qiyas al-Khalf: Reductio as a Compound Syllogism
Author explains the reductio ad absurdum as a type of composite syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct a qiyās al-khalf argument and identify its two component syllogisms: the categorical (iqtirānī) and the conditional (istithnā'ī).

Core Idea
Reductio in Islamic logic is a compound of two syllogisms: a categorical chain and a conditional exception.

This passage from al-Bajuri's commentary on al-Sullam teaches one of the most powerful argument forms in Islamic logic: qiyās al-khalf (reductio ad absurdum). While it looks simple—prove something by showing its opposite leads to contradiction—the logicians realized it has a precise structure. The commentator al-Taftazani clarifies the settled view: it is composed of a categorical (iqtirānī) syllogism followed by a conditional (istithnā'ī) syllogism. Let us dissect this structure using the kalām example of proving God's pre-eternity (qidam).

Guided Path
1
Identify the desired conclusion (matalub) and its contradictory
In the example, the matalub is 'God is pre-eternal' (qadim). Its contradictory (naqīḍ) is 'God is not pre-eternal.' The reductio aims to prove the matalub by showing that assuming the naqīḍ leads to an absurdity.
2
Construct the categorical (iqtirānī) syllogism
This syllogism (or chain of reasoning) shows that if the naqīḍ holds, an impossible consequence follows. In the text, the categorical part is implicit: it establishes the conditional 'If God were not pre-eternal, then the world would not exist.' The reasoning is that the world's existence depends on God's pre-eternity.
The categorical syllogism is not written out; it is the underlying argument that produces the conditional.
3
Form the conditional linking naqīḍ to the absurdity
The result of the categorical reasoning is a conditional statement: 'If God were not pre-eternal, then the world would not exist.' This conditional is the intermediate conclusion that will serve as the major premise of the next syllogism.
4
Use the conditional as major premise of the conditional (istithnā'ī) syllogism
In the exceptive syllogism, you affirm the negation of the consequent (the impossible consequence is shown false). Here, the minor premise is 'But the world exists' (i.e., the consequent 'the world does not exist' is false). This is a modus tollens: denying the consequent to deny the antecedent.
The minor premise ('the world exists') is a necessary truth in the kalām context.
5
Derive the desired conclusion and verify
From the conditional 'If not-p then not-q' and the affirmation of q, the conclusion follows: 'Therefore, God is pre-eternal' (p). This matches the original matalub, confirming the validity of the reductio structure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة:
مِنَ المركَّب قياس الخلف - بضم الخاء، وفتحها ، وهو : «قياس يُقصد به إثبات المطلوب بإبطال نقيضه»، ولم يذكره الناظم هنا من لواحق القياس؛ لأنَّه نوعٌ مِنَ المركب، وذكره القادري، فقال:
وَالخُلْفٌ وَهُوَ عِنْدَهُمْ أَنْ تُبْطِلًا نَقِيضَ مَطْلُوبِكَ كَيْ لِيُحَصِّلَا
قال السَّعدُ : وقد وقع فيه اختلاف عظيمٌ ، والذي استقرَّ عليه رأي الشيخ أنَّه مركَّب من قياسين : أحدهما اقتراني، والآخر استثنائي، ومثاله فيما إذا كان المطلوب ثبوت القدم لمولانا جل وعزَّ : لو لم يكن الله تبارك وتعالى قديماً، لكان ليس قديماً، ولو كان ليس قديماً لم يوجد العالم فينتج : لو لم يكن الله تعالى قديماً لم يوجد العالم»، وهذه النتيجة متّصلة لزومية، تجعلها كبرى لقياس استثنائي، وتستثني نقيض تاليها، فتقول: «لو لم يكن الله تعالى قديماً لم يوجد العالم، لكنَّ العالم موجود ضرورةً ، فـ : الله تعالى قديم، وهو مطلوبنا .
Issue: Among the composite [syllogisms] is the syllogism of khalf (reductio) – with damm on the khā' and fatha on it – and it is: 'a syllogism by which one intends to prove the desired [conclusion] by refuting its contradictory'. The author (al-nāẓim) did not mention it here among the appendices of the syllogism because it is a type of composite [syllogism], and al-Qādirī mentioned it, saying: 'And khalf – according to them it is that you refute the contradictory of your desired [conclusion] so that it may be obtained.' Al-Sa'd said: There has occurred in it great disagreement, and what the opinion of the Shaykh settled upon is that it is composed of two syllogisms: one categorical and the other conditional. Its example is when the desired [conclusion] is the pre-eternity of our Maula (majestic and exalted): 'If God (blessed and exalted) were not pre-eternal, then He would not be pre-eternal, and if He were not pre-eternal, the world would not exist, so it results: If God were not pre-eternal, then the world would not exist.' This result is a connected conditional, you make it the major premise of a conditional syllogism, and you negate the consequent of it, so you say: 'If God were not pre-eternal, then the world would not exist; but the world exists necessarily, therefore God is pre-eternal,' and that is our desired [conclusion].
Source pages p. 709

Commentary
This passage discusses qiyās al-khalf (reductio ad absurdum) as a subtype of composite syllogism. The author notes that al-khalf is a syllogism that aims to prove a desired conclusion by refuting its contradictory. The matn (from al-Sullam) states that khalf is when you refute the contradictory of your desired conclusion to obtain it. The commentator al-Sa'd (al-Taftazani) is quoted: there is great disagreement on its nature, but the settled view of the shaykh (likely al-Razi or al-Abhari) is that it is composed of two syllogisms: one categorical (iqtirānī) and one conditional (istithnā'ī). An example is provided in the context of proving the pre-eternity (qidam) of God. The argument proceeds: assume the opposite (that God is not pre-eternal); that leads to a contradiction (the world would not exist), but the world does exist, so the assumption is false, and the conclusion (that God is pre-eternal) is established. The structure is: a conditional syllogism (if not-p then not-q) followed by a conditional syllogism (if not-q then not-r), yielding a chain, then an exceptive syllogism that denies the consequent to affirm the antecedent. This is a classic reductio argument used in kalām.

اقتراني

D) It provides the major premise for the final conditional syllogism.

Key Takeaway
Qiyās al-khalf is not a single syllogism but a compound of a categorical reasoning that yields a conditional and an exceptive conditional syllogism that completes the proof.

Compound Syllogism and Its Types
Defines compound syllogism and distinguishes muttasil and mafṣul types with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between muttasil al-nata'ij and mafṣul al-nata'ij in a compound syllogism and construct a compound syllogism from a theological proof.

Core Idea
A compound syllogism chains multiple syllogisms; intermediate conclusions may be stated or implied.

In this passage, the author defines the compound syllogism, a crucial tool in kalam for constructing multi-step proofs. You have learned simple syllogisms; now see how they are chained. The passage gives a classical kalam argument for God's existence, showing both explicit and implicit presentation of intermediate conclusions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of compound syllogism
Find the phrase 'مجموع تلك الأقيسة قياسا مركبا' – it is a collection of multiple syllogisms arranged to produce the final conclusion.
2
Note the two subtypes based on explicit mention
Look for 'متصل النتائج' and 'مفصول النتائج'. The first states intermediate conclusions explicitly; the second omits them in wording but intends them in meaning.
3
Examine the muttasil example
Trace the chain: العالم متغير → كل متغير حادث → العالم حادث → كل حادث مفتقر إلى محدث → العالم مفتقر إلى محدث → كل مفتقر إلى محدث فخالقه الله → العالم خالقه الله. Each intermediate conclusion is stated.
4
Examine the mafṣul example
Notice that the intermediate conclusions are omitted: only the premises (العالم متغير, كل متغير حادث, كل حادث يفتقر إلى محدث, كل مفتقر إلى محدث فخالقه الله) and the final conclusion are given.
The omitted conclusions are still logically present.
5
Compare the two forms
Understand that the difference is only in verbal expression; both are logically equivalent. Muttasil is clearer for teaching; mafṣul is more concise.

That it ends at necessity as previously mentioned in his statement: 'and it ends at necessity'... the verse, so here they are proofs, i.e., arranged syllogisms resulting in the syllogism that produces the desired conclusion. And they called the totality of those syllogisms: a 'compound syllogism', and they made it one of the appendices of syllogism. Then if its conclusions are explicitly stated in it, it is called: 'connected in its conclusions' because of their connection with the premises; and if they are not explicitly stated, it is called: 'disconnected in its conclusions' because of their separation from the premises in mention, even though they are intended in meaning. The example of the first: The world is changing, and everything changing is temporally originated, so the world is temporally originated; then the world is temporally originated, and everything temporally originated is in need of an originator, so the world is in need of an originator; then the world is in need of an originator, and everything in need of an originator, its creator is Allah Almighty alone, so the world's creator is Allah Almighty alone, and that is the desired conclusion. And the example of the second: our statement in this example: The world is changing, and everything changing is temporally originated, and everything temporally originated is in need of an originator, and everything in need of an originator, its creator is Allah Almighty alone, it produces: The world's creator is Allah Almighty alone, and that is the desired conclusion.

Commentary
This passage defines the compound syllogism (قياس مركب) and divides it into two types. The author begins by explaining that a compound syllogism consists of multiple syllogisms arranged to arrive at the final desired conclusion. Scholars call this arrangement a 'compound syllogism' and classify it among the appendices of syllogism. Then the author distinguishes two subtypes: if the intermediate conclusions are explicitly stated, it is called 'muttasil al-nata'ij' (connected in its conclusions) because the conclusions are linked with the premises; if the intermediate conclusions are omitted from explicit mention (though still intended in meaning), it is called 'mafṣul al-nata'ij' (disconnected in its conclusions) because they are not verbally stated. The passage provides a classical theological example: demonstrating that the world has a single Creator. The first example (muttasil) states each intermediate conclusion explicitly: the world is changing → everything changing is originated → the world is originated; the world is originated → everything originated needs an originator → the world needs an originator; the world needs an originator → everything needing an originator has Allah as its Creator → the world's Creator is Allah. The second example (mafṣul) omits the intermediate conclusions and states only the premises, directly concluding the final result. The example uses principles from Islamic kalam (theology) about the temporality of the world and the need for a creator, which is a standard Islamic argument for God's existence.

Key Terms
القياس المركب
متصل النتائج
مفصول النتائج

C) In muttasil the conclusion is certain; in mafṣul it is probabilistic.
D) In muttasil the syllogisms are in the first figure; in mafṣul they are in the second figure.

Key Takeaway
A compound syllogism chains multiple syllogisms; the intermediate conclusions may be stated (متصلة) or omitted (مفصولة) while still intended.

The Syllogism
intermediate
8 min
Qiyas al-Khalf: The Compound Reductio Syllogism
Author defines qiyas al-khalf as a compound syllogism for proving the desired by refuting its opposite.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct a qiyas al-khalf argument as a compound of a categorical syllogism followed by an exceptive conditional syllogism, using an example from Islamic theology.

Core Idea
Qiyas al-khalf proves a truth by showing its opposite leads to absurdity — a compound of categorical and conditional reasoning.

This lesson unpacks a powerful logical tool used extensively in kalām: proving a claim by demonstrating that its negation leads to an absurdity. The passage defines qiyas al-khalf as a compound syllogism, explains its two-step structure (categorical then conditional), and illustrates it with a classic proof for God's pre-eternity. You will learn to identify and construct these arguments.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition
Qiyas al-khalf is a syllogism intended to prove the desired conclusion by invalidating its opposite. The term 'khalf' can mean absurdity or 'behind', indicating indirect proof.
The passage notes disagreement about its nature, but the settled view is that it is compound.
2
Identify the two component syllogisms
First, a categorical syllogism produces a conditional conclusion (e.g., 'If not-p then not-q'). Second, an exceptive conditional syllogism uses that conclusion as a major premise, then excepts the opposite of the consequent to derive the desired truth.
The first syllogism is iqtirānī (categorical), the second istithnā'ī (exceptive).
3
Map the theological example
Desired conclusion: God is pre-eternal. The argument: (1) If God were not pre-eternal, He would not be pre-eternal (categorical); (2) If God were not pre-eternal, the world would not exist (categorical conclusion); (3) But the world exists; therefore God is pre-eternal (exceptive syllogism).
This example is from kalām discussions of God's attribute of qidam.
4
Recognize the rationale for the name
Four explanations: (1) it leads to absurdity if the opposite is assumed; (2) it proves indirectly, 'from behind'; (3) the arguer abandons his own proof to refute the opponent; (4) the absurd conclusion is 'thrown behind the back'.
These highlight the indirect nature of reductio arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة: من المركب قياس الخلف - بضم الخاء، وفتحها، وهو: قياس يقصد به إثبات المطلوب بإبطال نقيضه، ولم يذكره الناظم هنا من لواحق القياس؛ لأنه نوع من المركب، وذكره القادري، فقال: والخلف وهو عندهم أن تبطلًا نقيض مطلوبك كي ليحصلًا. قال السعد: وقد وقع فيه اختلاف عظيم، والذي استقر عليه رأي الشيخ أنه مركب من قياسين: أحدهما اقتراني، والآخر استثنائي، ومثاله فيما إذا كان المطلوب ثبوت القدم لمولانا جل وعز: لو لم يكن الله تبارك وتعالى قديماً، لكان ليس قديماً، ولو كان ليس قديماً لم يوجد العالم فينتج: لو لم يكن الله تعالى قديماً لم يوجد العالم، وهذه النتيجة متصلة لزومية، تجعلها كبرى لقياس استثنائي، وتستثني نقيض تاليها، فتقول: لو لم يكن الله تعالى قديماً لم يوجد العالم، لكن العالم موجود ضرورةً، فالله تعالى قديم، وهو مطلوبنا. ولو اختصرنا لقلنا: لو لم يكن الله تعالى قديماً لكان ليس بقديم ولو كان ليس بقديم لم يوجد العالم، لكن العالم وجد، فكونه تعالى ليس بقديم باطل، وكونه قديماً حقٌ. وسمي: قياس الخلف؛ إما لأنه يؤول إلى الخلف؛ أي: الباطل على تقدير عدم حقيقة المطلوب، وإما لأنه يأتي المطلوب لا على استقامة، بل من خلفه؛ أي: من ورائه، وقيل: لأن المستدل يترك حجته خلف ظهره، ويعمد إلى قول خصمه فيبطله، وقيل: لأن نتيجته مما تنبذ خلف الظهر لبطلانها.
Issue: Among the compound [syllogisms] is qiyas al-khalf – with damma of the khā' and its fatha – and it is: a syllogism intended to prove the desired conclusion by invalidating its opposite. The author (al-nāẓim) did not mention it here among the appendices of syllogism because it is a type of compound [syllogism], and al-Qādirī mentioned it, saying: 'And al-khalf, according to them, is that you invalidate the opposite of your desired conclusion so that you may obtain it.' Al-Sa'd said: 'There has been a great disagreement about it, and what the Shaykh's opinion settled upon is that it is composed of two syllogisms: one categorical and the other conditional. Its example is when the desired conclusion is the establishment of pre-eternity for our Master, Glorified and Exalted: If God, Blessed and Exalted, were not pre-eternal, then He would be not pre-eternal; and if He were not pre-eternal, the world would not exist. This yields: If God Almighty were not pre-eternal, the world would not exist. This result is a connected, necessary conditional, which you make the major premise of a conditional syllogism, and you except the opposite of its consequent, saying: If God Almighty were not pre-eternal, the world would not exist; but the world does exist necessarily; therefore, God Almighty is pre-eternal, and that is our desired conclusion.' And if we simplified, we would say: If God Almighty were not pre-eternal, He would be not pre-eternal; and if He were not pre-eternal, the world would not exist; but the world does exist; therefore, His not being pre-eternal is false, and His being pre-eternal is true. It is called 'qiyas al-khalf' either because it leads to the absurdity (al-khalf), i.e., falsehood, on the assumption that the reality of the desired conclusion is not the case; or because it arrives at the desired conclusion not straightforwardly but from behind it (i.e., from its rear); or because the arguer leaves his proof behind his back and proceeds to his opponent's statement and invalidates it; or because its conclusion is something cast behind the back due to its falsehood.
Source pages p. 709

Commentary
This passage defines qiyas al-khalf (reductio ad absurdum) as a type of compound syllogism. The author notes that it was not mentioned by the poet (al-nāẓim) in his matn because it falls under compound syllogisms, but al-Qādirī mentioned it in his commentary. The passage then cites al-Sa'd, who explains that there was great disagreement about this kind of syllogism, but the settled opinion is that it comprises two syllogisms: one categorical (iqtirānī) and one conditional (istithnā'ī). The example given is a theological proof for God's pre-eternity (qidam). The first syllogism (categorical) is: If God were not pre-eternal, then He would be not pre-eternal; and if He were not pre-eternal, the world would not exist. This yields the conditional conclusion: If God were not pre-eternal, the world would not exist. This conditional then becomes the major premise of a second syllogism (conditional, specifically an exceptive syllogism): [If God were not pre-eternal, the world would not exist]; but the world does exist (exception of the opposite of the consequent); therefore, God is pre-eternal. The passage then gives a simplified version. Finally, it provides four explanations for why this syllogism is called 'khalf' (literally 'behind' or 'absurdity'): (1) it leads to an absurdity if the desired conclusion is denied; (2) it proves the conclusion indirectly, from behind; (3) the arguer abandons his own proof and refutes the opponent's claim; (4) the conclusion (of the reductio) is thrown behind the back because it is false. This illustrates the logical structure of reductio arguments common in Islamic theology, such as proving God's attributes by refuting their opposites.

Key Terms
قياس الخلف
الخلف
اقتراني
استثنائي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the example of qiyas al-khalf for God's pre-eternity, what is the middle conclusion that serves as the major premise of the second (exceptive) syllogism?
A) If God were not pre-eternal, He would be not pre-eternal.
B) If God were not pre-eternal, the world would not exist.
C) The world exists necessarily.
D) God is pre-eternal.

Key Takeaway
Qiyas al-khalf is a powerful compound syllogism that proves a desired conclusion by combining a categorical syllogism (producing a conditional) with an exceptive syllogism, widely used in kalām for establishing divine attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a qiyas al-khalf to prove that the Qur'an is uncreated (ghayr makhluq). Assume its opposite (that it is created) and show it leads to absurdity (e.g., contradiction with divine speech). Write the two-syllogism structure.
?
Read the following argument from Fakhr al-Din al-Razi: 'If the world were eternal, it would have an infinite number of past events; if it had an infinite number, it could not be traversed; but the world is actual; therefore it is not eternal.' Identify the categorical and exceptive components.

Poetic Syllogism: Purpose and Role of Imagination and Meter
Defines poetic syllogism and its purpose, with historical views on meter and imagination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the purpose of poetic syllogism as defined by al-Taftazani and distinguish the views of ancient and later scholars on the role of meter and imagination.

Core Idea
Poetic syllogism aims at emotional effect, not logical proof.

In the Islamic logical tradition, poetic syllogism (al-qiyas al-shi'ri) is one of the five arts. This passage from al-Bajuri's commentary explains its purpose: to move the soul emotionally, either toward contraction (grief, fear) or expansion (joy, hope). It also reveals a historical evolution in how scholars understood the means of achieving this effect, particularly the roles of imagination (takhayyul) and meter (wazn).

Guided Path
1
Identify the purpose of poetic syllogism
Find al-Sa'd's (al-Taftazani's) statement: 'al-gharaḍ min al-qiyās al-shiʿrī: infiʿāl al-nafs wa taʾthīruhā li-qabḍ aw basṭ'. What does this tell us about the goal? It is emotional change, not truth-seeking.
2
Recognize the role of imagination in the ancient view
The passage says: 'al-qudamā' kānū lā yaʿtabirūna fīhi al-wazna, wa yaqtaṣirūna ʿalā al-takhayyul'. The ancients relied solely on imaginative premises to affect the soul, regardless of meter.
3
Note the addition of meter by later scholars
Later scholars (al-muḥdithūn) added meter as a necessary component alongside imagination. The passage says: 'al-muḥdithūn iʿtabarū maʿahu al-wazna aydan'.
4
Understand the majority view
The majority (al-jumhūr) consider only meter essential, and this is the well-known contemporary position. The passage says: 'al-jumhūr lā yaʿtabirūna fīhi illā al-wazna, wa huwa al-mashhūru al-āna'.
5
Apply the distinction to a concrete example
The couplet about honey illustrates how identical content can produce opposite effects depending on wording. Analyze how imagination (the mental image of honey vs. vomit) creates contraction or expansion without changing the essence.
The example shows that poetic effect depends on expression, not logical truth.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَيَخْتَلِفُ التَّأْثِيرُ بِاخْتِلَافِ العِبَارَاتِ، وَأَنْوَاعِ الاِعْتِبَارَاتِ، وَبِحَسَبِ الأَوْزَانِ، وَإِنْشَادِهَا يضُرُوبِ الأَلْحَانِ، حَتَّى يَكُونَ الشَّيْءُ حَسَناً قَبِيحاً مِنْ جِهَتَيْنِ، وَمَحْمُوداً مَذْمُوماً بِاعْتِبَارَيْنِ ; كَمَا قِيلَ: تَقُولُ هَذَا لُعَابُ النَّحْلِ تَمْدَحْه وَإِنْ ذَمَمْتَ فَقُلْ قَيْءُ الزَّنَابِيرِ مَدْحُ وَذَمٌ وَذَاتُ الشَّيْءِ وَاحِدَة إِنَّ البَيَانَ يُرِي الظَّلْمَاءَ كَالنُّورِ قَالَ السَّعْدُ: وَالغَرَضُ مِنَ القِيَاسِ الشِّعْرِي : انْفِعَالُ النَّفْسِ وَتَأْثِيرُهَا لِقَبْضِ أَوْ بَسْط وَنَحْوِهِمَا , قَالَ : وَالقُدَمَاءُ كَانُوا لَا يَعْتَبِرُونَ فِيهِ الوَزْنَ، وَيَقْتَصِرُونَ عَلَى التَّخَيُّلِ، وَالمُحْدِثُونَ اعْتَبَرُوا مَعَهُ الوَزْنَ أَيْضاً , وَالجُمْهُورُ لَا يَعْتَبِرُونَ فِيهِ إِلَّا الوَزْنَ، وَهُوَ المَشْهُورُ الآنَ.
The effect varies with the difference of expressions and types of considerations, and according to meters and their recitation in various melodies, so that something becomes good and ugly from two aspects, and praiseworthy and blameworthy from two considerations; as it is said: 'You call this bee's spittle praising it, and if you blame, you say vomit of hornets. Praise and blame, and the essence of the thing is one; indeed, eloquence shows darkness like light.' Sa'd said: 'The purpose of poetic syllogism is the soul's emotion and its effect for contraction or expansion and the like.' He said: 'The ancients did not consider meter in it, and confined themselves to imagination; the later ones considered meter along with it; and the majority do not consider in it anything but meter, and that is the well-known view now.'
Source pages p. 719

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on the nature of poetic syllogism (qiyas shi'ri). It begins by noting that the effect of poetic speech varies with wording, perspective, meter, and delivery, so the same object can be described positively or negatively. The couplet illustrates this: honey can be praised as 'bee's spittle' or disparaged as 'hornet's vomit' — the essence is the same, but expression changes the effect. Then it quotes al-Sa'd (al-Taftazani) defining the purpose of poetic syllogism as affecting the soul emotionally (contraction or expansion). The passage then explains a historical disagreement: early poets considered only imaginative effect (takhayyul), later poets added meter, while most contemporary authorities consider only meter essential. This shows the evolving definition of poetic syllogism within the tradition.

القياس الشعري
التخيل

A) To demonstrate truth
B) To affect the soul with emotion like contraction or expansion
C) To deceive the opponent
D) To teach meter and rhyme

Key Takeaway
Poetic syllogism is defined by its emotional purpose, and its formal means (imagination vs. meter) have been debated historically.
