Defining Sophistry and Its Components
Defines sophistry and expands its classification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the defining components of sophistry (safsata), distinguish between wahmiyyat and mushabbahat premises, and categorize the types of fallacy based on user intention.

Core Idea
Sophistry is a poisonous syllogism that destroys religion; knowing it protects your faith.

In this passage, we learn the technical definition of sophistry (safsata) as a type of syllogism composed of false premises. The author explains two kinds of such premises – imaginative (wahmiyyat) and plausible (mushabbahat) – and gives a vivid example of how the imagination conflicts with reason. He then outlines the purpose of sophistry and its benefit, before expanding into a classification of fallacies according to the user's intention. This lesson will equip you to recognize sophistical arguments in theological debates and guard against them.

Guided Path
1
Extract the definition
Find the sentence that defines sophistry. Identify the genus (syllogism) and the differentia (composed of wahmiyyat or mushabbahat).
The definition is explicit in the opening line.
2
Analyze wahmiyyat premises
Read the description of wahmiyyat and the example of fearing a dead person. Notice how the wahm (imagination) makes a false judgment, and how reason exposes its falsity.
The test: wahm assists reason in premises leading to the opposite conclusion, then recoils.
3
Understand mushabbahat premises
Identify the definition of mushabbahat: false propositions that resemble mashhurat (commonly accepted truths). Consider why such premises are dangerous.
They deceive the listener by appearing acceptable.
4
Recognize the purpose and benefit
Find the sentence stating the purpose (tashlit al-khasm wa iskatihi) and the benefit (knowing it to avoid it). Note the comparison to poison.
The benefit is protective: knowing sophistry prevents its harm to religion.
5
Distinguish types based on user
Read the commentary's expansion. Identify the three types: sophist (knowing), self-fallacious (ignorant), and contentious (deceptive). Also note the extraneous type (insults, etc.).
Each type has a different ethical and logical status.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَأَمَّا السَّفْسَطَةُ فَهِيَ : قِيَاسٌ مُؤلَّفٌ مِنْ قَضَايَا وَهُمِيَّاتٍ أَوْ مُشْتَهَاتٍ : فَالوَهْمِيَّاتُ : قَضَايَا كَاذِبَةٌ يَحْكُمُ بِهَا الوَهْمُ فِي أُمُورٍ غَيْرِ مَحْسُوسَةٍ . وَمِمَّا يُعْرَفُ بِهِ كَذِبُ الوَهْم : أَنَّهُ يُسَاعِدُ العَقْلَ فِي المُقَدِّمَاتِ المُنْتِجَةِ لِنَقِيض مَا حُكِمَ كَمَا يَحْكُمُ الوَهْمُ بِالخَوْفِ مِنَ المَيِّتِ مَعَ أَنَّهُ يُوَافِقُ العَقْلَ فِي أَنَّ المَيِّتَ جَمَادٌ، وَالجَمَادُ لَا يُخَافُ مِنْهُ , المُنْتِجُ لِقَوْلِنَا : «المَيِّتُ لَا يُخَافُ مِنْهُ», فَإِذَا وَصَلَ العَقْلُ وَالوَهْمُ إِلَى النَّتيجَةِ نَكَصَ الوَهْمُ وَأَنْكَرَهَا . وَالمُرَادُ بِـ (المُشَبَّهَاتِ) : قَضَايَا كَاذِبَةٌ تُشْبِهُ المَشْهُورَاتِ . وَالغَرَضُ مِنَ السَّفْسَطَةِ : تَغْلِيطُ الخَصْم وَإِسْكَاتِهِ . وَأَقْوَى مَنَافِعِهَا : مَعْرِفَتِهَا ; لِيُتَحَرَّزَ عَنْهَا , وَلِذَا شَبَّهُوهَا بِالسُّمِّ يُعْلَمُ لِيَتَمَيَّزَ , فَيَسْلَمُ الإِنْسَانُ مِنْ شَرِّهِ، وَهُوَ تَشْبِيه حَسَنٌ ; إِذْ فِيهَا هَلَاكُ الدِّينِ، وَفِي السُّمِّ هَلَالُ البَدَنِ. قوله : (وَأَمَّا السَّفْسَطَةُ) المراد بها هنا : مطلق المغالطة; من باب استعمال المقيَّد في المطلق , فهي بحسب مستعملها ; أي: المستدل بها، وما استعملها فيه مِنَ الأحوال أنواع: فمَنِ استعملها عالماً بها يُوهِم النَّاس أنَّه حكيم مستنبط للبراهين فهي سفسطة وهو سوفسطائي. ومَن استعملها جاهلاً بها فهو مغالط لنفسه، ويسمّى القياس أيضاً : مغالطةٌ». - ومن استعملها مخادعاً لأهل الحقِّ ومشوّشاً لهم ; بأن نصب نفسه للجدال وقابل بها الجدليَّ، وقصد الغلبة فهي: «مشاغبة»، وهو مشاغب وممار. - واسم المغالطة يشمل النوعين، وقد تكون خارجة عنه، وتسمى : «خارجية» مثل تخجيل وترذيل قوله : والاستهزاء به وإظهار عيب يعرفه فيه، وقطع كلامه والإغراب عليه في اللغة، واستعمال ما لا مدخل له في المطلوب وشبه ذلك، وهو مع أنّه أقبح أنواع المغالطة يقصد فاعله إيذاء خصمه وإيهام العوام أنه قهره وأسكته أكثر استعمالاً في زماننا لمحبتهم الغلبة وعدم اعترافهم بالحقِّ وجهلهم بالقوانين .
As for sophistry, it is a syllogism composed of imaginary or plausible premises. The imaginary are false propositions that the imagination (wahm) judges in matters not perceived by the senses. Among the ways to know the falsehood of the imagination is that it assists reason in the premises that produce the contrary of what it judged, like when the imagination judges fear from a dead person, even though it agrees with reason that the dead person is an inert body, and an inert body is not to be feared, producing our statement: 'The dead person is not to be feared.' So when reason and imagination reach the conclusion, the imagination recoils and denies it. By 'plausible premises' is meant false propositions that resemble commonly accepted ones. The purpose of sophistry is to lead the opponent into error and silence him. The strongest benefit of it is knowing it, so that one may guard against it; therefore they likened it to poison: it is known so that it may be distinguished, and then a person is safe from its harm. This is a good simile, for in it is the destruction of religion, and in poison is the destruction of the body. His statement: 'As for sophistry' – what is meant here is absolute fallacy, by way of using the restricted in the unrestricted. It varies according to its user, i.e., the one who uses it as evidence, and the conditions in which it is used are types: whoever uses it knowingly, making people imagine that he is a wise discoverer of proofs, it is sophistry and he is a sophist. Whoever uses it ignorantly is a self-fallacious person, and the syllogism is also called 'fallacy'. Whoever uses it to deceive the people of truth and confuse them, by setting himself up for debate and confronting the dialectician with it, intending to overcome, it is 'contentious argument', and he is a contentious person. The name 'fallacy' includes both types, and there may be something outside them, called 'extraneous', such as insulting and debasing, mocking, showing a known fault in the opponent, interrupting his speech, using obscure language, using what has no bearing on the desired conclusion, and the like. This, although it is the ugliest type of fallacy, its doer intends to harm his opponent and make the common people think he has overpowered and silenced him; it is most commonly used in our time due to their love of victory, their lack of acknowledgment of truth, and their ignorance of the laws.
Source pages p. 720

Commentary
This passage defines sophistry (safsata) and its components. It begins by stating that sophistry is a syllogism composed of imaginary (wahmiyyat) or plausible (mushabbahat) premises. Wahmiyyaat are false propositions that the estimative faculty (wahm) judges in non-sensible matters. The passage gives a test to recognize the falsity of wahm: wahm assists reason in premises leading to a conclusion contrary to its own judgment; e.g., wahm causes fear of a dead body, yet agrees with reason that a dead body is inert and not to be feared. When reason and wahm reach the conclusion (the dead is not feared), wahm recoils and denies it. Mushabbahat are false propositions that resemble commonly accepted ones (mashhurat). The purpose of sophistry is to mislead and silence the opponent. Its greatest benefit is knowing it to avoid it; it is likened to poison – knowing poison protects one from its harm, just as knowing sophistry protects one's religion (as it can destroy religion). The commentary then expands: 'safsata' here means absolute fallacy (mujarrad al-mughalata). Depending on the user: if used knowingly to claim wisdom, it is sophistry (safsata) and the user is a sophist (sufista'i); if used ignorantly, it is self-deception and the syllogism is called 'fallacy' (mughalata); if used deceptively against people of truth to confuse and overcome in debate, it is 'contentious argument' (mushaghaba) and the user is contentious (mushaghib). The term 'fallacy' covers both, and there is also an 'extraneous' type (kharijiyya) that includes insults, mockery, interruption, obscure language, irrelevant points. This is the ugliest type, common in the author's time due to love of victory, not acknowledging truth, and ignorance of logical rules.

السفسطة
الوهميات
المشبهات
المغالطة
المشاغبة

Key Takeaway
Sophistry is a syllogism with false premises that must be known and avoided to protect one's faith, and it manifests in different forms depending on the user's intention and knowledge.

Rational vs Habitual Implication: The Ash'ari Dispute
Author presents four views on how premises lead to conclusion: rational, habitual, generation, obligatory, and details the first two.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the rational ('aqli) and habitual ('adi) views of implication in Ash'ari logic and explain their theological implications regarding divine power and human knowledge.

Core Idea
Rational necessity between premises and conclusion does not negate God's free choice; habitual custom does not impose necessity.

In this passage, the commentator unpacks a crucial debate within the Ash'ari school: Is the relationship between premises and conclusion one of rational necessity, like an accident necessarily inhering in a substance, or merely a habitual custom, like fire burning? The answer has profound implications for how we understand the certainty of syllogistic proofs and the scope of divine power. The passage presents two main positions within the Ahl al-Haqq school, attributed to Imam al-Haramayn/al-Ghazali and to al-Ash'ari himself. Understanding this dispute helps you appreciate the epistemological foundations of Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main views
Locate in the passage the two sub-views within the Ahl al-Haqq school: the view that implication is rational ('aqli) and the view that it is habitual ('adi). Note the metaphors used: accident in substance for rational, fire burning for habitual.
2
Analyze the rational view's argument
The passage cites al-Razi's proof: if you know that the world is changing and that every changing thing is originated, you cannot be ignorant that the world is originated. This shows the necessity of the conclusion given the premises. However, the commentator adds a condition: this holds only if the person lives long enough to formulate the conclusion. Consider why this condition is added.
3
Compare the theological implications
Both views affirm that Allah creates the knowledge of the conclusion. The rational view says that Allah creates both premises and conclusion together inseparably, while the habitual view says He can create premises without conclusion (breaking the habit). This raises a question: does the rational view limit God's power? The commentator argues it does not because separation is impossible (impossible things are not objects of power). So both views preserve divine omnipotence, but they differ on the nature of logical entailment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثُمَّ قَالَ: (۱۲۰) وَفِي دَلَالَةِ المُقَدِّمَاتِ عَلَى النَّنِيجَةِ خِلَافٌ آتِ (۱۲۱) عَقْلِيٌّ، أَوْ عَادِيٌّ، أَوْ تَوَلُّدُ، أَوْ وَاجِبٌ وَالأَوَّلُ المُؤَيَّدُ ; أَشَارَ بِهَذَا إِلَى الخِلَافِ فِي وَجْهِ إِفَادَةِ النَّظَرِ العِلْمَ بِالنَّتِيجَةِ، وَذَكَرَ فِي ذَلِكَ أَرْبَعَةَ أَقْوَالٍ، وَهِيَ فِي الحَقِيقَةِ ثَلَاثَةُ مَذَاهِبَ : (١) - أَحَدُهَا : مَذْهَبُ أَهْلِ الحَقِّ أَنَّ اللهَ تَعَالَى هُوَ الخَالِقُ لِلعِلْمِ بِالنَّتِيجَةِ عَقِبَ النَّظَرِ بِلَا وَاسِطَةٍ، وَلَا تَأثِيرِ لِغَيْرِهِ فِيهَا ، كَمَا لَا تَأثِيرَ لِغَيْرِهِ فِي شَيْءٍ مِنَ المُمْكِنَاتِ ; ثُمَّ اخْتَلَفُوا عَلَى قَوْلَيْنِ : أَحَدُهُمَا : قَوْلُ إِمَام الحَرَمَيْنِ ، وَقَالَ الغَزَالِيُّ : هُوَ قَوْلُ أَكْثَرِ أَصْحَابِنَا : إِنَّ اللُّزُومَ المَذْكُورَ عَقْلِيٌّ كَلُزُومِ العَرَضِ لِلجَوْهَرِ، فَلَا تَتَعَلَّقُ القُدْرَةُ بِخَلْقِ أَحَدِهِمَا دُونَ الْآخَرِ ; لِأَنَّ ذَلِكَ مُحَالٌ، بَلْ تَتَعَلَّقُ بِخَلْقِهِمَا مَعاً ، أَوْ بِتَرْكِهِمَا مَعاً كَسَائِرِ اللَّوَازِمِ الحَادِثَةِ مَعَ مَلْزُومَاتِهَا . وَكَوْنُ اللُّزُومِ بَيْنَ الحَادِثَيْنِ عَقْلِيًّا لَا يُنَافِي كَوْنَ كُلِّ مِنْهُمَا فِعْلُ القَادِرِ المُخْتَارِ ; لِأَنَّهُمَا يَصْدُقُ عَلَى كِلَيْهِمَا أَنَّهُ إِنْ شَاءَ فَعَلَهُ ، وَإِنْ شَاءَ تَرَكَهُ ، وَمَعْلُومٌ أَنَّهُ إِنَّمَا يَفْعَلُهُ عَلَى الوَجْهِ المُمْكِن لَا المُسْتَحِيل . القَوْلُ الثَّانِي : لِلشَّيْخِ أَبِي الحَسَنِ الأَشْعَرِيِّ : إِنَّ اللُّزُومَ المَذْكُورَ عَادِيٌّ كَلُزُومِ الإِحْرَاقِ لِمَس النَّارِ، فَيَجُوزُ أَنْ لَا يَخْلُقَهُ اللهُ تَعَالَى عَلَى طَرِيقِ خَرْقِ العَادَةِ . قَالَ بَعْضُهُمْ : وَهَذَا القَوْلُ هُوَ الظَّاهِرُ ; لأَنَّ العُلُومَ الحَادِثَةَ أَعْرَاضٌ يَعْقُبُ بَعْضُهَا بَعْضاً، فَأَيُّ مَانِعِ مِنْ أَنْ يَخْلُقَ اللهُ تَعَالَى لِلعَبْدِ العِلْمَ بِالمُقَدِّمَتَيْنِ المُتَرَنبَتَيْنِ، ثُمَّ لَا يَخْلُقَ لَهُ العِلْمَ بِالنَّتيجةِ بَعْدُ . قَالَ النَّاظِمُ : (وَفِي دَلَالَةِ المُقَدِّمَاتِ) : قوله : (إِنَّ اللُّزُومَ المَذْكُورَ عَقْلِيٌّ) أي: لا يمكن حصول العلم بمقدِّمتي القياس دون حصول العلم بالنتيجة، وبرهن عليه الرَّازِيُّ بأنَّ مَن علم أنَّ العالم متغيّر ، وكلُّ متغيّر حادثُ، يستحيل أن يجهل أنَّ العالم حادث. ثُمَّ إِنَّ هذا القائل باللُّزوم العقليّ إنَّما يقول به إنِ امتدَّ الأجل، بأن عاش الناظر عقب النَّظر قدر ما تخطر النتيجة بباله وأمَّا إن عاجلته المنية فيجوز حصول المقدمتين في ذهنه، ولا تحصل النتيجة، ومثل المنية الإغماء والجنون، ولم يذكر هذا القيد النَّاظم ولا الشارح، وقد أشار له شيخنا سيدي حمدون في «خريدته» بقوله : عَادَةً أَوْ عَقْلاً إِنِ امْتَدَّ الأَجَلْ . وفي تقديمه العادي على العقليّ؛ إشارة إلى ترجيحه قال شارحها : وهو الصحيح عند كثير من المحققين . قوله : ( عَلَى الوَجْهِ المُمْكِنِ لَا المُسْتَحِيلِ الوجه الممكن هو إن شاء فعل الملزوم واللازم معاً، وإن شاء تركهما معاً؛ إذ الانفكاك بينهما مستحيل لا تتعلق به القدرة فالملازمة العقلية بين مقدورين لا تنافي تعلّق الاختيار بهما ; بمعنى أنَّ الفاعل المختار إن شاء فعلهما، وإن شاء تركهما . قوله : (قَالَ بَعْضُهُمْ : وَهَذَا القَوْلُ هُوَ الظَّاهِرُ ممَّن رجّحه أبو العباس ابن زكري، لكن اختلف على هذا القول هل تتعلّق قدرة الناظر الحادثة بالنتيجة، فتكون كسبيَّة، أو هي بمحض القدرة الأزليَّة، ولا تعلُّق للقدرة الحادثة إلا بإحضار المقدمتين، فتكون غير كسبية.
Then he said: (120) And concerning the implication of premises upon the conclusion there is a dispute coming (121) rational, or habitual, or generation, or obligatory, and the first is supported. He pointed by this to the dispute regarding the manner of the consideration producing knowledge of the conclusion, and he mentioned in that four statements, but in reality they are three schools: (1) One of them: the school of the people of truth that Allah the Exalted is the creator of the knowledge of the conclusion after the consideration without any intermediary, and no effect of anything else in it, just as there is no effect of anything else in any of the possibles. Then they differed into two statements: One of them: the statement of Imam al-Haramayn, and al-Ghazali said: it is the statement of most of our companions: that the mentioned implication is rational like the inherence of an accident in a substance, so that power does not pertain to creating one of them without the other; because that is impossible, but rather it pertains to creating both together, or leaving both together like the rest of the accidental concomitants with their subjects. And that the implication between two originated things is rational does not contradict each of them being an act of the free agent; because both are described as 'if He willed He did it, and if He willed He left it', and it is known that He only does it in a possible manner, not an impossible one. The second statement: that of Sheikh Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari: that the mentioned implication is habitual like the implication of burning upon touching fire, so it is possible that Allah the Exalted does not create it by way of breaking the habit. Some of them said: And this statement is the apparent one; because originated knowledges are accidents that follow one another, so what prevents that Allah the Exalted creates for the servant the knowledge of the two premises that are conjoined, then He does not create for him the knowledge of the conclusion afterwards. The versifier said: (And concerning the implication of the premises): his saying: (the mentioned implication is rational) meaning: it is not possible to obtain knowledge of the two premises of the syllogism without obtaining knowledge of the conclusion, and al-Razi proved it by (saying) that one who knows that the world is changing, and every changing thing is originated, it is impossible that he is ignorant that the world is originated. Then indeed this proponent of rational implication only says so if the lifespan extends, such that the considerer lives after considering as much as the conclusion occurs to his mind. But if death hastens him, it is possible that the two premises occur in his mind and the conclusion does not occur. The same as death are fainting and insanity. And neither the versifier nor the commentator mentioned this condition, but our master Sayyid Hamdun indicated it in his 'Khulasa' by his saying: 'habitually or rationally if the lifespan extends'. And in his giving precedence to the habitual over the rational, there is an indication of its preference (the habitual). Its commentator said: and it is the correct view according to many of the verifiers. His saying: (in a possible manner, not an impossible one) the possible manner is if He wills He does the subject and the concomitant together, and if He wills He leaves both together; since separation between them is impossible and power does not pertain to it. So rational entailment between two things within power does not contradict the attachment of choice to them; meaning that the free agent if He wills does them, if He wills leaves them. His saying: (Some of them said: and this statement is the apparent one) from among those who preferred it is Abu al-Abbas Ibn Zikri, but there is disagreement on this statement: does the created power of the considerer relate to the conclusion, so that it is acquired, or is it by pure eternal power, and the created power only relates to bringing the two premises, so it is not acquired.
Source pages p. 726

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on a line from the matn that states there is a dispute about the implication of premises upon the conclusion, with four views: rational, habitual, generation, and obligatory, the first being supported. The author explains that these four reduce to three schools. The first school is that of the Ahl al-Haqq (Ash'aris) who affirm that Allah creates the knowledge of the conclusion directly after consideration, with no effect from anything else. Within this school, there are two sub-views. The first sub-view, attributed to Imam al-Haramayn and al-Ghazali, holds that the implication is rational ('aqli), like the necessary inherence of an accident in a substance. That is, it is impossible for the two premises to exist in the mind without the conclusion necessarily following. They argue that the power of Allah cannot create one without the other; it must create both or leave both, just as with any accident and its subject. However, this rational necessity does not negate free will, because both the premises and the conclusion are under divine power; if Allah wills, He creates both, if He wills, He leaves both, but separation is impossible because it would be a contradiction. The second sub-view, from Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari himself, holds that the implication is habitual ('adi), like the burning effect of fire. It is not rationally necessary but is a custom that Allah usually follows; He may break the custom and not create the conclusion even after the premises. This view is considered apparent by some because originated knowledges are accidents that can be separated. The commentator then references an argument by al-Razi: one who knows that the world is changing and that every changing thing is originated cannot be ignorant that the world is originated. This supports the rational necessity view. However, the rationalist only holds this view under the condition that the considerer lives long enough for the conclusion to come to mind; if death occurs before, the premises could be known without the conclusion. This condition is mentioned by Shaykh Hamdun in his Khulasa, and he further suggests preference for the habitual view by giving it precedence. The passage then discusses a related issue: if the habitual view is adopted, there is a question of whether the human power (kasb) pertains to the conclusion or not. This passage thus presents a sophisticated theological debate about the nature of logical implication and divine action, showing that the Ash'ari school distinguishes between rational necessity and habitual custom, with implications for human agency and knowledge.

اللزوم العقلي

Checkpoint
What is the main difference between the rational ('aqli) and habitual ('adi) views of implication according to the passage?
A) The rational view says the conclusion is created by human effort, while the habitual says it is from God
B) The rational view says the conclusion necessarily follows from premises by necessity, while the habitual says it follows by custom and God can break it
C) The rational view is held by al-Ash'ari, the habitual by al-Ghazali
D) The rational view denies free will, while the habitual affirms it

Key Takeaway
The dispute over whether implication is rational or habitual reveals the Ash'ari effort to safeguard both logical certainty and divine omnipotence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify which view (rational or habitual) is presupposed by the following statement: 'If you know that every intoxicant is forbidden and that nabidh is an intoxicant, then you must know that nabidh is forbidden.' Explain why the proponent of the habitual view would reject the 'must'.
?
Analyze the following argument from a theological debate: 'Either the rational implication limits God's power or the habitual view makes knowledge uncertain.' Use the passage to explain how the proponents of each side would respond.

The Syllogism
intermediate
8 min
Rational vs. Customary Necessity in Syllogistic Inference
Author explains and critiques the Ash'ari view of rational necessity in syllogistic inference.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the rational and customary views of the connection between premises and conclusion in a syllogism, and identify the condition that restricts rational necessity.

Core Idea
Rational necessity is conditional on survival; otherwise the connection is merely customary.

This passage from Bajuri's commentary presents two major views on how premises lead to the conclusion in a syllogism. The first view (Ash'ari) holds a rational necessary connection, but with a crucial condition often overlooked: the observer must live long enough to infer. The second view (al-Ash'ari) considers the connection customary, like fire burning, allowing divine interruption. Understanding these views and their conditions is essential for evaluating the epistemological certainty of syllogistic arguments in the Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two views presented
Locate the two statements: the first is the view of rational necessity (al-luzum al-'aqli) and the second is al-Ash'ari's view of customary necessity (al-'adi). Notice how each describes the relationship between premises and conclusion.
2
Extract key terms and their implications
Focus on 'luzum 'aqli' (rational concomitance) and 'qadir mukhtar' (free agent). Understand that rational necessity does not negate free will because the agent can will both premises and conclusion together or leave both, but cannot separate them.
The text explicitly states: 'If He wills He does both, and if He wills He leaves both.'
3
Analyze al-Razi's proof
Al-Razi's proof is given: 'Whoever knows that the world is changing and that everything changing is originated cannot be ignorant that the world is originated.' Formalize this as a categorical syllogism and note its validity.
This proof refutes the possibility of having premises without conclusion when both are known rationally.
4
Identify the condition for rational necessity
Find the condition that the proponent of rational necessity requires: the observer must live long enough after the premises to infer the conclusion. Death, fainting, or insanity prevent the conclusion from obtaining.
Shaykh Hamdun added this condition in his 'Kharida'.
5
Evaluate which view is preferred
Note that the text presents 'some said' that the customary view is the apparent one, and that presenting customary before rational indicates its preponderance. Many verifiers favor the customary view.
The discussion also touches on whether the conclusion is acquired (kasbi) or not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقسام الحجة
لأَنَّ ذَلِكَ مُحَالٌ، بَلْ تَتَعَلَّقُ بِخَلْقِهِمَا مَعاً ، أَوْ بِتَرْكِهِمَا مَعاً كَسَائِرِ اللَّوَازِمِ الحَادِثَةِ مَعَ مَلْزُومَاتِهَا . وَكَوْنُ اللُّزُومِ بَيْنَ الحَادِثَيْنِ عَقْلِيًّا لَا يُنَافِي كَوْنَ كُلِّ مِنْهُمَا فِعْلُ القَادِرِ المُخْتَارِ ؛ لِأَنَّهُمَا يَصْدُقُ عَلَى كِلَيْهِمَا أَنَّهُ إِنْ شَاءَ فَعَلَهُ ، وَإِنْ شَاءَ تَرَكَهُ ، وَمَعْلُومٌ أَنَّهُ إِنَّمَا يَفْعَلُهُ عَلَى الوَجْهِ المُمْكِن لَا المُسْتَحِيل . القَوْلُ الثَّانِي : لِلشَّيْخِ أَبِي الحَسَنِ الأَشْعَرِيِّ : إِنَّ اللُّزُومَ المَذْكُورَ عَادِيٌّ كَلُزُومِ الإِحْرَاقِ لِمَس النَّارِ، فَيَجُوزُ أَنْ لَا يَخْلُقَهُ اللهُ تَعَالَى عَلَى طَرِيقِ خَرْقِ العَادَةِ . قَالَ بَعْضُهُمْ : وَهَذَا القَوْلُ هُوَ الظَّاهِرُ ؛ لأَنَّ العُلُومَ الحَادِثَةَ أَعْرَاضٌ يَعْقُبُ بَعْضُهَا بَعْضاً، فَأَيُّ مَانِعِ مِنْ أَنْ يَخْلُقَ اللهُ تَعَالَى لِلعَبْدِ العِلْمَ بِالمُقَدِّمَتَيْنِ المُتَرَقِّبَتَيْنِ، ثُمَّ لَا يَخْلُقَ لَهُ العِلْمَ بِالنَّتِيجَةِ بَعْدُ.
قصارة.
العِلم بمقدِّمتي القياس دون حصول العِلم بالنتيجة، وبرهن عليه الرَّازِيُّ بأنَّ مَن علم أنَّ العالم متغيّر ، وكلُّ متغيّر حادثُ، يستحيل أن يجهل أنَّ العالم حادث.
ثمَّ إِنَّ هذا القائل باللُّزوم العقليّ إنَّما يقول به إنِ امتدَّ الأجل، بأن عاش الناظر عقب النَّظر قدر ما تخطر النتيجة بباله وأمَّا إن عاجلته المنية فيجوز حصول المقدمتين في ذهنه، ولا تحصل النتيجة، ومثل المنية الإغماء والجنون، ولم يذكر هذا القيد النَّاظم ولا الشارح، وقد أشار له شيخنا سيدي حمدون في «خريدته» بقوله :
عَادَةً أَوْ عَقْلاً إِنِ امْتَدَّ الأَجَلْ
وفي تقديمه العادي على العقليّ؛ إشارة إلى ترجيحه قال شارحها : وهو الصحيح عند كثير من المحققين .
:
قوله : ( عَلَى الوَجْهِ المُمْكِنِ لَا المُسْتَحِيلِ الوجه الممكن هو إن شاء فعل الملزوم واللازم معاً، وإن شاء تركهما معاً؛ إذ الانفكاك بينهما مستحيل لا تتعلق به القدرة فالملازمة العقلية بين مقدورين لا تنافي تعلّق الاختيار بهما ؛ بمعنى أنَّ الفاعل المختار إن شاء فعلهما، وإن شاء تركهما . قوله : (قَالَ بَعْضُهُمْ : وَهَذَا القَوْلُ هُوَ الظَّاهِرُ ممَّن رجّحه أبو العباس ابن زكري، لكن اختلف على هذا القول هل تتعلّق قدرة الناظر الحادثة بالنتيجة، فتكون كسبيَّة، أو هي بمحض القدرة الأزليَّة، ولا تعلُّق للقدرة الحادثة إلا بإحضار المقدمتين، فتكون غير كسبية.
Divisions of the proof.
Because that is impossible, rather they (the two) are attached to creating both together, or leaving both together like the rest of the accidental concomitants with their subjects. And that the concomitance between two originated things is rational does not negate that each of them is an act of the free agent; because both are described as: if He wills He does it, and if He wills He leaves it. And it is known that He only does it in a possible manner, not an impossible one. The second statement: of Shaykh Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari: that the mentioned concomitance is customary like the concomitance of burning with touching fire, so it is possible that Allah does not create it by way of breaking the custom. Some said: This statement is the apparent one; because the originated knowledges are accidents that follow one another, so what prevents Allah from creating for the servant the knowledge of the two premises under consideration, then not creating for him the knowledge of the conclusion afterwards?
Summary.
Knowledge of the two premises of the syllogism without obtaining knowledge of the conclusion. Al-Razi proved against him by saying: Whoever knows that the world is changing, and everything changing is originated, it is impossible that he does not know that the world is originated.
Then, this proponent of rational necessity only says it if the term is extended, i.e., the observer lives after the observation enough time for the conclusion to occur to his mind. But if death overtakes him, it is possible for the two premises to be in his mind without the conclusion obtaining. Similar to death is fainting and insanity. Neither the author nor the commentator mentioned this condition. Our teacher Sayyidi Hamdun indicated it in his 'Kharidatu' by saying:
By custom or by reason if the term extends.
And his presenting the customary before the rational indicates its preponderance. The commentator of that poem said: It is the correct view according to many verifiers.
:
His words: (In a possible manner, not an impossible one). The possible manner is that if He wills He does both the subject and the concomitant together, and if He wills He leaves both together; because separation between them is impossible and power does not relate to it. So rational concomitance between two objects of power does not negate the connection of choice to them; meaning that the free agent if He wills does both, and if He wills leaves both. His words: (Some said: This statement is the apparent one) from among those who preferred it is Abu al-Abbas Ibn Zakari. But there is disagreement on this statement: does the originated power of the observer relate to the conclusion, so that it is an acquisition (kasbi), or is it solely by eternal power, and the originated power only relates to bringing the two premises, making it non-acquired?
Source pages p. 727

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological discussion of the nature of the connection between premises and conclusion in a syllogism. It presents and critically examines the first of several madhabs concerning how premises yield knowledge of the conclusion. The author begins by stating the impossibility of the opposite—that the premises and conclusion are created separately—arguing that if they were created independently, the connection would be merely customary, not rational. Then the Ash'ari view of al-luzum al-'aqli (rational necessity) is explained: the conjunction between two originated things is rationally necessary, yet both are acts of the free agent. The key condition is that the agent may will them together or leave them together, but cannot separate them. A second statement is attributed to al-Ash'ari himself, that the connection is customary like fire burning—Allah can break the custom. Some scholars prefer this customary view because knowledge is accidental and sequentially created; Allah could create premises without conclusion. A summary section then refers to al-Razi's proof: if one knows both premises (e.g., the world is changing, everything changing is originated), it is impossible not to know the conclusion. This refutes the possibility of having premises without conclusion when both are known rationally. However, the author adds a condition: this rational necessity holds only if the observer lives long enough to infer; otherwise death or unconsciousness prevents the conclusion. This condition was omitted by the original versifier and commentator but noted by Shaykh Hamdun. The final paragraph explains the 'possible manner' and discusses whether the observer's power relates to the conclusion (making it acquired) or only to the premises. The passage thus refines the rational necessity view by exposing missing conditions and clarifying its scope.

Key Terms
اللزوم العقلي
القادر المختار
التوليد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what condition does the proponent of rational necessity require for the conclusion to necessarily follow from the premises?
A) The premises must be certain.
B) The observer must be a believer.
C) The observer must live long enough after the premises to infer the conclusion.
D) The conclusion must be memorized beforehand.

Key Takeaway
Rational necessity between premises and conclusion in a syllogism holds only if the observer lives long enough to infer; otherwise the connection is merely customary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the syllogism: 'Every intoxicant is unlawful; nabidh is an intoxicant; therefore nabidh is unlawful.' According to the rational necessity view, if someone knows the two premises and is alive and conscious, must they know the conclusion? Under what condition would that not hold?
?
Formalize al-Razi's proof as a categorical syllogism. Identify the major premise, minor premise, middle term, and figure. Then verify its validity.

The Syllogism
intermediate
8 min
The Mu'tazila View of Tawallud and Its Refutation
Author presents and refutes the Mu'tazila view of tawallud.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Mu'tazila theory of tawallud in syllogistic inference and identify the rational and scriptural arguments used to refute it.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila compare reasoning to a chain of generated acts, but Ash'ari orthodoxy denies that humans create any acts.

The Mu'tazila held that the conclusion of a syllogism is generated from the premises, similar to how hand movement generates key movement. This lesson examines their view and the Sunni counter-arguments from rational and scriptural proofs.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the analogy
Understand the Mu'tazila example: hand movement → key movement. In syllogism, nazar (reflection) directly created by the human generates knowledge of the conclusion. The human only directly creates the first act (nazar); the conclusion is generated, not directly created.
The text says: 'like the movement of the hand necessitating the movement of the key'.
2
Identify the scriptural refutation
The author cites Quranic verses (Fatir 3, As-Saffat 96, Az-Zumar 62) that assert Allah is the sole creator. These verses are used to deny that humans can create any act, whether directly or by generation.
The hashya elaborates that if the principle that the servant creates his acts is false, tawallud collapses.
3
Analyze the rational proof
The author claims rational proofs also show that only Allah creates beings. The implicit argument is that contingent acts require a creator, and only Allah can be the creator of all contingent acts. The Mu'tazila attributing creation to humans contradicts this.
Rational proof is described as 'clear' without elaboration, relying on intuition.
4
Understand the structure of the refutation
The refutation attacks the foundational premise of tawallud: that the servant creates his voluntary acts. Once this premise is falsified by rational and scriptural proofs, the subsidiary claim (that the conclusion is generated) is also falsified.
The hashya concludes: 'If the foundational principle is falsified, then the subsidiary is falsified.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المَذْهَبُ الثَّانِي: وَهُوَ القَوْلُ الثَّالِثُ فِي النَّظْمِ ، مَذْهَبُ المُعْتَزِلَةِ : إِنَّ الإِفَادَةَ المَذْكُورَةَ بِطَرِيقِ التَّوَلُدِ، وَمَعْنَاهُ عِنْدَهُمْ : أَنْ يُوجِبَ فِعْلُ لِفَاعِلِهِ فِعْلاً آخَرَ؛ كَحَرَكَةِ اليَدِ المُوجِبَةِ لِحَرَكَةِ المِفْتَاحِ الَّذِي فِي اليَدِ ، فَالنَّظَرُ عَلَى مَذْهَبِهِمُ البَاطِلِ مَخْلُوقٌ لِلعَبْدِ بِلَا وَاسِطَةٍ ؛ كَحَرَكَةِ يَدِهِ، وَالعِلْمُ بِالنَّتِيجَةِ مَخْلُوقٌ لَهُ بِوَاسِطَةِ فِعْلِهِ الأَوَّلِ الَّذِي هُوَ نَظَرُهُ كَحَرَكَةِ المِفْتَاح. وَكَفَى فِي بُطْلَانِ مَذْهَبِهِمْ تَضَافُرُ الأَدِلَّةِ القَطْعِيَّةِ عَقْلاً وَنَقْلاً عَلَى انْفِرَادِ اللَّهِ تَعَالَى بِإِيجَادِ الكَائِنَاتِ، لَا إِلَهَ إِلَّا هُوَ .
قصارة
قوله : (بِطَرِيقِ التَّوَلُدِ) إِنَّما قالوا : إنَّ النَّتيجة متولّدة جرياً منهم وبناء على مذهبهم الفاسد في كون العبد يخلق أفعاله الاختياريَّة، وأنَّها لا تخرج عن ذاته، وحكموا على ما خرج عنها مِنَ الأفعال الاختياريَّة بالتَّولَّد كالقتل مثلاً ، فإنَّه عندهم تولد عن القطع، والقطع عن حركة السيف، وحركة السَّيف عن حركة اليد وحركة اليد اخترعها الإنسان؛ فغاية ما خلق الله له القدرة على حركة اليد، وحركة السّيف وما بعدها متحرّكة عنها، كذلك غاية ما خلق الله له الإقدار على تركيب القياس، وأما نفس القياس والنتيجة فمتولدان عنه، فالثَّاني بواسطة الأوّل.
قوله : (عَقْلاً وَنَقْلاً) أمَّا العقليّة فواضحةٌ .
وأمَّا النَّقليَّة ، فقال تعالى : ﴿ هَلْ مِن خَالِقٍ غَيْرُ اللهِ ﴾ [فاطر: 3]، ﴿ وَاللهُ خَلَقَكُمْ وَمَا تَعْمَلُونَ ﴾ [الصافات : ٩٦]، ﴿ اللهُ خَالِقُ كُلِّ شَيْءٍ ﴾ [الزمر : ٦٢] ، وإذا بطل الأصل المبني عليه وهو كون العبد يخلق أفعاله، بطل الفرع المبني.
The second madhab, and it is the third statement in the versification, the madhab of the Mu'tazila: that the mentioned benefit (i.e., knowledge of the conclusion) is by way of generation (tawallud). Its meaning according to them is that the act of the agent necessitates another act, like the movement of the hand necessitating the movement of the key that is in the hand. So, on their false madhab, the reflection (nazar) is created by the servant without intermediary, like the movement of his hand; and the knowledge of the conclusion is created by him through the intermediary of his first act which is his reflection, like the movement of the key. And sufficient for the falsehood of their madhab is the confluence of definite proofs, rationally and transmittedly, that Allah alone is the creator of beings—there is no god but Him.
Summary:
His words: (by way of generation) They only said that the conclusion is generated, proceeding from and built upon their corrupt madhab that the servant creates his voluntary acts, and that they do not exit his essence. And they judged whatever voluntary acts exit from it as generated, like killing for example: for according to them it is generated from cutting, cutting from movement of sword, movement of sword from movement of hand, and movement of hand is invented by the human. So the utmost that Allah has created for him is the power over the movement of the hand; the movement of the sword and what follows are moved by it. Likewise, the utmost that Allah has created for him is empowerment over composing the syllogism; as for the syllogism itself and the result, they are generated from him, so the second through the intermediary of the first.
His words: (rationally and transmittedly) As for the rational proofs, they are clear. As for the transmitted proofs, He says: 'Is there any creator other than Allah?' [Fatir 3]; 'And Allah created you and what you do' [As-Saffat 96]; 'Allah is the Creator of everything' [Az-Zumar 62]. And if the foundational principle—that the servant creates his acts—is falsified, then the subsidiary (i.e., generation) is falsified.
Source pages p. 728

Commentary
This passage presents and vigorously refutes the Mu'tazila theory of tawallud (generation) as applied to syllogistic inference. The author first defines the madhab: the knowledge of the conclusion is 'generated' from the premises, analogous to hand movement generating key movement. On this view, the reflective act (nazar) is directly created by the human agent, and the conclusion-knowledge is indirectly created via that act. The refutation uses both rational and scriptural arguments. Rational: it is self-evident that only Allah creates all beings. Scriptural: Quranic verses asserting Allah's sole creativity. The hashya (summary) elaborates: the Mu'tazila analogize killing: the agent directly creates hand motion, which generates sword motion, cutting, and death. Similarly, they claim the agent directly composes the syllogism, and the syllogism generates the conclusion. The author points out that this collapses if the premise (servant creates) is false—as affirmed by Sunni doctrine.

Key Terms
التوليد
النظر
النتيجة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What analogy does the Mu'tazila use to explain tawallud in the context of syllogism?
A) A fire burning wood.
B) Hand movement causing key movement.
C) A builder constructing a house.
D) A teacher teaching a student.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila view of tawallud in syllogism is refuted by the Ash'ari with rational and scriptural proofs that only Allah is the creator; if the premise that humans create any acts is false, then tawallud collapses.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Task 1: Using the analogy of hand and key from the passage, explain in your own words how the Mu'tazila extend tawallud to syllogistic reasoning. Then state the Ash'ari objection in one sentence.
?
Task 2: Find another Quranic verse (other than the three cited) that supports the Ash'ari claim that Allah is the sole creator. Explain how it would be used to refute tawallud.
